\hypertarget{intro}{%
\chapter{Introduction}\label{intro}}

Resources

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \href{http://alex.smola.org/drafts/thebook.pdf}{INTRODUCTION TO MACHINE LEARNING}
\item
  \href{https://web.stanford.edu/~hastie/ISLRv2_website.pdf}{An introduction to Statistical Learning}
\item
  \href{https://web.stanford.edu/~hastie/ElemStatLearn/printings/ESLII_print12.pdf}{The Elements of Statistical Learning}
\end{itemize}

Machine learning is also known as statistical learning

\begin{itemize}
\item
  two types of learning:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    supervised learning: predicting or estimating output based on inputs
  \item
    unsupervised learning: understanding the relationships among inputs without output
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

3 types of data

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Structured Data (tables form)
\item
  Semi-structured Data (e.g., XML, JSON, HTML)
\item
  Unstructured Data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Texts
  \item
    Images: Videos, photos,
  \item
    Networks: Social Networks
  \end{itemize}
\item
  ML is concerned with the the development, the analysis, and the application of algorithm that allow computers to learn
\item
  Learning:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A computer learns if it improves its performance at some tasks with experience (i.e., data)
  \item
    Extracting a model of system from the sole observation (or the simulation)
  \item
    A model = some relationships between the variables used to describe the system.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Goals:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Prediction
  \item
    Inference
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Learning if useful when:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Human expertise does not exist (navigating on Mars)
  \item
    Humans are unable to explain their expertise (speech recognition)
  \item
    Solution changes in time (routing on a computer network)
  \item
    Solution needs to be adapted to particular cases (user biometrics)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Applications in different domains:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Retail: Market basket analysis, customer relationship management (CRM)
  \item
    Finance: Credit scoring, fraud detection.
  \item
    Manufacturing: Optimization, troubleshooting
  \item
    Medicine: medical diagnosis
  \item
    Telecommunications: Quality of service optimization, routing
  \item
    Bioinformatics: Motifs, alignment WEb mining, search engines.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Steps}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Data generation: data types, sample size
  \item
    Preprocessing: normalization, missing values, feature selection/extraction
  \item
    Machine Learning: Hypothesis, choice of learning paradigm/algorithm.
  \item
    Hypothesis validation: cross-validation, model deployment.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Types of machine learning:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Supervised: manually label variables
  \item
    Unsupervised:
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Training and testing

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Training is the process of making the machine learn
  \item
    No free lunch:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Training set and testing set come from the same distribution
    \item
      Need to make some assumptions.
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Factors affecting the performance:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Types of training provided
  \item
    The form and extend of prior knowledge
  \item
    Type of feedback provided
  \item
    The learning algorithms used
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Two factors:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Modeling
  \item
    Optimization
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Algorithms:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    ML depends on algorithms.
  \item
    The algorithms control the search to find and build the knowledge structures
  \item
    The learning algorithms should extract useful information from training examples.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Types of learning (algorithms):

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Supervised learning (\(\{ X_n \in R^d, y_n \in R\}^N_{n=1}\))

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Prediction
    \item
      Classification (discrete labels), regression (real values)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Unsupervised learning (\(\{ X_n \in R^d\}^N_{n=1}\))

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Clustering
    \item
      Probability distribution estimation
    \item
      Finding association (in features)
    \item
      Dimension reduction
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Semi-supervised learning
  \item
    Reinforcement learning

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Decision making (robot, chess machine)
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{general-framework}{%
\section{General Framework}\label{general-framework}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(Y\) = output
\item
  \(X = (X_1,...,X_p)\) = p inputs
\end{itemize}

Under supervised learning, additive error model:

\[
Y = f(x) + \epsilon
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(f(.)\) = unknown fixed function
\item
  \(\epsilon\) = random error (\(E(\epsilon) = 0; \epsilon \perp X\))
\end{itemize}

For prediction (blackbox)

\[
\hat{Y} = \hat{f}(X)
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Reducible Error: \(\hat{f}(.)\) can be improved (in principle)
\item
  Irreducible Error: \(\epsilon\) because it's not a function of the inputs, which might contain unknown variables or uncontrolled variation
\end{itemize}

\[
\begin{aligned}
E(Y - \hat{Y})^2 &= E[f(X) + \epsilon - \hat{f}(X)]^2 \\
&= [f(X) - \hat{f}(X)]^2 + var(\epsilon) \\
&= \text{reducible} + \text{irreducible}
\end{aligned}
\]

For inference, \(f(X) = E(Y|X)\), but the distribution of Y\textbar X is unknown; hence, must be estimated.

We try to find \(\hat{f}\) such that \(Y \approx \hat{f}(X) \forall (X,Y)\)

Ways to estimate \(f\):

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Parametric

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \item
    Make an assumption about the function form of \(f(X)\)
  \item
    Try to fit data to that specified form (i.e., estimate the parameters) using OLS in regression or MLE in general.
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Nonparametric
\end{itemize}

Tradeoff between prediction accuracy and model interpretability

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.29}}\raggedright
Restrictive Model\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.25}}\raggedright
Flexible Model\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.29}}\raggedright
More inference

More interpretable\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.25}}\raggedright
More prediction\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{assessing-model-accuracy---model-fit}{%
\section{Assessing Model Accuracy - Model fit}\label{assessing-model-accuracy---model-fit}}

\hypertarget{continuous-output-mean-squared-error-mse}{%
\subsection{Continuous output: Mean squared error (MSE)}\label{continuous-output-mean-squared-error-mse}}

\[
MSE = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \hat{f}(x_i))^2
\]

Generalization Error is a measure of how accurately an algorithm or model is able to predict outcome values for test set.

Tradeoff between flexibility of the method and training MSE , but there is an U-shaped relationship between flexibility and test MSE. This is a manifestation of bias-variance tradeoff

\[
E(MSE) = E(y_0 - \hat{f}(x_0))^2\\
= var(\hat{f}(x_0)) + [bias(\hat{f}(x_0))]^2 + var(\epsilon)
\]

where \(0\) subscript means training data set

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(E(MSE) \ge var(\epsilon)\) because \(\epsilon\) is irreducible model error and serves as the lower bound on the quality of potential model fit
\item
  More flexible means more variance, but less bias.
\item
  Less flexible (parametric) means less variance, but more bias.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{categorical-output-training-error-rate}{%
\subsection{Categorical output: training error rate}\label{categorical-output-training-error-rate}}

\textbf{Training error rate} is the proportion of mistakes in classification from applying \(\hat{f}\) to the training set:

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n I(y_i \neq \hat{y}_i)
\]

and \textbf{test error rate} is

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n I(y_0 \neq \hat{y}_0)
\]

\textbf{Bayes classifier} is a simple classifier where it assigns each observation to the most likely class, given its predictor values (i.e., conditional probability that \(Y = j | x_0\) )

In other words, assigns \(x_i\) to class j where \(P(Y=j | X=x_0)\) is largest

Bayes classifier should produce the lowest possible test error rate, known as Bayes error rate:

\[
1 - E_x(max_j [P(Y = j |X)])
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  where it is greater than 0 if the classes overlap
\item
  analogous to the irreducible error
\end{itemize}

In reality, we don't know \(P(Y|X)\), hence, we try to approximate this conditional probability.

For example, KNN

\[
P(Y =j |X = x_0 ) \approx \frac{1}{K}\sum_{i \in N_0} I(y_i = j)
\]

\hypertarget{curse-of-dimensionality}{%
\subsection{Curse of dimensionality}\label{curse-of-dimensionality}}

Example: with a p-dimensional hypercube of unit volume, to capture a fraction (r) of the observations in a subcude, the length of a side of the sub-cube averages to \(e_p(r) = r^{1/p}\)

\[
e_1(.01) = .01 ; e_1(.1) = .1\\
e_{10}(.01) = .63; e_{10} (.1) = .8
\]

To cover 1\% of the observation in 10 dimensional cub, we have to cover 60\% of the entire sample space.

Hence, there is no ``local'' neighborhood in high-dimensions.

\hypertarget{reminder}{%
\subsection{Reminder}\label{reminder}}

Linear Regression

\[
Y = f(X) + \epsilon
\]

where \(f(X) = \beta_0 + \sum_{j=1}^p X_j \beta_j\) and \(X_J\) can be:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  quantitative inputs
\item
  transformations of quantitative inputs
\item
  polynomial terms
\item
  interactions
\item
  Discretize (i.e., dummy) variable of quantitative inputs.
\end{itemize}

We assume additive independent errors.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
Relationship between n and p & Result\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\(n >>p\) & low bias, low predictive variance, fit well with test data\tabularnewline
\(n \approx p\) & substantial variability, overfit, fit poorly with test data\tabularnewline
\(n <p\) & no unique solution, variance is infinite\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

And we fit to (i.e., find \(\beta\)) to minimize the residual sum of squares (RSS):

\[
RSS(\beta) = \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i - f(x_i))^2 = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i -\beta_0 - \sum_{j=1}^p x_{ij} \beta_j)^2 \\
= (\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X\beta})^T (\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X\beta}) \equiv  ||\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X\beta}||^2
\]

which is the square of the \(L^2\) norm

differentiating with respect to \(\beta\)

\[
\mathbf{X}^T  (\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X\beta}) = 0 
\]

then

\[
\hat{\beta} = \mathbf{X}^T\mathbf{X}^{-1}\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{y}
\]

and

\[
var(\hat{\beta}) = (\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X})^{-1} \sigma^2 \\
 = (\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X})^{-1} var(\epsilon)
\]

\textbf{Prediction}

\[
\hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X} \hat{\beta} = \mathbf{X}(\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^T\mathbf{y}
\]

where

\[
\mathbf{H} \equiv \mathbf{X}(\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^T
\]

known as the hat matrix, which projects orthogonally the \(\mathbf{y}\) vector onto the subspace of \(\mathbf{X}\)

From the Gauss-Markov theorem, OLS estimates are unbiased and have the smallest variance among all linear unbiased estimates.

For an etimator \(\tilde{\beta}\)

\[
MSE(\tilde{\beta}) = E(\tilde{\beta} - \beta)^2 = var(\tilde{\beta} + [E(\tilde{\beta}) - \beta]^2 \\
 = variance + bias ^2 
\]

Since OLS is an unbiased estimator \(\tilde{\beta} = \hat{\beta}\)

We could get smaller MSE if we allow the estimator to be biased. Hence, OLS might not be the best variance-bias trade-off.

Inference

\[
\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n - p - 1} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2
\]

assuming that \(\epsilon \sim N(0, \sigma^2)\) then

\[
\hat{\beta} \sim N(\beta, (\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X})^{-1}\sigma^2)
\]

\[
(n-p-1) \hat{\sigma}^2 \sim \sigma^2 \chi^2_{n-p-1}
\]

If p is large (i.e., a lot of covariates), you will likely find at least one variable to be significant by chance, thus, you could find a significant partial t-statistics. The F-test does not suffer from this issue because it accounts for the number of predictors.

Issues with linear regression

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Nonlinearity of the response -predictor relationship
\item
  correlated errors
\item
  Non-constant error variance
\item
  high-leverage points
\item
  Collinearity
\end{itemize}

Rule of thumb:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  parametric methods will tend to outperform non-parametric methods when the number of observations per predictor is small.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{model-assessment}{%
\subsection{Model Assessment}\label{model-assessment}}

The ``generalization'' performance of a learning method is how well it predicts independent test data, which is an objective way to choose between models (in terms of \textbf{model selection} and \textbf{model assessment})

Suppose we have

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Y = output variable
\item
  X = vector of inputs
\item
  \(\hat{f}(X)\) = prediction model estimated form a training set \(T\)
\end{itemize}

Consider errors between Y and \(\hat{f}(X)\) through some loss function. For example, \textbf{squared-error loss}

\[
L(Y, \hat{f}(X)) = (Y- \hat{f}(X))^2
\]

or \textbf{absolute error loss}

\[
L(Y, \hat{f}(X)) = |Y- \hat{f}(X)|
\]

\hypertarget{types-of-error}{%
\subsubsection{Types of Error}\label{types-of-error}}

\textbf{Test error (or generalization error)} is the prediction error over an independent test sample, and is defined as

\[
Err_T = E_{X^\circ, Y^\circ}[L(Y^\circ, \hat{f}(X^\circ))|T]
\]

where X and Y are assumed to be drawn randomly from their joint population distribution and training set \(T\) is fixed

Thus, the test error refers to the error over an independent sample when \(f(.)\) is estimated with this specific training set

\textbf{Expected prediction error (expected test error)}

\[
Err = E_T E_{X^\circ, Y^\circ}[L(Y^\circ, \hat{f}(X^\circ))|T] = E_T [Err_T]
\]

which averages over possible training sets

\textbf{Training error} is the average loss over the specific training sample, given by

\[
\bar{err} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \hat{f}(x_i))
\]

Even though the interest is in the test error, \(Err_T\) , which is almost impossible without a separate test data set. because within a single data set, we attempt to approximate such a validation step by adjusting the training error (e.g., AIC model selection) or by efficient re-use (cross-validation/bootstrap) of the training sample, but then we can only get good estimates of \(Err\) (expected prediction error), not \(Err_T\)

The bias-variance tradeoff when considering loss function is related to the performance of the training error as a measure of test error

\hypertarget{model-error-model-selection}{%
\subsubsection{Model Error/ Model Selection}\label{model-error-model-selection}}

Training error is more optimistic (less than) the true test error

To read more on quantifying this optimism \citep{hastie2001a} Ch. 7.4 where

the amount that \(\bar{err}\) underestimates the true test error depends on how strongly \(y_i\) affects its own prediction.

In other words, the larger \(cov(\hat{y}_i, y_i)\), the more optimistic the training error is.

The greater the effective number of parameters (less degrees of freedom) (more flexible model), the larger this co variance

\citep{hastie2001a} Ch. 7.5 showed that most of model selection criterion (e.g., AIC, BIC \(C_p\)) try to estimate prediction error by estimating the optimism and adjusting the training error accordingly to obtain an unbiased estimate of prediction error.

Alternatively, cross-validation and bootstrap methods provide direct estimates of the expected prediction (test) error, \(Err\)

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A model with more effective number of parameters is more flexible
\end{itemize}

Let

\[
\mathbf{y} = (y_1, \dots, y_n)'
\]

then a linear fitting method can be written as \(\mathbf{\hat{y}} = \mathbf{Sy}\)

where \(\mathbf{S} = n \times n\) matrix depending on the input vectors \(x_i\), but not on \(y_i\)

For example, in linear regression \(\mathbf{S = X(X'X)^{-1}X'}\)

Then the effective number of parameters (df) is

\[
df(\hat{\mathbf{y}}) = df (\mathbf{S}) = trace(\mathbf{S})
\]

which is the sum of the diagonal elements fo \(\mathbf{S}\)

If \(\hat{\mathbf{y}}\) arises from an additive-error model, \(Y = f(X) + \epsilon\), with \(var(\epsilon) = \sigma^2_\epsilon\), then

\[
df(\hat{\mathbf{y}}) = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^ncov(\hat{y}_i, y_i)}{\sigma^2_\epsilon}
\]

\hypertarget{cross-validation}{%
\subsubsection{Cross-validation}\label{cross-validation}}

If you have a large data set, you could randomly divide it into a training set (to fit the model) and a validation (or holdout, or test) set (to evaluate the test error rate relative to your loss function of choice such as MSE). But it has 2 issues:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  It can be highly variable
\item
  With fewer observations to fit the model, the statistical models perform worse, and validation set error rate may overestimate the test error rate
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\hypertarget{leave-one-out-cv-loocv}{%
\paragraph{Leave-One-Out CV (LOOCV)}\label{leave-one-out-cv-loocv}}

A special case of K-fold CV when K = n

\[
CV_{(n)} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n MSE_i
\]

where \(MSE_i = (y_i - \hat{f}^{-i}(x_i))^2\) (alternative loss function could also be used)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  LOOCV has low bias as an estimate of the expected test error rate, but very high variance
\item
  Individual MSE's in LOOCV are highly correlated because they pretty much have the same data set expect one observation.
\item
  LOOCV is expensive to implement
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\hypertarget{k-fold-cross-validation}{%
\paragraph{K-Fold Cross-Validation}\label{k-fold-cross-validation}}

We randomly split the data set into K equal size parts (or folds). Each fold is held out and the model is trained on the remaining K -1 folds and then the loss function of choice is evaluated on the fold that was held out

Consider \(k = 1, \dots, K\) folds

let \(\hat{f}^{-k}(x)\) denote the fitted function computed with the k-th fold removed. We compute the loss based on the samples in the k-th fold.

For example, with squared-error loss, we compute \(MSE_k\) for the observations in the k-th fold, \(k = 1,\dots,K\). The K-fold cross-validation estimate is

\[
CV_{(K)} = \frac{1}{K} \sum_{k=1}^K MSE_k
\]

Empirically, good choices for K are 5 or 10.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cv.error}\FloatTok{.10} \OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ (i }\ControlFlowTok{in} \DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{) \{}
\NormalTok{    glm.fit }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(mpg }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \FunctionTok{poly}\NormalTok{(horsepower, i), }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ Auto)}
\NormalTok{    cv.error}\FloatTok{.10}\NormalTok{[i] }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{cv.glm}\NormalTok{(Auto, glm.fit, }\AttributeTok{K =} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{delta[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\CommentTok{\# K{-}fold cross validation}
\NormalTok{\}}
\NormalTok{cv.error}\FloatTok{.10}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##  [1] 24.21946 19.39499 19.34473 19.40776 19.00064 19.24864 18.63274 18.94513
##  [9] 19.10414 19.11054
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{generalized-cross-validation}{%
\paragraph{Generalized Cross-Validation}\label{generalized-cross-validation}}

Recall PRESS statistics

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n [y_i - \hat{f}^{-i}(x_i)]^2 = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n [\frac{y_i - \hat{f}(x_i)}{1- S_{ii}}]^2
\]

where \(S_{ii}\) is the i-th diagonal element of \(\mathbf{S}\) (or the i-th element of the ``hat matrix'' diagonal in linear regression). Hence, we only have to fit the model once.

Generalized cross-validation (GCV)is a similar approximation when the trace of \(\mathbf{S}\) can be computed more easily than the individual elements \(S_{ii}\)

\[
GCV(\hat{f})= \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n [\frac{y_i - \hat{f}(x_i)}{1- trace(\mathbf{S})/n}]^2
\]

\hypertarget{cv-and-classification}{%
\paragraph{CV and Classification}\label{cv-and-classification}}

Common loss function is 0-1 loss where the error rate is defined as

\[
L(G, \hat{G}(X)) = I(G \neq \hat{G}(X))
\]

where \(\hat{G}(X)\) is the highest estimated conditional probability given the inputs.

Alternatively, we could also use deviances that factor in the estimated conditional probability directly. If \(p_k(X) = P(G = k|X)\) and \(\hat{G}(X)\) = the k that gives the maximum \(\hat{p}_k(X)\)

\[
L(G, \hat{p}(X)) = -2 \sum_{k=1}^K I(G = k) \log \hat{p}_k (X) \equiv -2 \log \hat{p}_G (X)
\]

which is -2 times the log-likelihood (i.e., the deviance).

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  CV must be applied to the entire sequence of modeling steps (i.e., samples must be removed before any selection or filtering steps are applied so long as these steps involve the outputs

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    An exception: if there was an unsupervised step in the process associated with the inputs. Since it's unrelated to the outputs, we don't need to do CV on this component.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{bootstrapping}{%
\subsubsection{Bootstrapping}\label{bootstrapping}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  To quantify the uncertainty associated with a given estimator
\item
  Get new data sets by sampling from the observed data set many times with replacement
\item
  Sample \(B\) different sets and calculate the quantity of interest about which you want to do inference \(\hat{\alpha}^{*1},\dots, \hat{\alpha}^{*B}\)
\item
  Then, you look at the distribution of samples and apply Monte Carlo procedures to do inference
\item
  For example, you want to get the standard error
\end{itemize}

\[
SE_B (\hat{\alpha}) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{B-1} \sum_{r=1}^B (\hat{\alpha}^{*r} - \frac{1}{B} \sum_{r'=1}^B \hat{\alpha}^{*r'})^2}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  In principle, you could evaluate model error, but it often overfits because the training and test samples contain some of the same observations, which can be improved by ``leave-one-out'' bootstrap procedures, but there are still large training set bias problems with the bootstrap.
\item
  \citep{hastie2001a} give an estimator that helps reduce this bias.
\item
  In general, cross-validation is more effective for evaluating model performance, and bootstrapping is more effective when evaluating the properties of estimators (for inference).
\item
  Rule of thumb for ``big data'' prediction problem, we can combine cross-validation and hold-out validation:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Use K-fold CV on the training data to choose the parameters that control flexibility of the model
  \item
    Use the entire training data to fit the model selected by CV
  \item
    Use a large hold-out sample to evaluate the test error from this fitted model (compare to the CV error).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\CommentTok{\# )}

The difference in SE estimates from bootstrapping and from a linear model comes from:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  LM estimates \(\sigma^2\) suing the RSS, which relies heavily on the linear model being correct. In other words, for example, if we have non-linearity in our data, the residuals (\(e\)) will be inflated, and leads to \(\hat{\sigma}^2\) being inflated as well.
\item
  LM assumes \(x_i\) fixed and variability comes from \(var(\epsilon_i)\)
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{improving-prediction-accuracy-and-interpretability}{%
\section{Improving Prediction Accuracy and Interpretability}\label{improving-prediction-accuracy-and-interpretability}}

If we have

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  large number of predictors
\item
  highly dependent predictors
\item
  low interpretability
\end{enumerate}

then, we can either

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{subset-selection}{Subset Selection}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{shrinkage-methods}{Shrinkage Methods} (also known as ``\emph{regularization}''): force (shrink) p predictors' parameters towards zero to reduce variance. It can also be used for variable selection
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{dimension-reduction-1}{Dimension Reduction}: Project the p predictors in an M-dimensional subpsace where \(M <p\) (i.e., compute M different linear combination or projection of the p predictors and use them as new predictors). (e.g., principal component analysis)
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{subset-selection}{%
\subsection{Subset Selection}\label{subset-selection}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Also known as \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/ordinary-least-squares.html\#variable-selection}{Variable Selection} (for more detail click the hyperlink)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{best-subset-selection}{%
\subsubsection{Best Subset Selection}\label{best-subset-selection}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Fit all \(p\) one by one, then all \(p(p-1)/2\) two predicotrs models, etc. to have \(2^p\) possible models. Then you find the best one using

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \(C_p\)
  \item
    AIC
  \item
    BIC
  \item
    Adjusted \(R^2\)
  \item
    cross-validation error measure
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A possible algorithm is the leaps and bounds algorithm by \citet{furnival1974} (up to 40 p)
\item
  Usually, we do best-subset first then apply cross-validation
\end{itemize}

\begin{Highlighting}[]

\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ISLR2)}
\FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{(Hitters) }\CommentTok{\# check dataset }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}

\hypertarget{stepwise-selection}{%
\subsubsection{Stepwise Selection}\label{stepwise-selection}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  With very large numbers of p, stepwise selection can be less computational intensive
\item
  Forward stepwise selection

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    can be used when \(p >n\), but only models up to order \(n-1\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Backward stepwise selection

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    can't be applied when \(p>n\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Hybrid stepwise
\item
  When applying cross-validation, to account fro the variability, we apply the \textbf{one-standard-error rule}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Evaluates cross-validation criteria (MSE) for each model size
  \item
    Select the smallest model where the estimated test error is within one SE of the lowest point on the curve (i.e.more parsimonious model)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{shrinkage-methods}{%
\subsection{Shrinkage Methods}\label{shrinkage-methods}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Constrain the Least Squares solution in a way that regularizes (shrinks) the coefficients towards 0.
\item
  This leads to biased estimates, but reduce variance
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{ridge-regression}{%
\subsubsection{Ridge regression}\label{ridge-regression}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  can also deal with multicolinnearity
\item
  Adding a constant to the diagonal of the \(\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X}\) matrix to get well-behaved (low variance) regression estimates even when \(\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X}\) is nearly singular.
\end{itemize}

In LS regression, we minimize the residual sum of squares

\[
RSS = \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i - \beta_0 - \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij})^2
\]

whereas in ridge regression, we estimate \(\hat{\beta}^R\) that minimize

\[
\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i- \beta - \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij})^2 + \lambda \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j^2
\]

where \(\lambda\) = tuning parameter

\(\lambda \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j^2\) is an \(l_2\)-norm shrinkage penalty that is small if the \(\beta\) are near 0.

Minimizing this objective function subject to this constraint forces \(\hat{\beta}^R\) to be closer to 0 than the LS estimates.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  When \(\lambda = 0\), we get the LS estimates
\item
  When \(\lambda \to \infty\) , the coefficients go to 0.
\end{itemize}

Because in the \(l_2\)-norm does not contain the intercept (i.e, we don't shrink the intercept), we have to center all the covariates by removing the mean of each column before we do the regression (\(\hat{\beta}_0= \bar{y}\)).

\[
\hat{\beta}^R = (\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X} + \lambda \mathbf{I})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{y}
\]

To estimate \(\lambda\), we use cross-validation

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Ridge estimator is not scale-invariant, so coefficients change substantially depending on the scale of the covariates. Hence, before performing ridge regression, we have to scaled and centered covariates (i.e., after removing the mean, divided each column of \(\mathbf{X}\) by its standard deviation.
\item
  \(\lambda\) allows for a continuous range of bias-variance trade-offs.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    As \(\lambda\) increases, the variance decreases but the bias increases. Hence, it works well when LS estimates have high variance.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Possible even when \(p>n\)
\item
  Under Bayesian framework, if \(y_i \sim N(\beta_0 + x^T_i\beta, \sigma^2)\) is the likelihood and the parameters have the independent prior distributions \(\beta_j \sim N(0, \tau^2)\) with known \(\tau^2, \sigma^2\), then \(\lambda = \sigma^2 / \tau^2\) (the ratio of regression to prior variances), and the ridge estimate is the posterior mode (and mean, since the posterior is Gaussian)
\item
  The ridge estimates are related to principal component regression. When principal components associated with the predictors, ridge regression shrinks the low variance principal component the most.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{lasso-regression}{%
\subsubsection{Lasso regression}\label{lasso-regression}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Even though ridge regression shrinks the parameters to 0, unless \(\lambda= \infty\), it cant set any parameter exactly equal 0. Hence, \citet{tibshirani1996} proposed lasso to handle this problem
\end{itemize}

The lasso coefficients, \(\hat{\beta}^L\) are those that minimize the objective function

\[
\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \beta_0- \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij})^2 + \lambda\sum_{j=1}^p|\beta_j|
\]

The only difference between ridge and lasso is the penalty term:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Lasso considers \(l_1\) norm penalty (given by \(||\beta||_1 = \sum_j |\beta_j|\))
\item
  Ridge considers \(l_2\) norm penalty (given by \(||\beta||_2 = \sqrt{\sum_j \beta_j^2}\)) similar to Euclidean distance
\end{itemize}

When tuning parameter (\(\lambda\)) is large enough, some coefficients are 0 (are dropped from the model)

There is no closed form for the lasso estimates \(\hat{\beta}^L\) , the choice of \(\lambda\) can be derived from cross-validation

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Lasso outperforms ridge when there are redundant predictors, but ridge outperforms lasso when all included predictors are needed
\item
  Lasso shrinks each parameter by approximately the same amount, while ridge shrinks proportionally
\item
  Lasso equivalent Bayesian interpretation is that: if the prior distribution on the parameters \(\beta_j\) are independent double exponential (Laplace) distribution with mean 0 and scale parameter \(\lambda\), the posterior mode for \(\beta\) is the lasso solution (but no the posterior mean); the Laplace prior puts more ``mass'' at 0, giving more a priori belief that the coefficients are 0.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{comparison}{%
\subsubsection{Comparison}\label{comparison}}

\[
\displaystyle{\min_{\beta}} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \beta_0- \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij})^2 \text{ subject to }
\begin{cases}
\sum_{j=1}^p |\beta_j| \le s \text{ (lasso)} \\
\sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j^2 \le s \text{ (ridge)} \\
\sum_{j=1}^p I(\beta_j \neq 0) \le s \text{ (best subset)}
\end{cases}
\]

All seek to minimize the RSS subject to constraints on the parameters.

In the best subset method, no more than s coefficients can be nonzero, and can't be computed for large p

\hypertarget{cross-validation-fitting}{%
\subsubsection{Cross-validation Fitting}\label{cross-validation-fitting}}

Steps:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Choose a ``grid'' of \(\lambda\) values
\item
  Compute the cross-validation error for each vlaue of \(\lambda\)
\item
  Select the \(\lambda\) for which the cross-validation error is smallest (one-standard error rule)
\item
  Refit the model using all available observations for the selected tuning parameter.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{generalization}{%
\subsubsection{Generalization}\label{generalization}}

We could generalize the objective function to

\[
\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \beta_0- \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij})^2 + \lambda \sum_{j=1}^p |\beta_j|^q
\]

for \(q \ge 0\), and \(q =0,1,2\) correspond to subset selection lasso, and ridge respectively

We could estimate q from the data, but it isn't worth.

Elastic net penalty (= combination of ridge and lasso) will be an alternative

The penalty component in the objective function is the priori distribution on the \(\beta\) under Bayesian framework (e.g., a normal prior = ridge regression and a Laplace prior = lasso). Hence, we can use \textbf{stochastic search variable selection (SSVS)} to consider model selection \citet{george1993}

SSVS is a hierarchical mixture of normal distribution

\[
\beta_j |\gamma_j \sim \gamma_j N(0, c_j \tau^2_j) + (1- \gamma_j)N(0, \tau^2_j)
\]

\[
\gamma_j \sim^{idd}Bern(\pi_j)
\]

where \(\gamma_j=1\) means the j-th variable is in the model with probability \(\pi_j\)

We would want \(\tau_j\) to be small so that when \(\gamma_j =0\), it's reasonable to estimate \(\beta_j\) close to 0.

We would want \(c_j\) to be large so that we are more likely to get a non-zero \(\beta)j\) when \(\gamma_j = 1\) (which is analogous to ridge regression)

An alternative to SSVS produce is \textbf{spike and slab} which replaces the second mixture distribution with a delta function at 0 (analogous to lasso)

\hypertarget{dimension-reduction}{%
\subsection{Dimension Reduction}\label{dimension-reduction}}

Let \(Z_1, \dots, Z_M\) represent \(M<p\) linear combination of the original \(p\) predictors:

\[
Z_m = \sum_{j=1}^p \phi_{jm} X_j, m = 1,\dots, M
\]

for constants \(\phi_{1m}, \dots, \phi_{pm}\)

Consider the least squared linear regression model

\[
y_i = \theta_0 + \sum_{m=1}^M \theta_m z_{im} + \epsilon_i, i = 1, \dots,n
\]

Hence, we will try to find an optimal way to choose \(\phi_{jm}\) so that we will have better results than the least squared solution on the original inputs

Special note

\[
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{m=1}^M \theta_m z_{im} &= \sum_{m=1}^M \theta_m \sum_{j=1}^p \phi_{jm} x_{ij} \\
&= \sum_{j=1}^p  \sum_{m=1}^M \theta_m \phi_{jm} x_{ij} \\
&= \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j x_{ij} \\
\end{aligned}
\]

Hence,

\[
\beta_j = \sum_{m=1}^M \theta_m \phi_{jm}
\]

This means the reduced dimension regression is a special case of OLS except that the parameters \(\beta_j\) are constrained.

Hence, when \(M = p\) and \(Z_m\) are all linearly independent (no constraints), the reduced dimension regression is equivalent to the OLS . The only benefit is that \(Z_m\)'s are orthogonal to each other

Similarly to the \protect\hyperlink{shrinkage-methods}{Shrinkage Methods}, the constrained solution is biased, but typically leads to lower variance in the fitted coefficients - especially when \(M << p\)

\hypertarget{principal-component-regression}{%
\subsubsection{Principal Component Regression}\label{principal-component-regression}}

Steps:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Find the linear combination of the inputs that accounted for the largest fraction of variance of the inputs
\item
  Find another linear combination of the inputs that accounted for the next highest amount of variation (subject to being orthogonal to the first)
\item
  Continue the process
\end{enumerate}

Mathematically, principal components could also be obtained using \textbf{singular value decomposition} (SVD) fo the centered data matrix

Let \(\mathbf{X}\) (\(n \times p\)) be the centered data matrix, then

\[
\mathbf{X} = \mathbf{UDV}^T
\]

where \(\mathbf{U}\) and \(\mathbf{V}\) are \(n \times p\) and \(p \times p\) orthogonal matrices

\(\mathbf{D}\) (\textbf{singular values}) is \(p \times p\) diagonal matrix with diagonal entries \(d_1 \ge d_2 \ge \dots \ge d_p \ge 0\)

Then,

\[
\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X} = \mathbf{VD}^2 \mathbf{V}^T
\]

which is the eigen-decomposition of \(\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X}\) and of the sample covariance matrix \(\mathbf{S} = \frac{\mathbf{X}^T \mathbf{X}}{n}\) up to the factor n

And the columns of \(\mathbf{V}\) are \textbf{eigenvectors} and correspond to the \textbf{principal component weights}

Hence, the new variables are

\[
\mathbf{z}_1 = \mathbf{Xv}_1 , \dots, \mathbf{z}_M = \mathbf{Xv}_M
\]

where \(\mathbf{v}_m\) is the m-th column of \(\mathbf{V}\)

These \(\mathbf{z}\)'s are the principal components.

The elements of \(\mathbf{v}_m\) are \(\phi\)'s in the dimension reduction summary

\[
var(\mathbf{z}_m) = \frac{d^2_m}{n}
\]

Thus, large (small) singular values correspond to directions in the column space of \(\mathbf{X}\) that have large (small) variance

Then principle component regression basically construct the first \(M\) principal components (i.e., \(Z_1, \dots, Z_M\)) and uses these components as predictors int the linear regression model fit by least squares

Assumption:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Directions in which \(X_1, \dots , X_p\) have the most variation are the directions that are most associated with \(Y\) (not always the case, but typically effective). In other words, these X's are mostly associated with Y (best predict), but we cannot be certain that X's variation causes Y's variation.
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  While capturing most of the variance from inputs, since \(M <<p\) we don't run into the problem of overfitting
\item
  Another benefit is that the new predictors are orthogonal (\(\mathbf{z}^T_j \mathbf{z}_k =0, j \neq k\)), which in the LS estimation we typically run into the problem of collinearity
\item
  Even though PCR uses a low-dimensional set of predictors, no model selection was performed because we only translate all \(p\) variables into a linear combination that makes the new PC inputs. Hence, it's closely related to \protect\hyperlink{ridge-regression}{Ridge regression} than \protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso regression} or \protect\hyperlink{best-subset-selection}{Best Subset Selection}. Seeing Singular Value Decomposition

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \(\mathbf{X}\hat{\beta}^R = \sum_{j=1}^p \mathbf{u}_j \frac{d_j^2}{d_j^2+ \lambda}\mathbf{u}_j^T \mathbf{y}\)
  \item
    where \(\mathbf{u}_j\) corresponds to the j-th column of \(\mathbf{U}\) from the SVD
  \item
    Since LS estimate gives \(\mathbf{X}\hat{\beta} = \mathbf{UU}^T \mathbf{y}\), ridge regression goes to the LS solution when \(\lambda = 0\)
  \item
    The singular vectors that have smaller variation (i.e., smaller \(d_j^2\)) are shrunk more than those with larger variation
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Rule of thumb: standardize the predictors before performing the Singular Value Decomposition to generate the principle components.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If we don't standardize, the predictors with the largest scale typically account for the most variance, regardless of their importance relative to the output.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{partial-least-squares}{%
\subsubsection{Partial Least Squares}\label{partial-least-squares}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Find linear combinations of the data (or directions in the \(\mathbf{X}\) matrix) that maximize \textbf{variance} and are \textbf{orthogonal}
\item
  Find the weight just like {[}Principle Component Regression{]}, but PCR in a unsupervised way because the choice of these directions is not dependent on \(Y\). Hence, might not provide good predictions of \(Y\)
\item
  PLS is a supervised way to do dimension reduction that finds new features \(Z_1, \dots, Z_M\) that are linear combinations of the original inputs, but it selects these in a away to account for the covariability withe the response Y
\end{itemize}

Steps to calculate the PLS directions (\(Z_m = \sum_{j=1}^p\phi_{jm}X_j\))

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Standardize the \(p\) predictors
\item
  For \(Z_1\), set each \(\phi_{j1}\) = the coefficient from the simple linear regression of Y on \(X_j\) (this is proportional to the correlation between Y and \(X_j\))
\item
  Calculate \(Z_2\), by calculating the residuals of a regression of each variable (separately) on \(Z_1\) (call these \(e_j\)), which corresponds to the info remaining that has not been accounted for by \(Z_1\)
\item
  Set \(\phi_{j2}\) = the coefficient from the simple linear regression of \(Y\) on each \(e_j\) to get \(Z_2\)
\item
  Repeat \(M\) to get \(Z_1, \dots, Z_2\)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Comparison to PCR:} \citet{hastie2001a} (chapter 3.5)

The m-th PC direction coefficients \(\mathbf{v}_m\) are the \(\alpha\) that solve

\[
\max_\alpha[var(\mathbf{X\alpha})]
\]

subject to \(||\alpha||=1, \alpha^T \mathbf{Sv}_l = 0, l = 1,\dots, m-1\) (the last condition ensures that each \(\mathbf{z}_m = \mathbf{Xv}_m\) is uncorrelated with all of the previous \(\mathbf{z}_l\))

where \(\mathbf{S}\) is the sample covariance matrix associated with \(\mathbf{X}\)

The m-th PLS direction \(\hat{\phi}_m\) have the \(\alpha\) that solves:

\[
\max_\alpha  [corr^2(\mathbf{y}, \mathbf{X \alpha}) var(\mathbf{X \alpha})]
\]

subject to \(||\alpha|| =1, \alpha^T \mathbf{S}\hat{\phi}_l =0,l = 1, \dots, m-1\)

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The extra component in PLS helps make the weight more relatable to Y (but sometimes it's more efficient, but sometimes it isn't)
\item
  Both X and Y should be standardized before performing PLS
\item
  The choice of \(M\) is made based on \protect\hyperlink{cross-validation}{Cross-validation}
\item
  Although PLS tries to maximize both the correlation to the output and the variance in the inputs, the variance tends to dominate and PLS behaves very similarly to ridge regression and PCR
\item
  The use of the output to supervise the selection of the reduced dimension can reduce bias, but increase variance (bias-variance trade-off), and reduce the added value of the approach relative to PCR
\item
  PLS is closely related to canonical correlation analysis (CCA) in multivariate statistic, which seeks to find a reduced set of linear combinations of inputs and linear combination of outputs that maximize the correlation (e.lg., finding the coefficient \(\alpha, \beta\) s.t
\end{itemize}

\[
\max_{\alpha, \beta} [corr(\mathbf{Y \beta}, \mathbf{X \alpha})]
\]

where a set of \(M\) with orthogonality constraints

\begin{itemize}
\item
  In high-dimensional space ( \(p \approx n; p >n\) ), models are easily overfitted (too flexible). Hence, they might appear better than they really are. Hence, to deal with high dimensional data, we use less flexible models (i.e., those that are regularized or constrained), such as \protect\hyperlink{best-subset-selection}{Best Subset Selection}, \protect\hyperlink{ridge-regression}{Ridge regression}, \protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso regression}, \protect\hyperlink{principal-component-regression}{Principal Component Regression}, \protect\hyperlink{partial-least-squares}{Partial Least Squares}. But we still need to consider their issues

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Large \(p\) always has collinearity; \protect\hyperlink{best-subset-selection}{Best Subset Selection} or \protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso regression} can't guarantee the chosen model is a scientific one
  \item
    p-values are not considered under high-dimensional regressions
  \item
    Tuning parameter selection is utmost important for good predictive performance
  \item
    The test error tends to increase as the dimensionality of the problem increases (\protect\hyperlink{curse-of-dimensionality}{Curse of dimensionality})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{part-supervised-learning}{%
\part{SUPERVISED LEARNING}\label{part-supervised-learning}}

\hypertarget{part-1.-local-based}{%
\part{1. LOCAL BASED}\label{part-1.-local-based}}

\hypertarget{spline-regression}{%
\chapter{Spline Regression}\label{spline-regression}}

Beyond Linearity (including transformation of variables), we have arbitrary basis expansion (where the basis functions are dependent on X)

\hypertarget{basis-function-representations}{%
\section{Basis Function Representations}\label{basis-function-representations}}

Let the k-th basis function of X be denoted by \(b_k(X), k = 1,\dots, K\) which is function that maps from the p-dimensional real numbers to the real line.

Then, the linear functional representation of \(f(X)\) given by these basis functions is

\[
f(X) = \beta_0 + \sum_{k=1}^K \beta_k b_k(X)
\]

Equivalently

\[
\mathbf{Y} = \mathbf{B} \beta + \mathbf{\epsilon}
\]

where \(\mathbf{B}\) is the \(n \times (K+1)\) matrix columns given by a vector of 1 for the intercept and \(\mathbf{b}_k =(b_k(x_1), \dots, b_k (x_n))', k = 1, \dots, K\)

Then, we have

\[
\hat{\beta} = (\mathbf{B}^T \mathbf{B})^{-1} \mathbf{B}^T\mathbf{y}
\]

if the basis functions are \textbf{orthogonal}, then we have a special case \(\hat{\beta} = \mathbf{B}^T \mathbf{y}\)

Some common basis functions;

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Original linear model: \(b_k(X) = X_k, k = 1, \dots, p\)
\item
  Polynomials: \(b_k(X) = X^2_j; X^3_j; X_jX_m\)
\item
  Non-linear transformations: \(b_k(X)= \log(X_j); \sqrt{X_j}\)
\item
  Step-functions: \(b_k(X) = I(c_k \le X \le v_k)\), an indicator for a region of \(X\)

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \item
    Breaking the region up into non-overlapping regions and step-function response
  \item
    Example: for a single \(X\), \(b_0 (X) = I(X < c_1), \dots, b_{K-1}(X) = I(c_{K-1} \le X \le c_K)\) where \(c_1, \dots, c_k\) are cut points (i.e., making dummy variables and piece wise constant regression fits)
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Other: sines and cosines, wavelets, empirical basis functions
\end{itemize}

Another representation

More info can be found on \href{https://www.stat.cmu.edu/~ryantibs/advmethods/notes/smoothspline.pdf}{CMU stat}

Definition: a k-th order spline is a piecewise polynomial function of degree k, that is continuous and has continuous derivatives of orders 1,\ldots, k -1, at its knot points

Equivalently, a function f is a k-th order spline with knot points at \(t_1 < ...< t_m\) if

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  f is a polynomial of degree k on each of the intervals \((-\infty, t_1], [t_1,t_2],...,[t_m, \infty)\)\\
\item
  \(f^{(j)}\), the j-th derivative of f, is continuous at \(t_1,...,t_m\) for each j = 0,1,\ldots,k-1
\end{itemize}

A common case is when k = 3, called cubic splines. (piecewise cubic functions are continuous, and also continuous at its first and second derivatives)

To parameterize the set of splines, we could use \textbf{truncated power basis}, defined as

\[
g_1(x) = 1 \\
g_2(x) = x \\
... \\
g_{k+1}(x) = x^k \\
g_{k+1+j}(x) = (x-t_j)^k_+
\]

where j = 1,\ldots,m and \(x_+\) = max\{x,0\}

However, now software typically use B-spline basis.

\hypertarget{regression-splines}{%
\section{Regression Splines}\label{regression-splines}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Spline basis functions are built upon polynomial regression and piecewise constant regression

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    We fit piece wise polynomial regressions, but add some continuity and smoothness constraints.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Example:

\[
y_i =
\begin{cases}
\beta_{01} + \beta_{11}+ \beta_{21}x_i^2 + \beta_{31}x_i^3+ \epsilon & \text{if } x_i <c \\
\beta_{02} + \beta_{12}+ \beta_{22}x_i^2 + \beta_{32}x_i^3+ \epsilon & \text{if } x_i \ge c 
\end{cases}
\]

where \(c\) is a \textbf{knot} point

The more flexible we want our model to be, the more knots to be added.

If we have \(K\) knots through the range of \(X\), we fit \(K+1\) different (cubic or any or constants) polynomials.

To restrict piece coming together at the knot points, we add the constraint that the fitted curve must be \textbf{continuous}.

We constrain the first and second derivatives to be continuous for \textbf{smoothness}.

If the polynomials are cubic, then it's cubic spline.

Each constraint that we add reduces the number of parameters to be fit (frees up df), and less complex

A cubic spline with \(K\) knots has \(K +4\) df

We can rewrite the cubic spline into the general basis notation

\[
b_1(X) = X; b_2(X) = X^2; b_3(X) = X^2; b_4(X) = h(X,\xi_1);\\
b_5(X) = h(X, \xi_2) , \dots, b_{K+3} (X) = h(X, \xi_K)
\]

where

\[
h(x, \xi) = (x- \xi)^3_+ = 
\begin{cases}
(x- \xi)^3 & \text{if } x > \xi \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

and \(\xi\) is the knot. Fitting this model with an intercept requires estimating \(K+4\) regression coefficients (i.e., \(K +4\) df)

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Splines have high variance at the outer range of the predictors.

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \protect\hyperlink{natural-splines}{Natural splines} are regression splines with extra constrains on the boundary that mitigates this variance problem by requiring the function to be linear in the regions where \(X\) is smaller than the smallest knot, and larger than the largest knot.
  \item
    To decide number and place of knots (\(K\))

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      To choose number of \(K\), people usually do cross-validation
    \item
      Given \(K\) knots, we locate them uniformly in the quantile domain of \(X\)

      \begin{itemize}
      \item
        Ex: \(K = 3\), a knot would be placed at the 25th, 50th, and 75th percentiles of the variable X.
      \item
        Alternatively, we can place knots based on local data abundance.
      \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Another representation

To estimate the regression function \(r(X) = E(Y|X =x)\), we can fit a k-th order spline with \textbf{knots} at some prespecified locations \(t_1,...,t_m\)

Regression splines are functions of

\[
\sum_{j=1}^{m+k+1} \beta_jg_j
\]

where

\(\beta_1,..\beta_{m+k+1}\) are coefficients \(g_1,...,g_{m+k+1}\) are the truncated power basis functions for k-th order splines over the knots \(t_1,...,t_m\)

To estimate the coefficients

\[
\sum_{i=1}^{n} (y_i - \sum_{j=1}^{m} \beta_j g_j (x_i))^2
\]

then regression spline is

\[
\hat{r}(x) = \sum_{j=1}^{m+k+1} \hat{\beta}_j g_j (x)
\]

If we define the basis matrix \(G \in R^{n \times (m+k+1)}\) by

\[
G_{ij} = g_j(x_i) 
\]

where \(i = 1,..,n\) , \(j = 1,..,m+k+1\)

Then,

\[
\sum_{i=1}^{n} (y_i - \sum_{j=1}^{m} \beta_j g_j (x_i))^2 = ||y - G \beta||_2^2
\]

and the regression spline estimate at x is

\[
\hat{r} (x) = g(x)^T \hat{\beta}= g(x)^T(G^TG)^{-1}G^Ty
\]

\hypertarget{natural-splines}{%
\section{Natural splines}\label{natural-splines}}

A natural spline of order k, with knots at \(t_1 <...< t_m\), is a piecewise polynomial function f such that

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  f is polynomial of degree k on each of \([t_1,t_2],...,[t_{m-1},t_m]\)
\item
  f is a polynomial of degree \((k-1)/2\) on \((-\infty,t_1]\) and \([t_m,\infty)\)
\item
  f is continuous and has continuous derivatives of orders 1,.,,, k -1 at its knots \(t_1,..,t_m\)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Note}

natural splines are only defined for odd orders k.

\hypertarget{smoothing-splines}{%
\section{Smoothing splines}\label{smoothing-splines}}

We are interested in a function \(g(x)\) and try to minimize \(RSS = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i -g(x_i))^2\)

But we wouldn't want to overfit (because \(g(x)\) that minimizes this RSS will try to interpolate all of the \(y_i\)), then we want to a smooth \(g(x)\) that minimizes RSS:

\[
\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - g(x_i))^2 + \lambda g''(x)^2 dx
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\lambda\) is a non-negative smoothness
\item
  \(g(.)\) is a \textbf{smoothing spline}
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  RSS here is a \emph{loss function}
\item
  second term is a \emph{smoothness penalty term}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Second derivative controls the smoothness of the function (smaller in absolute value means smoother)
  \item
    Parameter \(\lambda\) controls the tradeoff between the smoothness and fit
  \end{itemize}
\item
  When \(\lambda =0\), the function is just like linear function (not smooth at all)
\item
  When \(\lambda \to \infty\), then \(g\) approaches a straight line that passes through the training data
\item
  The smoothing spline function corresponds to a cubic polynomial basis with knots at the unique values of \(x_1, \dots, x_n\) with continuous first and second derivatives at each knot

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \item
    \(g(x)\) that minimizes the smoothing spline constraint is just a natural cubic spline with knots at the unique \(x\) observations
  \item
    \(\lambda\) controls the level of shrinkage for smoothing splines, but there is none in regression spline
  \item
    At first glance, we might think that smoothing splines might have too many degrees of freedom since we have knots at every data points (i.e., \(K = n\)). But \(\lambda\) controls roughness and make the \textbf{effective degrees of freedom} much smaller (as \(\lambda\) increases from \(0 \to \infty\), \(df_\lambda\) decreases from \(n \to 2\))
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

Let \(\hat{\mathbf{y}}_\lambda\) be the n-vector containing the fitted values for the smoothing spline function for a given \(\lambda\), then

\[
\hat{\mathbf{y}}_\lambda = \mathbf{S}_\lambda \mathbf{y}
\]

which means that it is linear in \(\mathbf{y}\)

According to \citet{hastie2001a} (section 5.4) showed

\(\mathbf{S}_\lambda = \mathbf{N}(\mathbf{N}^T\mathbf{N}+ \lambda \mathbf{\Omega}_N)^{-1} \mathbf{N}^T\) where \(\{N\}_{ij} = b_j (x_i)\) and

\(\{\mathbf{\Omega}\}_{jk} = \int b''_j (t) b''_k(t)dt\)

The effective degrees of freedom to be trace of \(\mathbf{S}_\lambda\) (\textbf{smoother matrix} because it smooths the responses in \(\mathbf{y}\)) is

\[
df_\lambda = \sum_{i=1}^n \{\mathbf{S}_\lambda\}_{ii}
\]

\(\mathbf{S}_\lambda\) is related to other types of smoothing in \protect\hyperlink{local-regression}{Local Regression} and \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing}

To choose \(\lambda\), we can use cross-validation (could also leverage the relationship between \(\lambda\) and effective degrees of freedom).

If we can write a \textbf{linear predictor}, we can use a simplified LOOCV formula:

\[
\begin{aligned}
RSS_{CV}(\lambda)&= \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i - \hat{y}_\lambda^{(-i)}(x_i))^2 \\
&= \sum_{i=1}^n[\frac{y_i - \hat{y}_\lambda(x_i)}{1-\{\mathbf{S}_\lambda\}_{ii}}]^2
\end{aligned}
\]

where we only have to fit the model once for each \(\lambda\)

\hypertarget{multidimensional-splines}{%
\section{Multidimensional Splines}\label{multidimensional-splines}}

We can extend the above one-dimensional spline models to higher dimensions using \textbf{tensor products} to form higher dimensional basis functions

Consider \(X \in R^2\) and

\begin{itemize}
\item
  basis functions \(b_{1j}(X_1), j = 1, \dots, K_1\) for representing coordinate \(X_1\)
\item
  \(b_{2k}(X), k = 1, \dots, K_2\) for coordinate \(X_2\)
\end{itemize}

then the \emph{tensor product basis} is:

\[
g_{jk}(X) = b_{1j}(X_1)b_{2k}(X_2)
\]

where \(j = 1, \dots, K_1, k = 1, \dots, K_2\)

which can be used as

\[
f(X) = \sum_{j=1}^{K_1} \sum_{k=1}^{K_2}\beta_{jk}g_{jk}(X)
\]

Then, we seek to minimize

\[
\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - f(x_i))^2 + \lambda J[f]
\]

where \(J\) is the penalty function for the dimension of the problem

In our example (\(X \in R^2\)), then

\[
J[f] = \int\int[(\frac{\partial^2f(x)}{\partial x_1^2})^2 + 2(\frac{\partial^2 f(x)}{\partial x_1 \partial x_2})^2 + (\frac{\partial^2f(x)}{\partial x^2_2})^2]dx_1 dx_2
\]

Similar to the one-dimensional case

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\lambda \to 0\) gives an interpolating function that goes through all points
\item
  \(\lambda \to \infty\) gives a solution approaching the least squares plane
\item
  We seek a \(\lambda\) that compromises between these extremes
\end{itemize}

This splines method is known as \textbf{thin-plates splines}.

The solution to the thin-plate spline minimization gives

\[
f(x) = \beta_0 + \beta^T x + \sum_{j=1}^n \alpha_j b_j(x)
\]

where \(b_j(x) = ||x - x_j||^2\log ||x-x_j||\) (known as \textbf{radial basis functions})

Additional splines basis functions could be

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  B-spline
\end{itemize}

\protect\hyperlink{local-regression}{Local Regression} and \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing} are related to the spline basis regression formulations.

Another representation

These estimators use a regularized regression over the natural spline basis: placing knots at all points \(x_1,...x_n\)

For the case of cubic splines, the coefficients are the minimization of

\[
||y - G\beta||^2_2 + \lambda \beta^T \Omega \beta
\]

where \(\Omega \in R^{n \times n}\) is the penalty matrix

\[
\Omega_{ij} = \int g''_i(t) g''_j(t) dt,
\]

and i,j = 1,..,n

and \(\lambda \beta^T \Omega \beta\) is the \textbf{regularization term} used to shrink the components of \(\hat{\beta}\) towards 0. \(\lambda > 0\) is the tuning parameter (or smoothing parameter). Higher value of \(\lambda\), faster shrinkage (shrinking away basis functions)

\textbf{Note}\\
smoothing splines have similar fits as kernel regression.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
& Smoothing splines & kernel regression\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
tuning parameter & smoothing parameter \(\lambda\) & bandwidth h\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\includegraphics{advanced-data-analysis_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-13-2.pdf}

\hypertarget{kernel-smoothing}{%
\chapter{Kernel Smoothing}\label{kernel-smoothing}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  also known as kernel regression
\item
  The idea is that we fit a separate, but simple, model at each selected point \(x_0\) by using only those observation that are ``close'' to \(x_0\) such that \(\hat{f}(X)\) is smooth in \(R^p\)
\item
  The ``localization'' is accomplished by a \textbf{weighting function} (or \textbf{kernel}) \(K_\lambda(x_i, x_i)\) that assigns a weight to \(x_i\) based on its distance from \(x_0\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    This kernel depends on a parameter \(\lambda\) that defines the ``width'' or neighborhood" of the kernel
  \end{itemize}
\item
  This method requires less training than traditional regression models (typically, only \(\lambda\) need be chosen), but are \textbf{memory-based methods} in that one needs all the training data any time we want to make a prediction at a location \(x_0\)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{one-dimensional-kernel-smoothers}{%
\section{One-Dimensional Kernel Smoothers}\label{one-dimensional-kernel-smoothers}}

Under k-nearest neighbor, we try to fit

\[
\hat{f}(x) = Ave (y_i |x_i \in N_k(x))
\]

where \(N_k(x)\) refers to the set of k neighbors nearest the point x in squared distance.

Alternatively, we can consider \textbf{``Nadaraya-Watson'' kernel-weighted average} for our estimate

\[
\hat{f}(x_0)= \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n K_\lambda(x_0,x_i) y_i}{\sum_{i=1}^n K_\lambda(x_i, x_i)}
\]

where the kernel is given by

\[
K_\lambda(x_0,x) = D(\frac{|x-x_0|}{\lambda})
\]

The difference is that now we have a \textbf{continuous} function, as compared to \textbf{discrete} under the nearest neighbor fit (which can come in handy in some cases).

Examples of the kernel function

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Epanechinikov quadratic kernel (has no continuous derivatives at the boundary of its support)
\end{enumerate}

\[
D(t) = 
\begin{cases}
\frac{3}{4}(1-t^2) & \text{if } |t| \le \lambda \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  Tri-Cube kernel (compact, has 2 continuous derivatives at the boundary of its support)
\end{enumerate}

\[
D(t) = 
\begin{cases}
(1- |t|^3)^3 & \text{if } |t| \le \lambda \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

where \(t = |x- x_0|\)

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{2}
\tightlist
\item
  Gaussian density function (continuously differentiable, but infinite support, could be more taxing on computation):
\end{enumerate}

\[
D(t) = \frac{\exp(-\frac{t^2}{2\sigma^2})}{\sqrt{2 \pi}\sigma}
\]

where \(\sigma\) is the ``width''

Alternatively, we can also use \textbf{adaptive neighborhoods} where the \(\lambda\) parameter depends on the location \(x_0\) For example,

\[
K_\lambda (x_0, x) = D(\frac{|x-x_0|}{h_\lambda(x_0)})
\]

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Large \(\lambda\) implies lower variance (we average over more observations, larger n), but higher bias (we are approximating \(f(x_0)\) with observation further away from \(x_0\)
\item
  An adaptive parameter \(h_\lambda(x)\) can keep the bias \textbf{constant} but the variance is \textbf{inversely proportional} to the local density of observation.
\item
  Biggest problem with kernel smoothers is estimation near the boundaries (primarily due to the asymmetry of the kernels near the boundary); hence, the fits are badly biased near the boundaries, which \protect\hyperlink{local-regression}{Local Regression} can help.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{selecting-the-kernel-width}{%
\section{Selecting the Kernel Width}\label{selecting-the-kernel-width}}

\(\lambda\) controls the ``width'' of kernel \(K_\lambda\)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Epanechnikov/Tri-Cube: \(\lambda\) is the radius of the support region
\item
  Gaussian: \(\lambda\) is the standard deviation
\item
  Nearest Neighbor: \(\lambda\) is the number of \(k\) of nearest neighbors
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Smaller width means less averaging, hence higher variance and smaller bias
\item
  Larger width means more averaging, hence less variance and higher bias
\end{itemize}

To select \(\lambda\), we typically use \protect\hyperlink{cross-validation}{Cross-validation}

\hypertarget{kernel-density-estimation}{%
\section{Kernel Density Estimation}\label{kernel-density-estimation}}

Another use of kernels is to estimate density (which was the original purpose of kernel before kernel regression).

Kernel density estimation is

Assume random sample \(x_1, \dots, x_n\) from a probability density \(f_X(x)\) and we want to estimate \(f_X\) at a point \(x_0\) (assuming one dimension \(X \in R\))

The \textbf{Parzen estimate} is

\[
\hat{f}_X(x_0) = \frac{1}{n \lambda}\sum_{i=1}^n K_\lambda (x_0, x_i)
\]

where a choice of kernels is the Gaussian kernel with standard deviation \(\lambda\), which could be extended to higher dimensional density estimation by using multivariate (Gaussian) kernels.

Non parametric density estimates can be used under the Bayes' theorem

Suppose \(J\) classes and fit the nonparametric densities \(\hat{f}_j (X), j = 1, \dots, J\) separately for each class

Assume we have prior probability for each class \(\hat{\pi}_j\) (which might be from the sample proportions or some previous knowledge), then

\[
\hat{P}(G=j | X=x_0) = \frac{\hat{\pi}_j \hat{f}_j (x_0)}{\sum_{k=1}^J \hat{\pi}_k \hat{f}_k (x_0)}
\]

For more details, see \citet{hastie2001a} (section 6.6)

\hypertarget{local-regression}{%
\chapter{Local Regression}\label{local-regression}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  This method improve bias from \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing} at the boundaries by fitting straight lines instead of constants.
\item
  Also known as the \textbf{locally weighted linear regression}
\end{itemize}

It seeks to

\[
\min_{\alpha(x_0), \beta(x_0)} \sum_{1}^n K_\lambda (x_0, x_i)[y_i - \alpha (x_0) - \beta(x_0)x_i]^2
\]

and gives the estimate \(\hat{f}(x_0) =\hat{\alpha}(x_0)+ \hat{\beta}(x_0)x_0\)

To fit this, we let \(b(x)^T = (1,x)\) then define the \(n \times 2\) matrix \(\mathbf{B}\) to have i-th row given by \(b(x_i)^T\)

Then, let \(\mathbf{W}(x_0)\) be the \(n \times n\) diagonal matrix with the i-th diagonal given by \(K_\lambda(x_0, x_i)\). Then

\[
\hat{f}(x_0)=b(x_0)^T (\mathbf{B}^T \mathbf{W}(x_0)\mathbf{B})^{-1}\mathbf{B}^T \mathbf{W}(x_0)\mathbf{y} = \sum_{i=1}^n l_i(x_0)y_i
\]

Thus, the fitted solution is linear in the \(y_i\) with the weights in the linear combination given by \(l_i(x_0)\), which are a combination of the kernel weights and least squares equations, which is called \textbf{equivalent kernel}

An advantage of this method is that it corrects the boundary bias issues to first order (i.e., the bias is only a function of the second derivatives of \(f(x_0)\) and so is small on average).

Thus, there is a small price we pay in that this fit tends to be a little bit biased in regions of curvature in the true function.

We can improve this bias by using \textbf{locally polynomial regression} (e.g., \textbf{locally quadratic regression}):

\[
\min_{\alpha(x_0), \beta_j(x_0),j = 1, \dots, d}\sum_{i=1}^n K_\lambda(x_0,x_i)[y_i - \alpha(x_0)- \sum_{j=1}^d \beta_j (x_0)x_i^j]^2
\]

which gives the solution \(\hat{f}(x_0) = \hat{\alpha}(x_0)+ \sum_{j=1}^d \hat{\beta}_j(x_0)x_0^j\)

The reduction in bias under locally quadratic fit offset by higher variance

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Local linear fits are much better at the boundaries than higher order polynomials in reducing bias, without leading to excessive variance increase.
\item
  Local quadratic fits do not help bias much at the boundaries, but increase the variance a lot
\item
  Local quadratic fits are usually used in reducing bias from curvature in the interior of the domain
\item
  Local polynomials of odd degree dominate those of even degree (based on asymptotic analysis because asymptotically the MSE is dominated by boundary effects).
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{higher-dimensions}{%
\section{Higher Dimensions}\label{higher-dimensions}}

To generalize \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing} and \protect\hyperlink{local-regression}{Local Regression} with \(p\)-dimensional kernel, we consider local hyperplanes

Example:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If \(d= 1\) (polynomial degree) and \(p=2\), we have \(b^T(X) = (1,X_1,X_2)\)
\item
  If \(d =2\) and \(p=2\) we have \(b^T(X) = (1, X_1, X_2, X_1^2, X_2^2, X_1 X_2)\)
\end{itemize}

then, we solve

\[
\min_{\beta(x_0)} \sum_{i=1}^n K_\lambda(x_0,x_i)[y_i -b(x_i)^T \beta(x_0)]^2
\]

to fit \(\hat{f}(x_0) = b(x_0)^T \hat{\beta}(x_0)\)

Then, the kernels of the general form would be

\[
K_\lambda(x_0, x) = D(\frac{||x-x_0||}{\lambda})
\]

where \(||.||\) is the Euclidean norm.

Note: because the Euclidean norm is sensitive to the scale fo the variables, we have to standardize the predictors first.

The boundary bias issues are exponentiated under higher dimensions because of the \protect\hyperlink{curse-of-dimensionality}{Curse of dimensionality} (larger fraction of the data locations are on or near the boundary)

Similar to the one-dimensional case, the local polynomial regression accounts for this boundary bias issue to first order. However, the problem is to balance localness (low bias) and low variance as the dimension increases without the sample size increasing exponentially in \(p\)

Hence, we do not see and \textbf{should not use smoothing or local polynomial regression in dimensions beyond three.}

Adding structural assumption to the modeling framework can help in cases where the dimension to sample size ratio high.

An example under kernels could be

\[
K_{\lambda, \mathbf{A}(x_0, x)}= D(\frac{(x-x_0)^T \mathbf{A}(x-x_0)}{\lambda})
\]

where \(\mathbf{A}\) is a \(p \times p\) positive semi-definite matrix, where it can give different weights to the elements of \(x-x_0\) and/or recombine the elements fo this vector in various ways to shrink some dimensions (``compress'') the points into a smaller region of space

Another way would be do add structure through \textbf{varying coefficient models}

Assume \(p\) inputs and a regression on the first \(q\) variables where the parameters depend on the remaining inputs \(Z= (X_{q+1}, X_{q+2}, \dots, X_p)\)

\[
f(x) = \alpha (Z) + \beta_1 (Z) X_1 + \dots + \beta_q (Z) X_q
\]

where the coefficients are functions of \(Z\)

Under Kernel weighted local regressions, then

\[
\min_{\alpha (z_0), \beta(z_0)} \sum_{i=1}^n K_\lambda (z_0,z_i)[y_i - \alpha(z_0)-x_{1i} \beta_1(z_0)- \dots - x_{qi} \beta_q (z_0)]^2
\]

\hypertarget{application-1}{%
\section{Application}\label{application-1}}

Alternative package \texttt{locfit}

\hypertarget{part-2.-transformation-based}{%
\part{2. TRANSFORMATION BASED}\label{part-2.-transformation-based}}

\hypertarget{generalized-additive-models}{%
\chapter{Generalized Additive Models}\label{generalized-additive-models}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A more flexible method for nonlinear regression models
\end{itemize}

\[
E(Y|X_1,X_2,\dots,X_p)= \alpha + f_1(X_1) + f_2(X_2)+ \dots + f_p(X_p)
\]

where \(f_j(.),j = 1,\dots, p\) are unspecified smooth (nonparametric) functions

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  We call this model additive because we separate \(f_j\) for each \(X_j\)
\item
  Complicated functions for each features are allowed, and we can still retain the interpretability
\end{itemize}

Equivalently, the model can be rewritten as

\[
y_i = \alpha + \sum_{j=1}^p f_j (x_{ij}) + \epsilon_i
\]

\(\epsilon\) usually is chosen to follow Gaussian distribution

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If each of the function \(f_j(.)\) were written as a basis expansion, then we are back at least squares
\item
  A general approach to fit each function would be a ``scatterplot smoother'' (e.g., cubic \protect\hyperlink{smoothing-splines}{Smoothing splines} or \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing}) using an algorithm that simultaneously estimates all p-functions
\item
  Transformation of the mean response (e.g., link function) as in \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/generalized-linear-models.html}{Generalized linear models} can also be applied.
\end{itemize}

Example:

Consider two-group classification via logistic regression. We relate the mean of the binary response

\[
\mu(X) = P(Y=1|X)
\]

to functions of the predictors via an additive regression model using a logit link function

\[
\log(\frac{\mu(X)}{1- \mu(X)}) = \alpha + f_1 (X_1) + \dots + f_p(X_p)
\]

where each \(f_j\) is an unspecified smooth function

In general, we relate the conditional mean response to the additive function of the predictors via a link function, \(g\):

\[
g(\mu(X)) = \alpha + f_1(X_1) + \dots + f_p(X_p)
\]

Examples:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(g(\mu) = \mu\) the identity link
\item
  \(g(\mu) = logit(\mu)\) or \(g(\mu) = probit(\mu)\) as in the \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/generalized-linear-models.html}{glm} case for binary and binomial data (probit function is the inverse Gaussian cumulative distribution function: \(probit (\mu) = \Phi^{-1}(\mu)\)
\item
  \(g(\mu) = \log(\mu)\): Poisson count data
\end{itemize}

Function \(f_j\) can be nonlinear or linear or any other parametric forms. or a combination of them:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(g(\mu) = X^T \beta + \alpha_k + f(Z)\): a semi-parametric model, where

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \(X\) is a vector of predictors to be modeled linearly
  \item
    \(\alpha_k\) the effect for the k-th level of a qualitative input V
  \item
    the effect of the predictor \(Z\) is modeled nonparametrically
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \(g(\mu) = f(X) + g_k(Z)\) where

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(k\) indexes the levels of a qualitative input V and thus creates an interaction term with Z, \(g(V,Z) =g_k(Z)\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \(g(\mu)= f(X)+g(Z,W)\): where \(g\) is a nonparametric function in two features, \(Z\) and \(W\)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{fitting-additive-models}{%
\section{Fitting Additive Models}\label{fitting-additive-models}}

We choose the function \(f_j\) to be ``smoothers.'' Then, we minimize a penalized sum of squares

\[
PRSS(\alpha, f_1,\dots, f_p) = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \alpha- \sum_{j=1}^p f_j(x_{ij}))^2 + \sum_{j=1}^p \lambda_j \int f''_j (t_j)^2 dt_j
\]

where the \(\lambda_j \ge 0\) are tuning parameters

The minimizer is an additive cubic spline model with each of the functions \(f_j\) a cubic spline in \(X_j\) and with knots at each of the unique values of \(x_{ij}, i = 1,. \dots, n\)

This solution is not unique because \(\alpha\) is not identifiable without another constraint. Hence, we add \(\sum_{i=1}^n f_j(x_{ij})=0 \forall j\) which implies \(\hat{\alpha}= ave(y_i)\)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If \(\mathbf{X} = \{x_{ij}\}\) is of full column rank, then the minimizer is unique
\item
  If this isn't the case (i.e., \(\mathbf{X'X}\) is singular) then the \textbf{linear portions} of the components \(f_j\) c\textbf{annot be uniquely determined, but the nonlinear parts can}
\end{itemize}

To fit the model, we have to use an iterative (backfitting) procedure

Let \(S_j\) be the cubic smoothing spline for the j-th function. We apply it to the target residuals \(\{y_i - \hat{\alpha} - \sum_{k \neq j}\hat{f}(x_{ik}) \}^n_1\) as a function of \(x_{ij}\) to obtain the new estimate \(\hat{f}_j\)

The procedure is done for each predictor using the current estimates of the other functions \(\hat{f}_k\) when computing the differences.

The process continue until the estimates \(\hat{f}_j\) stabilize

\hypertarget{the-backfitting-algorithm-for-additive-models}{%
\subsection{The Backfitting Algorithm for Additive Models}\label{the-backfitting-algorithm-for-additive-models}}

Step 1: Initialize

\[
\hat{\alpha} = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{1}^N y_i, \hat{f}_j \equiv 0, \forall i, j
\]

Step 2: \(j = 1,2, \dots, p, \dots, 1, 2, \dots, p, \dots\)

\[
\hat{f}_j \leftarrow S_j [\{ y_i - \hat{\alpha}- \sum_{k \neq j} \hat{f}_k (x_{ik})\}^N_1]
\]

\[
\hat{f}_j \leftarrow \hat{f}_j - \frac{1}{N} \sum_{i=1}^N \hat{f}_j (x_{ij})
\]

until the functions \(\hat{f}_j\) change less than a prespecified threshold

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Any smoother can be used to take the place of \(S_j\) such as

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    other univariate regression smoothers (e.g., local polynomial regression and \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing})
  \item
    linear regression operators yielding polynomial fits, piecewise constant fits, parametric spline fits, Fourier series fits
  \item
    smoothers for second or higher-order interactions or periodic smoothers for seasonal effect.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  If we consider the smoother at the training points, it can be represented by the \(n \times n\) smoother matrix \(\mathbf{S}_j\) which can be used to estimate the df associated with the j-th term from \(trace(\mathbf{S}_j) -1\)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{fitting-generalized-additive-models}{%
\section{Fitting Generalized Additive Models}\label{fitting-generalized-additive-models}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The optimization criterion here is the penalized log-likelihood
\item
  It maximizes by combining the backfitting procedure with a likelihood maximizer
\item
  The weighted linear regression component is replaced by a weighed backfitting algorithm \citet{hastie2001a} (section 9.2)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{local-scoring-algorithm}{%
\subsection{Local Scoring Algorithm}\label{local-scoring-algorithm}}

Step 1: Compute \(\hat{\alpha} = \log [\frac{\bar{y}}{1- \bar{y}}]\) where \(\bar{y} = ave(y_i)\), the sample proportion of ones, and set \(\hat{f}_j\equiv 0 \forall j\)

Step 2: Define \(\hat{\eta}_i = \hat{\alpha} + \sum_j \hat{f}_j(x_{ij})\) and \(\hat{p}_i = \frac{1}{1 + \exp(-\hat{\eta}_i)}\)

Iterate:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Construct the working target variable: \(z_i = \hat{\eta}_i + \frac{y_i - \hat{p}_i}{\hat{p}_i(1-\hat{p}_i)}\)
\item
  Construct weights \(w_i = \hat{p}_i (1-\hat{p}_i)\)
\item
  FIt an additive model to the targets \(z_i\) with weights \(w_i\), using a weighted backfitting algorithm. This gives new estimates \(\hat{\alpha}, \hat{f}_j, \forall j\)
\end{enumerate}

Step 3: Continue step 2, until the change in the functions falls below a pre-specified threshold.

\hypertarget{summary}{%
\section{Summary}\label{summary}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}\raggedright
Pros\strut

Cons\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}\raggedright
we can fit a nonlinear \(f_j\) to each \(X_j\) and automatically model nonlinear relationships (which we can't do under standard linear regression)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.57}}\raggedright
Model is restricted to be \textbf{additive}. Technically, we can include interactions, but we have to make them manually, and we can only do so with low-dimensional interaction terms as additive functions\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}\raggedright
The nonlinear fits can make more accurate \textbf{predictions}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.57}}\raggedright
Difficult to fit in high dimensions (because all variables have to be fit)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}\raggedright
Since the model is additive, we can examine the effect of each \(X_j\) on \(Y\) individually while holding all of the other variables fixed (interprebility)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.57}}\raggedright
\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}\raggedright
The smoothness of the function \(f_j\) for the variable \(X_j\) can be summarized by df\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.57}}\raggedright
\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

An extension of GAM is to include random effects, which is called \textbf{Generalized Additive Mixed Models} (GAMMs) (can refer to \href{https://highstat.com/index.php/beginner-s-guide-to-generalized-additive-models-with-r}{this book})

\begin{verbatim}
## Analysis of Deviance Table
## 
## Model 1: wage ~ s(age, 5) + education
## Model 2: wage ~ year + s(age, 5) + education
