\hypertarget{gaussian-process-regression}{%
\chapter{Gaussian Process Regression}\label{gaussian-process-regression}}

Suggested reading:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \citet{rasmussen2005}
\item
  \citet{wikle2019} (section 4.2)
\end{itemize}

Under Gaussian Process (GP), we assume that \(Y\) is a random (Gaussian) process that is controlled by a mean function and covariance function.

Response model:

\[
z_i = Y_i + \epsilon_i, i = 1, \dots, n
\]

Assumptions:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(Y\) = a latent process specified by a GP
\item
  \(\epsilon_i\) independent and identically distributed mean-zero observation errors that are independent of \(Y\) and have variance \(\sigma_\epsilon^2\)
\item
  the errors are assumed to have a normal (Gaussian) distribution \(\epsilon_i \sim iid N (0, \sigma^2_\epsilon)\) these errors could be correlated
\end{itemize}

Consider a \textbf{linear predictor} of the form

\[
\hat{Y}(x_0) = k_0 + \sum_{i=1}^n w_i z_i
\]

where \(w_i\) are weights and \(k_0\) is some constant

To pick these weights, we choose ``optimal'' weight based on mean squared error notion, which requires us to consider the random process that controls \(Y\)

\hypertarget{latent-process-model}{%
\section{Latent Process Model}\label{latent-process-model}}

Assume that we have the latent process follows the model

\[
Y(x) = f(x)
\]

where \(f(x) \sim GP (m(x), c(x,x'))\) is a random process (specifically a Gaussian Process) and

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(m(x)\) = mean function \(E[f(x)]\)
\item
  \(c(x,x')\) = covariance function \(c(x,x)'= E[(f(x) - m(x)) (f(x') - m(x'))^T]\)
\end{itemize}

The difference between \textbf{Gaussian distribution} and a \textbf{Gaussian process} is

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Gaussian Distribution: a distribution over vectors

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Fully specified by a \textbf{mean vector} and \textbf{covariance matrix}: (e.g., \(\mathbf{Y} \sim N(\mathbf{\mu}, \mathbf{\Sigma})\) which is a multivariate normal distribution)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Gaussian Process: a distribution over functions

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Fully specified by a mean function (\(m\)) and covariance function (\(c\)) (e.g., \(Y \sim GP(m,c)\) , where we specify a mean function and covariance function to define a GP)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{gaussian-processes}{%
\section{Gaussian Processes}\label{gaussian-processes}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A GP is an infinite-dimensional object
\item
  Allow us to make a prediction anywhere in our \(x\) domain assuming that we know the mean and covariance functions (data generating process), we need the covariance between ANY two \(x\) locations
\item
  We actually need to consider finite-dimensional distributions because

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    We only have a finite set of data and also we are interested in a finite set of predictions
  \item
    Any finite collection of GP random variables has a joint Gaussian distributions (i.e., a multivariate normal distribution).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{gaussian-distributions}{%
\section{Gaussian Distributions}\label{gaussian-distributions}}

\[
\left(
\begin{array}
{c}
\mathbf{v} \\
\mathbf{v}_*
\end{array}
\right)
\sim 
Gau 
\left(
\left(
\begin{array}
{c}
\mathbf{\mu} \\
\mathbf{\mu}_*
\end{array}
\right),
\left(
\begin{array}
{cc}
\mathbf{\Sigma} & \mathbf{\Sigma}_* \\
\mathbf{\Sigma}^T & \mathbf{\Sigma}_{**}
\end{array}
\right)
\right)
\]

where \(E(\mathbf{v}) = \mathbf{\mu},E(\mathbf{v}_*) = \mathbf{\mu}_*, \mathbf{\Sigma}= cov(\mathbf{v}, \mathbf{v}), \mathbf{\Sigma}= cov(\mathbf{v}, \mathbf{v}_*),\mathbf{\Sigma}_{**} = cov(\mathbf{v}_*, \mathbf{v}_*)\)

Then, partition

\[
\mathbf{v}_* | \mathbf{v} \sim Gau (\mathbf{\mu}_*+ \mathbf{\Sigma}^T_* \mathbf{\Sigma}^{-1} (\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{\mu}), \mathbf{\Sigma}_{**} - \mathbf{\Sigma}^T_* \mathbf{\Sigma}^{-1} \mathbf{\Sigma}_*)
\]

\hypertarget{optimal-linear-prediction}{%
\section{Optimal Linear Prediction}\label{optimal-linear-prediction}}

We are interested in predicting \(Y(x_0)\) (i.e., at some arbitrary \(x\)) given the data \(\mathbf{z} \equiv (z_1, \dots, z_n)^T\)

Assume that the mean function \(\mu(x) \forall x\) (e.g., a constant)

We seek linear predictors that minimize the mean squared prediction error of the form

\[
\begin{aligned}
\hat{Y} (x_0) &=  k(x_0) + \sum_{i=1}^n w_i (x_0) z_i \\
&= k(x_0) + \mathbf{w}^T_0 \mathbf{z}
\end{aligned}
\]

The optimal linear predictor is also the conditional expectation \(E(Y(x_0) |\mathbf{z})\).

Hence, we seek the conditional distribution \(Y(x_0) |\mathbf{z}\)

Rewriting the data model in vector form:

\[
\mathbf{z} = \mathbf{Y} + \epsilon, \epsilon \sim Gau(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{C}_\epsilon)
\]

where \(\mathbf{Y}\) and \(\epsilon\) are ordered in the same way as the data vector \(\mathbf{z}\)

\[
cov(\mathbf{Y}, \mathbf{Y}) \equiv \mathbf{C}_y
\]

\[
cov(\epsilon,\epsilon) \equiv \mathbf{C}_\epsilon
\]

\[
cov(\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z}) = \mathbf{C}_y + \mathbf{C}_\epsilon
\]

\[
\mathbf{c}^T_0 \equiv cov( Y(x_0), \mathbf{z})
\]

\[
c_{0,0} \equiv var(Y(x_0))
\]

\(\mu_0\) is mean at \(x_0\) and \(\mathbf{\mu}\) is the mean corresponding to the observations.

Then, the joint Gaussian distribution:

\[
\left(
\begin{array}
{c}
Y (x_0) \\
\mathbf{v}_*
\end{array}
\right)
\sim 
Gau 
\left(
\left(
\begin{array}
{c}
\mathbf{\mu} \\
\mathbf{\mu}
\end{array}
\right),
\left(
\begin{array}
{cc}
c_{0,0} & \mathbf{c}^T_0 \\
\mathbf{c}_0 & \mathbf{C}_{z}
\end{array}
\right)
\right)
\]

Using the joint/conditional \protect\hyperlink{gaussian-distributions}{Gaussian Distributions} formula

\[
Y(x_0)|\mathbf{z} \sim Gau (\mu_0+ \mathbf{c}^T_0 \mathbf{C}_z^{-1}(\mathbf{z}- \mathbf{\mu}), c_{0,0}- \mathbf{c}^T_0 \mathbf{C}_z^{-1} \mathbf{c}_0)
\]

The predictor is given by the \textbf{mean} of this distribution

\[
\hat{Y}(x_0) = \mu_0 + \mathbf{c}_0^T \mathbf{C}^{-1}_z(\mathbf{z} - \mathbf{\mu})
\]

and the prediction \textbf{variance} is

\[
\sigma^2_{Y_0} = c_{0,0} - \mathbf{c}^T_0 \mathbf{C}_z^{-1} \mathbf{c}_0
\]

This predictor is the \textbf{optimal predictor} because it minimizes the \textbf{mean squared prediction error}:

\[
MSPE = E[(Y(x_0) - \hat{Y}(x_0))^2]
\]

Hence, the \textbf{weights} are given by

\[
\mathbf{w}_0 = \mathbf{c}'_0 \mathbf{C}_z^{-1}
\]

and

\[
k(x_0) = \mu_0 - \mathbf{c}'_0 \mathbf{C}^{-1}_z \mathbf{\mu}
\]

which is an effective kernel (just like \protect\hyperlink{spline-regression}{Spline Regression} and \protect\hyperlink{local-regression}{Local Regression}

These formulas can be extended to multiple locations (each location has its own vector of weights). However, if there are a lot of sample locations, there could be computational problem regarding calculating the matrix inverse \(\mathbf{C}_z^{-1}\)

In cases where you have no measurement errors (i.e., \(z_i = y_i\)) (equivalently, replacing \(\mathbf{C}_z\) with \(\mathbf{C}_y\))

\hypertarget{covariance-functions}{%
\section{Covariance Functions}\label{covariance-functions}}

To predict at new \(x\) locations, we must specify:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  a mean function (doable)
\item
  a covariance function between ANY two locations (hard)
\end{enumerate}

In the real world, we hardly ever know the variances and covariances that make up \(\mathbf{C}_y, \mathbf{C}_\epsilon, \mathbf{c}_0, c_{0,0}\)

A seemingly simple solution is to specify covariance functions in terms of parameters \(\mathbf{\theta}\) that can give a covariance between any pairs of coordinates and then estimate the parameters, which is the same thing that we did in \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/linear-mixed-models.html}{Linear Mixed Models} (e.g., AR models, exponential models)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Covariance functions must be positive semi-definite} to ensure that prediction variances are non-negative
\item
  Covariance functions should be realistic (i.e., account for realistic dependencies), which can be difficult sometime. Typically, the assumption that ``nearby'' observations (similar in the case of \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing}) are more dependent is sufficient.
\end{itemize}

In practice, we usually have to employ a \textbf{stationarity assumption}

Example:

If the random process is assumed to be second-order (weakly) stationary then it has constant expectation and a covariance function that can be expressed in terms of the distance between the \(x\) locations (\(h = x- x'\)), we specify

\[
c(x,x') = c(h, \mathbf{\theta})
\]

In two or more dimensions, if this distance lag does not depend on direction (i.e., \(h = ||\mathbf{h}||\)), then we call the function \textbf{isotropic}

The benefits of stationarity assumption:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  allows for more \textbf{parsimonious parameterization} of the covariance function
\item
  provides \textbf{pseudo-replication} of dependencies at given lags, which facilitate estimation
\end{itemize}

To construct covariance functions with this assumption, traditional machine learning would use \textbf{separable covariance functions} (e.g., for \(x_1\) and \(x_2\) - 2 dimension prediction)

\[
c(h_1;h_2) \equiv c(h_1) \times c_2(h_2)
\]

which is valid if both covariance functions are valid

This separability property helps with computation in prediction locations (e.g., matrix inverses we mention above), but it can also be unrealistic in cases like spatio-temporal data

Then to have valid spatial and temporal covariance functions, we can employ the following covariance functions:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The Matern
\item
  Power exponential: \(c(\tau, \theta) = \sigma^2 \exp \{ - \frac{|\tau|}{\theta} \}\) where \(\sigma^2\) is the variance parameter and \(\theta\) is the dependence parameter
\item
  Gaussian classes
\end{itemize}

To estimate the parameters in the covariance functions, and in the mean function we can either

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Under the frequentist perspective, we can use REML, MLE (like \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/linear-mixed-models.html}{Linear Mixed Models}) and BLUP for random process
\item
  Under the Bayesian perspective, we use GP for \(f(x)\) as prior distribution, and update this with the observations, \(\mathbf{z}\)
\end{enumerate}

With a lot of data locations, REML, MLE, and Bayesian can be problematic because of the high-dimensional covariance matrix \(\mathbf{C}_z, \mathbf{C}_y\) (which research are still being conducted to improve)

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If we model the mean function in terms of covariates (\(\mu(x) = \mathbf{w}(x)'\mathbf{\beta}\)m where \(\mathbf{w}\) is a vector of covariates. Then, it's \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/linear-mixed-models.html}{Linear Mixed Models} with difference that the covariates must be available for ANY \(x\)
\item
  In cases where you don't measurement uncertainty in the observation, (\(\epsilon\)) then the prediction at an observation location will be exactly the same as the observation. And the other way around will filter the uncertainty with the observation
\item
  we can also have non-Gaussian observations conditioned on the GP (like \href{https://bookdown.org/mike/data_analysis/nonlinear-and-generalized-linear-mixed-models.html}{GLMM})
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{application-3}{%
\section{Application}\label{application-3}}

Other packages: \texttt{mlegp}

\citet{macdonald2015}

Simulation:

\[
y(x) = \log(x + .1) + sin(5 \pi x)
\]

A Latin Hypercube design is a space-filling design, used in the design of computer experiments.

The Maximin version does space-filling design by maximizing the minimum distance between design points.

\begin{Shaded}

\NormalTok{\}}

\includegraphics{advanced-data-analysis_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-18-1.pdf}

\hypertarget{part-3.-tree-based}{%
\part{3. TREE BASED}\label{part-3.-tree-based}}

\hypertarget{classification-and-regression-trees}{%
\chapter{Classification and Regression Trees}\label{classification-and-regression-trees}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Segmenting the predictor space into a number of simple regions.
\item
  Develop a collection of splitting rules that make these segments.
\item
  The splitting rules can be thought of as ``tree'' (therefore, ``decision trees'')
\item
  The root of the tree (i.e., the first splitting variable) is typically chosen based on the the smallest sum of squared residuals (SSRs), then the process begins again to choose another branch.
\item
  Decision trees can be used for both classification and regression, ergo the name CART

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Under regression, the prediction is the average of the partitioned predictor space (i.e., segments)
  \item
    Under classification, we classify based on the most prevalent class in each segment
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Compared to linear models

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    If the underlying relationship is linear, the linear regression/ classification setting works better than CART
  \item
    IF the underlying relationship is nonlinear and complex between the features and the response, the CART model can be better.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Issues:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Categorical predictors can prove to be difficult to implement CART when we have \(q >2\) possible unordered values of the predictors as there are \(2^{q-1} - 1\) possible partitions of the q values into 2 groups. Usually lead to over-fitting.
  \item
    Missing predictor values:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      For categorical variables, we can make a separate category for ``missing''. Or we can construct surrogate variables that mimic the split of the training data using only the non-missing variables
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Non-binary splits: not recommended because it splits the data too quickly and doesn't leave enough data for the next level of the tree
  \item
    Linear Combination Splits: we can make linear combination of the variables to choose the splits (akin to PC-regression), which can help prediction, but reduce interpretation
  \item
    Instability of trees: CART has high variance (i.e., a small change in the data can lead to different splits). To handle this problem, we can use ``bagging'' (i.e., bootstrapping procedure). This can happen even after you prune.
  \item
    Lack of Smoothness: CART can lead to non-smooth prediction surface, which can be problematic for regression applications. The MARS procedure can be used instead
  \item
    Additive structure: Since CART uses binary structure, it can't accommodate additive structure, but MARS can
  \item
    Trees do not have good predictive accuracy as other regression and classification approaches.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Advantages:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Easily explainable (to non-statisticians)
\item
  Decision trees may be closer to human decision making than traditional regression and classification
\item
  Trees are easily interpreted (with graphical depiction)
\item
  Trees can handle qualitative predictors without dummy variables
\end{itemize}

\hfill\break

\hypertarget{regression-trees}{%
\section{Regression Trees}\label{regression-trees}}

Say we have \(p\) inputs and a response for each of \(n\) observations, \((x_i, y_i), i = 1, \dots, n\) with \(x_i = (x_{i1}, \dots , x_{ip})^T\)

Suppose that we have a partition of the predictor space into \(J\) regions \(R_1, \dots, R_j\) . Then, our prediction in each region is some constant, \(c_j\). Thus, we could write the response function as

\[
f(x) = \sum_{j=1}^J c_j I (x \in R_j)
\]

where \(I(x \in R_j)\) is an indicator function (1 when \(x\) is in the j-th region, 0 otherwise).

Given the partitions, the estimate \(\hat{c}_j\) is the average of of \(y_i\) in \(R_j\)

\[\hat{c}_j = \hat{y}_{R_j} \equiv ave(y_i |x_i \in R_j)\] To find the partitions, we seek to divide the predictor space into high-dimensional rectangles (boxes) (i.e., we want to find the boxes \(R_1, \dots, R_j\) that minimize the residual sum of squares)

\[
\sum_{j=1}^J \sum_{i \in R_j} (y_i - \hat{y}_{R_j})^2
\]

where \(\hat{y}_{R_j}\) is the mean response for the training observations within the j-th box (e..g, \(\hat{c}_j\) )

Since we can't consider every possible partition (computationally constraint), we seek binary partitions and specifically, a top down, greedy approach (i.e., at each step the best split is made at that step, once we make a split, we don't go back and change it) known as \textbf{recursive binary splitting}.

\hypertarget{recursive-binary-splitting}{%
\subsection{Recursive Binary Splitting}\label{recursive-binary-splitting}}

Step 1: we select the predictors \(X_j\) and the cutpoints such that splitting the predictor space into the regions \(\{ X|X_j <s\}\) and \(\{ X | X_j \ge s\}\) leads to the maximum reduction in RSS (i.e., for any j and s, we define the regions (half-planes):

\[
R_1 (j,s) = \{ X|X_j <s\} ; R_2(j,s) = \{ X|X_j \ge s\}
\]

and seek the value of j and s the minimizes

\[
\sum_{i: x_i \in R_1 (j,s)} (y_i - \hat{y}_{R_1})^2 + \sum_{i: x_i \in R_2 (j,s)} (y_i - \hat{y}_{R_2})^2
\]

where \(\hat{y}_{R_1} = \hat{c}_1\) is the mean response in \(R_1(j,s)\)

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  When \(p\) is small, it's computationally efficient to find values of j and s.
\item
  After the partition is selected, we repeat the process, looking for the best predictor (j) and best cut point (s) to split one of the two new regions to minimize the RSS, which will give us three regions. We continue to split one of these three regions to minimize the RSS
\item
  The stopping criterion is usually when a region contains fewer than some specified number of observations (because we have to the averages of these regressions for our mean response)
\item
  The end regressions of the tree \((R_1, \dots, R_J)\) are known as \textbf{terminal nodes} or \textbf{leaves} of the tree
\item
  The points along the tree where the predictor space is spit are \textbf{internal nodes}
\item
  Regression trees can usually lead to over-fitting on the test set, to reduce this problem, we can prune (i.e., remove leaves) to obtain a \textbf{subtree} using cross-validation.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{cost-complexity-pruning}{%
\subsection{Cost complexity pruning}\label{cost-complexity-pruning}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  also known as \textbf{weakest link pruning}
\end{itemize}

Intuitively,

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Calculate the total Sum of Squared Residual for each tree
\item
  Calculate a Tree Score: \(\text{Tree Score} = SSR + \alpha T\) where

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    SSR = Sum of Squared Residuals for the tree or sub-tree
  \item
    T = number of leaves (or Terminal nodes) in the tree or sub-tree
  \item
    \(\alpha\) = tuning parameter (based on cross-validation)
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Pick tree with lowest tree score
\end{enumerate}

The Tree Complexity Penalty (\(\alpha T\)) compensates for the difference in the number of leaves

Mathematically,

We first grow a very large tree \(T_0\). Instead of considering every possible subtree, we consider a sequence of trees indeed a non-negative tuning parameters, \(\alpha\) (i.e., for each value of \(\alpha\) there is a subtree \(T\) such that \(T \in T_0\) that minimizes

\[
\sum_{j=1}^{|T|} \sum_{i:x_i \in R_j} (y_i - \hat{y}_{R_j})^2 + \alpha |T|
\]

where \(|T|\) is the number of terminal nodes

The parameter \(\alpha\) controls a tradoff between the subtree's complexity and how well it fits the training data (\(\alpha = 0\) implies \(T = T_0\)). As \(\alpha\) gets larger, the penalty for a larger tree is higher, so it penalizes similarly to the \protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso regression}. Moreover, as \(\alpha\) increases, the branches get pruned in a nested a predictable fashion. Hence, it's possible to get the whole sequence of subtrees as a function of \(\alpha\) (based on cross-validation). After getting \(\alpha\) using cross-validation, we can use that value with the full data set to obtain the fitted subtree

\hypertarget{algorithm-for-regression-tree}{%
\subsection{Algorithm for regression tree}\label{algorithm-for-regression-tree}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{recursive-binary-splitting}{Recursive Binary Splitting} for growing a tree with your choice of stopping rule (e.g., observation-based)
\item
  Use \protect\hyperlink{cost-complexity-pruning}{Cost complexity pruning} to obtain a sequence of best subtrees as a function of \(\alpha\)
\item
  Use \protect\hyperlink{k-fold-cross-validation}{K-Fold Cross-Validation} to choose \(\alpha\)
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}

\hypertarget{classification-trees}{%
\section{Classification Trees}\label{classification-trees}}

When we have a qualitative response we can use classification tree.

The main difference from \protect\hyperlink{regression-trees}{Regression Trees} is that we predict the response based on the most commonly occurring class in the region associated with that terminal node.

We may also be interested in the class proportion for each node when interpreting a classification tree

We grow a classification tree in the same manner as the \protect\hyperlink{regression-trees}{Regression Trees}, with \protect\hyperlink{recursive-binary-splitting}{Recursive Binary Splitting}. However, instead of using RSS we use other measure related to the classification summary.

First we define the proportion of class \(k\) observation in the j-th node as

\[
\hat{p}_{jk} = \frac{1}{n_j} \sum_{x_i \in R_j} I(y_i = k)
\]we classify an observation in node j to class k with the maximum value of \(\hat{p}_{jk}\)

The three measures for growing tree:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Classification error rate: \(1 - \max_{{k}} (\hat{p}_{jk})\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    measures the fraction of the training observations in that region that do not belong to the most common class
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Gini index: \(\sum_{k=1}^K \hat{p}_{jk}(1 - \hat{p}_{jk})\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    measures the total variance across the K classes
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Cross-Entropy or Deviance: \(-\sum_{k=1}^K \hat{p}_{jk} \log (\hat{p}_{jk})\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    related to an information measures and the deviance
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

The Gini index and Cross-Entory both take small values when all \(\hat{p}_{jk}\) are close to 1 or 0. Hence, we say they measure node purity, which are more sensitive to node probabilities than the classification error rate. Hence, we typically use the last two to grow the tree.

Rule of thumb: use Gini and Cross-Entropy to build and prune a tree and use classification error rate for pruning if prediction accuracy of the pruned tree is the goal

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(tree)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ISLR2)}
\FunctionTok{attach}\NormalTok{(Carseats)}
\NormalTok{High }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{ifelse}\NormalTok{(Sales }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}=} \DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"No"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Yes"}\NormalTok{)) }\CommentTok{\# transform Sales (conitnuous) to binary variable}

\NormalTok{Carseats }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(Carseats, High)}

\NormalTok{tree.carseats }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{tree}\NormalTok{(High }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ . }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ Sales, Carseats) }\CommentTok{\# using all variables but Sales}

\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(tree.carseats) }\CommentTok{\# training error rate of 9\%}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Classification tree:
## tree(formula = High ~ . - Sales, data = Carseats)
## Variables actually used in tree construction:
## [1] "ShelveLoc"   "Price"       "Income"      "CompPrice"   "Population" 
## [6] "Advertising" "Age"         "US"         
## Number of terminal nodes:  27 
## Residual mean deviance:  0.4575 = 170.7 / 373 
## Misclassification error rate: 0.09 = 36 / 400
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# tree.carseats}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Deviance formula:

\[
-2 \sum_m \sum_k n_{mk} \log \hat{p}_{mk}
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(n_{mk}\) = number of observations in the m-th terminal node in the k-th class
\end{itemize}

Smaller deviance is preferable (good fit to the training data).

Residual mean deviance formula

\[
\frac{-2 \sum_m \sum_k n_{mk} \log \hat{p}_{mk}}{n - |T_0|}
\]

where \(|T_0|\) = number of terminal nodes

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{multivariate-adaptive-regression-splines}{%
\chapter{Multivariate Adaptive Regression Splines}\label{multivariate-adaptive-regression-splines}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Also known as MARS
\item
  an adaptive procedure for regression when there are a large number of input variables.
\item
  Can also be viewed as a generalization of stepwise linear regression or a modification of the \protect\hyperlink{classification-and-regression-trees}{CART} procedure that improves its performance in the regression setting
\item
  uses expansions in the piecewise linear \protect\hyperlink{basis-function-representations}{basis function}
\item
  To mimic \protect\hyperlink{classification-and-regression-trees}{CART}, the MARS procedure makes the following changes:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Replace the piece wise linear basis function by step functions (e..g, \(I(x-t>0); I(x-t) \le 0)\)
  \item
    When a model term is in a multiplication by a candidate term, it gets replaced by the interaction, and hence it not available for further interaction
  \item
    With these changes, the MARS forward procedure is similar to the CART tree-growing algorithm (i.e., multiplying a step function by a pair of reflected step functions is equivalent to splitting a node at the step. The second restriction is a node may not be split more than once (leaning to the binary tree structure)). Hence, MARS is more powerful because it allows for additive structures because it's not limited to the binary tree structure
  \end{itemize}
\item
  MARS is not used that much today anymore.
\end{itemize}

Consider basis function of the form (``+'' means ``positive part'' with a not at t)

\[
(x-t)_+
\begin{cases}
x-t & \text{ if } x > t \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

and

\[
(t-x)_+
\begin{cases}
t- x & \text{ if } x < t \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

These are linear splines and the pair is \textbf{reflected pair}.

We form such reflected pairs for each input \(X_j\) with knots at each observed value \(x_{ij}\) of that input variable.

The collection of basis function is

\[
C = \{(X_j - t)_+ , (t-X_j)_+\}_{t \in \{x_{1j}, \dots, x_{nj} \}}, j = 1, \dots, p
\]

there can be as many as \(2np\) basis functions (if all of the input values are distinct), which can be large.

Consider a model fo the form

\[
f(X) = \beta_0 + \sum_{m=1}^M \beta_m h_m (X) 
\]

where each \(h_m(X)\) is a function in \(C\), or a product of two or more such functions.

Similar to forward stepwise linear regression, we try to build a final model using these functions of the inputs (instead of the original inputs)

For a given choice of \(h_m\), we estimate the \(\beta_m\) using linear squares (i.e., minimizing the residual sum of squares). Hence, the choice of \(h_m(X)\) is very important

First, we choose the constant function \(h_0(X) =1\) and all of the functions in \(C\) are candidates.

At each stage, we consider a new basis function pair all products of a function \(h_m\) in the existing model set \(M\) with one of the reflected pairs in \(C\), i.e., the

\[
\hat{\beta}_{M + 1} h_l(X) \times (X_j -t)_+ + \hat{\beta}_{M + 2} h_l(X) \times (t - X_j )_+, h_l \in M
\]

that produces the largest decrease in training error

\(h_l\) here is some basis function that is already in the model

Then, these are added to the model set \(M\)

Example:

First, consider adding to the model a function fo the form \(\beta_1 (X_j - t)_+ + \beta_2(t-X)j)_+: t \in \{ x_{ij} \}\) (multiplication by the constant function produces the function itself)

Assume that for a particular data set the best choice is \(\hat{\beta}_1 (X_2 - x_{72})_+ + \hat{\beta}_2 (x_{72} - X_2)\) we then add this to the set \(M\)

Then, we consider adding a pair of products of the form

\[
h_m (X) \times (X_j - t)_+
\]

\[
h_m(X) \times (t- X_j)_+, t \in \{  x_{ij} \}
\]

where for \(h_m\) we have the choices

\[
h_0(X) = 1, h_1(X) = (X_2 - x_{72})_+
\]

or

\[
h_2(X) = (x_{72} - X_2)_+
\]

For example, the third choice produces functions such as \((X_1 - x_{51})_+ \times (x_{72} - X_2)_+\)

The procedure continues until a pre-specified number of terms is in the model, which will be a large model that overfits the data.

Hence, we use backward deletion (i.e., the term that leads to the smallest increase in residual squared error is deleted at each stage). Thus, we have an estimated ``best'' model \(\hat{f}_\lambda\) of each size (number of terms) \(\lambda\)

We could use cross-validation to choose the best model, but it's computationally intensive. Instead, we use generalized cross-validation, which is defined as

\[
GCV(\lambda) = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \hat{f}_\lambda(x_i))^2}{(1- M(\lambda)/n)^2}
\]

where \(M(\lambda)\) is the effective number of parameters in the model. (i.e., accounting for \(\lambda\) and the number of parameters used in selecting the optimal position fo the knots).

Typically, we use \(M(\lambda) = r + cK\) where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(r\) = number of linearly independent basis functions
\item
  \(K\) = number of knots selected in the forward process
\item
  \(c = 3\) (typically) or 2 if the model is restricted to be additive
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Advantages}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A major advantage of these piecewise linear basis functions is that they are ``local'' (i.e., non-zero in only a small portion of the features space, even when multiplied together), which facilitates model fitting in high dimensions.
\item
  A computational advantage because it takes on the order of \(n\) operations to try very knot in each step of the process (for each of the \(j = 1, \dots, p\) variables)
\item
  If higher order products are in the model, lower order components must also be included in the model. (in reality, we don't have to, but it's a good modeling practice)
\item
  MARS can be extended to classification settings (e..g, setting the response variable to 0 and 1) but it's artificial. There are specific MARS-like algorithms designed for classification
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Disadvantages}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Each input can occur at most one time in a product. Thus, \textbf{higher order powers of an input are not allowed}. If you include it, then it can produce unstable fits, particularly at the boundaries

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    One can also restrict products to be of a certain order (e..g, only second-degree or third-degree)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{bagging}{%
\chapter{Bagging}\label{bagging}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Bootstrapping can help tree methods (e.g., \protect\hyperlink{classification-and-regression-trees}{CART})
\item
  Bagging is a special of random forest with \(m = p\) where \(m\) is the number of input variables (used in a tree), and \(p\) is the number of available variables.
\item
  Since \protect\hyperlink{classification-and-regression-trees}{CART} and \protect\hyperlink{multivariate-adaptive-regression-splines}{MARS} suffer from high variance (because if we split the training data into 2 samples at random and fit both halves, the results can be quite different), we use \textbf{bootstrap aggregation} (i.e., \textbf{bagging}), which is a general-propose procedure to reduce variance
\item
  If the procedure has low variance, it will yield similar results if applied repeatedly to distinct data sets (e.g., as in linear regression when \(n\) is larger relative to \(p\))
\item
  The idea of bagging is that \textbf{averaging reduces variance} (somewhat like measurement error). To reduce variation, we take many training sets, and build a separate prediction model, and average them.
\end{itemize}

To execute bagging, we calculate \(\hat{f}^1(X),\hat{f}^2(X), \dots, \hat{f}^B(X)\) using \(B\) separate training set, and average them

\[
\hat{f}_{bag} (X) = \frac{1}{B} \sum_{b=1}^B \hat{f}^b (x)
\]

However, in reality, we don't have access to multiple training sets, so we use bootstrap samples from our (single) training set (i.e., we generate \(B\) bootstrap sample - \textbf{sampling randomly with replacement}). Hence, \(\hat{f}_{bag}(x)\) is the result of averaging the fits from these \(B\) bootstrap samples.

In the case of regression trees with bagging, we construct \(B\) regression trees and average the resulting predictions, but the trees are grown large with no pruning. Hence, each tree has high variance but low bias and averaging then reduces the variance. In practice, \textbf{100} samples is sufficient to reduce the variance significantly.

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Bagging can be used for classification, where we record the class predicted by each of the \(B\) trees for a given test observation and then choose the category that occurs the most often.
\item
  We can also estimate the error associated with a bagged model \textbf{without having to perform cross-validation}.

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    The observations not used in the bootstrap samples (for each sample) are the out-of-bag (OOB) observations.
  \item
    We predict the response for the i-th observation using each of the trees in which the observation was OOB. Then, we average to get a single prediction, and get OOB MSE (or classification error)
  \item
    OOB error is a valid estimate of the test error for the bagged model because the response for each observation is predicted using only the trees that were fit without using that observation.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Disadvantages

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Although bagging can improve predictive power (relative to a single tree), it reduced interpretation (i.e., when we average over many different trees, the variable splits (or basis function included) can change dramatically, preventing interpretation). Thus, we can't know which variable is important

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    To deal with this problem, we can keep track of (to get measure of variable importance in the tree)

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      the total amount that the residual sum of squares is decreased due to splits over a given predictor averaged over all \(B\) trees (in the case of regression), or
    \item
      add up the total amount that the Gini index is decreased by splits over a given predictor, averaged over all \(B\) trees (in case of classification).
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{boosting}{%
\chapter{Boosting}\label{boosting}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Similar to \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} but more powerful, where we can improve the predictions from decision trees
\item
  It was designed for classification problems (but now we can also apply to regression settings).
\item
  In the machine learning world, this method combines the output of many ``weak'' learners to produce a power ``committee''
\item
  A classic boosting algorithm for classification is ``AdaBoost.M1'' \citep{freund1997b}
\item
  It can be considered as one of the best off-the-shelf classifiers
\item
  Under boosting, trees are \emph{not grown independently}, but rather \textbf{sequentially}
\item
  There are no bootstrap samples with boosting because we build trees on the residuals in a step-by-step manner
\item
  In the literature, ``fitting the data hard'' means we fit a single large decision tree to the data, but we will \textbf{learn slowly} by fitting a decision tree to the residuals (not Y) from the model, which helps to improve the part of the tree that did not perform well in the existing tree structure.
\item
  We add this new decision tree into the fitted function with a shrinkage parameter (\(\lambda\)) to slow the process down to add more trees to areas that don't fit well (also helps prevent overfitting). Hence, each tree after will depend strongly on the trees that have already been grown.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{regression-boosting}{%
\section{Regression Boosting}\label{regression-boosting}}

Algorithm for boosting regression trees

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Set \(\hat{f}(x) = 0\) and \(r_i = y_i \forall i\) (in the training set)
\item
  For \(b = 1, \dots, B\) repeat

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    Fit a tree \(\hat{f}^b\) with \(d\) splits (\(d + 1\) terminal nodes) to the training data \((X,r)\)
  \item
    Update \(\hat{f}\) by adding in a shrunken version of the new tree \(\hat{f}(x) \leftarrow \hat{f}(x) + \lambda \hat{f}^b (x)\)
  \item
    Update the residuals \(r_i \leftarrow r_i - \lambda \hat{f}^b (x_i)\)
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Final boosted model \(\hat{f}(x) = \sum_{b=1}^B \lambda \hat{f}^b (x)\)
\end{enumerate}

Boosting has 3 tuning parameters:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(B\): the number of trees. boosting can overfit if \(B\) is too large, we can use \protect\hyperlink{cross-validation-fitting}{Cross-validation Fitting} to select \(B\)
\item
  \(\lambda\): the shrinkage parameter, which controls the rate at which boosting learns. Typical values are 0.01 or 0.001 (best choice depends on the data at hand). The smaller the \(\lambda\), the larger \(B\) must be to achieve good performance
\item
  \(d\): the number of splits in each tree. \(d = 1\) works well (surprisingly), which we call the tree a ``stump.'' With \(d =1\), the boosted ensemble is fitting an additive model since each term only involves one variable. More generally, \(d\) is the interaction depth and controls the interaction order of the boosted model (since d splits can involve at most d variables).
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{classification-boosting}{%
\section{Classification Boosting}\label{classification-boosting}}

For classification problem, consider a two-class classification code such that \(Y \in \{ -1, 1\}\) and a vector of predictor variables, \(X\).

We have a classifier \(G(X)\) that produces prediction that selects either -1 or 1. the error rate on the training sample is

\[
\bar{err} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n I(y_i \neq G(x_i))
\]

A weak classifier is one whose error rate is only slightly better than random guessing. Now, boosting sequentially applies a weak classification algorithm to produce a sequence of weak classifiers, \(G_m(x), m = 1, \dots, M\) (\(M\) is above \(B\))

The predictions are then combined through a \textbf{weighted majority vote} to produce the final prediction

\[
G(x) = sign (\sum_{m=1}^M \alpha_m G_m (x))
\]

where the weights \(\alpha_m, m = 1, \dots, M\) are computed by the boosting algorithm and give highest influence to the best classifiers

At each boosting step, weights \(w_1, \dots, w_n\) are applied to the training observations \((x_i, y_i), i = 1, \dots, n\).

To start, weights are set equal, \(w_i = \frac{1}{n}\) so there is no preference for the first classification.

For each successive iteration, the observation weights at iteration \(m\) are modified such that those observations that were misclassified by the classifier \(G_{m-1}(x)\) have their weights increased, those that were correctly classified have their weights decreased

As iterations continues, observations that are difficult to classify correctly receive ever-increasing influence. Each successive classifier is forced to concentrate on those training observations that are missed by the previous classifiers in the sequence

Why boosting works so well?

From the perspective of a basis function expansion (\ref{basis-function-representations}:

\[
f(x) = \sum_{m=1}^M \beta_m b(x; \gamma_m)
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\beta_m\) are the expansion coefficients
\item
  \(b(x, \gamma)\) are simple functions of the multivariate inputs \(x\) and parameters \(\gamma\)
\end{itemize}

then, boosting is a way of fitting such an additive expansion where the basis functions are the individual classifiers \(G_m(x) \in \{ -1, 1\}\)

To fit this model, we minimize some loss function averaged over the training data (e..g, squared error loss).

\[
\min_{\{\beta_m , \gamma_m \}^M_1} \sum_{i=1}^n L (y_i, \sum_{m=1}^M  \beta_m b(x_i; \gamma_m))
\]

For most loss functions \(L(y, f(x))\) and basis functions \(b(x; \gamma)\), the numerical optimization algorithms are usually computationally intensive.

In some cases, a solution is to solve the subproblem of fitting just a single basis function

\[
\min_{\{\beta, \gamma\}} \sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \beta b (x_i; \gamma))
\]

Then, we approximate the more general minimization problem by sequentially adding new basis functions to the expansion without adjusting the parameters and coefficients that have already been added. This process is called \textbf{forward stagewise additive modeling} (i.e., at each iteration \(m\), we solve for the optimal basis function \(b(x, \gamma_m)\) and coefficient \(\beta_m\) to add to the current expansion \(f_{m-1}(x)\), which gives \(f_m(x)\) and we repeat the process while previously added terms remain constant)

Example: squared error loss

\[
L(y, f(x)) = (y - f(x))^2
\]

then

\[
\begin{aligned}
L(y_i, f_{m-1}(x_i) + \beta_m b(x_i; \gamma_m) &= (y_i - f_{m-1}(x_i) - \beta_mb(x_i; \gamma_m))^2 \\
&= (r_{im} - \beta_mb(x_i; \gamma_m))^2
\end{aligned}
\]

where \(r_{im} = y_i - f_{m-1}(x)\) is the residual of the current model on the i-th observation.

The term \(\beta_m b(x; \gamma_m)\) that best fits the current residuals is added to the expansion at each step

\hypertarget{adaboost}{%
\section{AdaBoost}\label{adaboost}}

Ideas of AdaBoost

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  It combines ``weak learners'' (e.g., stumps) to make classifications
\item
  Weights of stumps are different
\item
  Each stump is made sequentially based on previous stump's mistakes.
\end{enumerate}

AdaBoost.M1

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Initialize the observation weights \(w_i = \frac{1}{N}, i = 1, \dots, N\)
\item
  For \(m = 1\) to M

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    Fit a classifier \(G_m(x)\) to the training data with weights \(w_i\)
  \item
    Calculate \(err_m = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^{N} w_i I(y_i \neq G_m(x_i))}{\sum_{i=1}^N w_i}\)
  \item
    Calculate \(\alpha_m = \log (\frac{1 - err_m}{err_m})\)
  \item
    Set \(w_i \leftarrow w_i \times \exp(\alpha_m \times I (y_i \neq G_m(x_i))), i = 1, \dots, N\)
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  \(G(x) = sign(\sum_{m=1}^M \alpha_m G_m(x))\)
\end{enumerate}

AdaBoost.M1 is equivalent to forward stagewise modeling using the loss function \citep[10.4]{hastie2001a}

\[
L(y, f(x)) = \exp(-y f(x))
\]

which is the ``exponential loss'' (closely related to the binomial negative log-likelihood (deviance), although they have different properties in finite data (real-world) settings )

This loss function is attractive because it facilitates computation

There are more loss functions. Typically we might choose a loss function because

\begin{itemize}
\item
  it's robust to certain properties (e.g., outliers)
\item
  it facilitates computation
\end{itemize}

When a loss function is differentiable, we can make use of the gradient of the loss function to improve the optimization, which is the basis of gradient boosting (``gradient boosting models (GBM)''; formerly, Multiple Additive Regression Trees (MART)).

\hypertarget{gradient-boosting}{%
\section{Gradient Boosting}\label{gradient-boosting}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The algorithm XGBoost has become one of the standard implementations of gradient boosting, which is present in both R and Python.

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    It sues Newton-Raphson for its optimization (i.e., a second order Taylor's approximation for the loss function to make the connection) instead of gradient descent
  \item
    It improves efficiency for more complicated settings than the original gradient boosting algorithms
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Additional regularization and built-in cross-validation and handling of missing data are standard
\item
  Compare to \protect\hyperlink{adaboost}{AdaBoost}: Gradient Boost makes a single leaf, representing an initial guess for the Weights of all of the samples. Similar to \protect\hyperlink{adaboost}{AdaBoost}, a new tree is made based on previous tree's errors and gradient boost scales the trees (but by the same amount). Unlike \protect\hyperlink{adaboost}{AdaBoost}, each tree can be larger than a stump.
\item
  Scaling the tree by the Learning Rate makes a small step in the right direction
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{gradient-boost-regression}{%
\subsection{Gradient Boost Regression}\label{gradient-boost-regression}}

\textbf{Input}: Data \(\{(x_i, y_i)\}^n_{i=1}\) and a differentiable Loss Function \(L(y_i, F(x))\) (e.g., Regression: squared residual: \(0.5(\text{Observed} - \text{Predicted})^2\) )

Step 1: Initialize model with a constant value: \(F_0(x) = \underset{\gamma}{\operatorname{argmin}} \sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \gamma)\)

Step 2: for \(m = 1 \to M\) (number of trees):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Compute the (pseudo) residual: \(r_{im} = - [ \frac{\partial L( y_i, F(x_i))}{\partial F(x_i)}]_{F(x) = F_{m-1}(x)}\) for \(i = 1, \dots, n\) (where \(\frac{\partial L( y_i, F(x_i))}{\partial F(x_i)}\) is the gradient, ergo the name gradient boost)
\item
  Fit a regression tree to the \(r_{im}\) values and create terminal regions \(R_{jm}\) for \(j = 1, \dots, J_m\)
\item
  For \(j = 1, \dots, J_m\) compute \(\gamma_{jm} = \underset{\gamma}{\operatorname{argmin}} \sum_{x_i \in R_{ij}} L(y_i, F_{m-1}(x_i) + \gamma)\)
\item
  Update \(F_m (x) = F_{m-1}(x) + \nu \sum_{j=1}^{J_m} \gamma_{jm} I(x \in R_{jm})\) where \(\nu\) is the learning rate (0 to 1) (smaller value means slower learning rate)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Output}: \(F_M(x)\)

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(gbm)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ISLR2)}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(tree)}

\FunctionTok{attach}\NormalTok{(Boston)}


\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{train }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sample}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(Boston), }\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(Boston)}\SpecialCharTok{/}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{boston.test }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ Boston[}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{train, }\StringTok{"medv"}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{boost.boston }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}
    \FunctionTok{gbm}\NormalTok{(}
\NormalTok{        medv }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ .,}
        \AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ Boston[train, ],}
        \AttributeTok{distribution =} \StringTok{"gaussian"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# because this is regression setting}
        \CommentTok{\# distribution = "bernoulli", \# use this for classification}
        \AttributeTok{n.trees =} \DecValTok{5000}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# number of trees}
        \AttributeTok{interaction.depth =} \DecValTok{4} \CommentTok{\# limits the depth of each tree }
\NormalTok{    )}

\end{Highlighting}

\hypertarget{gradient-boost-classification}{%
\subsection{Gradient Boost Classification}\label{gradient-boost-classification}}

\textbf{Input}: Data \(\{(x_i, y_i)\}^n_{i=1}\) and a differentiable Loss Function \(L(y_i, F(x))\) (e.g., Classification: negative log likelihood: \(- \sum_{i=1}^N \mathbf{y}_i \times\log (\mathbf{p}) + (1- \mathbf{y}_i) \times \log (1 - \mathbf{p})\) where \(y_i\) is the observed value, equivalently, \(- \text{Observed} \times \log(\text{odds}) + \log(1 + \exp(\log(\text{odds}))\) which is differentiable)

Step 1: Initialize model with a constant value: \(F_0(x) = \underset{\gamma}{\operatorname{argmin}} \sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \gamma)\) where \(\gamma\) is a log(odds) value

Step 2: for \(m = 1 \to M\) (number of trees):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Compute the (pseudo) residual: \(r_{im} = - [ \frac{\partial L( y_i, F(x_i))}{\partial F(x_i)}]_{F(x) = F_{m-1}(x)}\) for \(i = 1, \dots, n\) (where \(\frac{\partial L( y_i, F(x_i))}{\partial F(x_i)}\) is the gradient, ergo the name gradient boost)
\item
  Fit a regression tree to the \(r_{im}\) values and create terminal regions \(R_{jm}\) for \(j = 1, \dots, J_m\)
\item
  For \(j = 1, \dots, J_m\) compute \(\gamma_{jm} = \underset{\gamma}{\operatorname{argmin}} \sum_{x_i \in R_{ij}} L(y_i, F_{m-1}(x_i) + \gamma)\)
\item
  Update \(F_m (x) = F_{m-1}(x) + \nu \sum_{j=1}^{J_m} \gamma_{jm} I(x \in R_{jm})\) where \(\nu\) is the learning rate (0 to 1) (smaller value means slower learning rate)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Output}: \(F_M(x)\)

\[
\frac{\sum \text{Residual}_i}{\sum[\text{Previous Prob}_i \times (1 - \text{Previous Prob}_i)]}
\]

\hypertarget{light-gradient-boosting}{%
\section{Light Gradient Boosting}\label{light-gradient-boosting}}

Reference: \citet{machado2019} and \href{https://papers.nips.cc/paper/2017/hash/6449f44a102fde848669bdd9eb6b76fa-Abstract.html}{here}

Based on new techniques:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Gradient-based One Side Sampling (GOSS)
\item
  Exclusive Feature Bundling (EFB)
\end{enumerate}

Advantages:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Faster and higher efficiency
\item
  Lower Memory
\item
  Better accuracy
\item
  Can be used with parallel, distributed computing, GPU learning
\item
  Can handle big data.
\item
  An improvement on \protect\hyperlink{xgboost}{XGBoost}
\end{itemize}

\href{https://lightgbm.readthedocs.io/en/latest/R/reference/}{Function Reference}

Example by \href{https://lightgbm.readthedocs.io/en/latest/R/index.html}{lightgbm}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\FunctionTok{data}\NormalTok{(agaricus.train, }\AttributeTok{package=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}lightgbm\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{dtrain }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{lgb.Dataset}\NormalTok{(train}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{data, }\AttributeTok{label =}\NormalTok{ train}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{label)}

\end{Shaded}

\NormalTok{data.drop([}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}workclass\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}education\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}marital\_Status\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}occupation\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}relationship\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}race\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sex\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}native\_country\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,inplace}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{) }

 

\CommentTok{\#Merging one hot encoded features with our dataset \textquotesingle{}data\textquotesingle{} }
\NormalTok{data}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{pd.concat([data,one\_hot\_workclass,one\_hot\_education,one\_hot\_marital\_Status,one\_hot\_occupation,one\_hot\_relationship,one\_hot\_race,one\_hot\_sex,one\_hot\_native\_country],axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }

\CommentTok{\#removing duplicate columns }
\NormalTok{\_, i }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.unique(data.columns, return\_index}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{) }
\NormalTok{data}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{data.iloc[:, i] }

\CommentTok{\#Here our target variable is \textquotesingle{}Income\textquotesingle{} with values as 1 or 0.  }
\CommentTok{\#Separating our data into features dataset x and our target dataset y }
\NormalTok{x}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{data.drop(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Income\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }
\NormalTok{y}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{data.Income }

 

\CommentTok{\#Imputing missing values in our target variable }
\NormalTok{y.fillna(y.mode()[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{],inplace}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{) }

\CommentTok{\#Now splitting our dataset into test and train }
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.model\_selection }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ train\_test\_split }
\NormalTok{x\_train,x\_test,y\_train,y\_test}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{train\_test\_split(x,y,test\_size}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{.3}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#The data is stored in a DMatrix object }
\CommentTok{\#label is used to define our outcome variable}
\NormalTok{dtrain}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{xgb.DMatrix(x\_train,label}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{y\_train)}
\NormalTok{dtest}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{xgb.DMatrix(x\_test)}

\CommentTok{\#setting parameters for xgboost}
\NormalTok{parameters}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}max\_depth\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}eta\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}silent\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}objective\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}binary:logistic\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}eval\_metric\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}auc\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}learning\_rate\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\FloatTok{.05}\NormalTok{\}}

\CommentTok{\#training our model }
\NormalTok{num\_round}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{50}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ datetime }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ datetime }
\NormalTok{start }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ datetime.now() }
\NormalTok{xg}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{xgb.train(parameters,dtrain,num\_round) }
\end{Highlighting}

\hypertarget{partial-dependence-plots}{%
\section{Partial Dependence Plots}\label{partial-dependence-plots}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It can help interpret parameter effects from tree-based models (e..g, \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} and \protect\hyperlink{boosting}{Boosting})
\end{itemize}

Consider a subvector \(X_S\) of \(l < p\) of the input predictor space \(X\), indexed by \(S \subset \{ 1, \dots, p \}\)

Let \(C\) be the complement such that \(S \cup C = \{1, \dots, p \}\)

We expect that \(f(X) = f(X_S, X_C)\) and define the partial dependence of \(f(X)\) on \(X_S\) to be

\[
f_S(X_S) = E_{X_C} f(X_S, X_C)
\]

which is the marginal average of \(f\)

It can serve as a useful description of the effect of the chosen subset on \(f(X)\) when elements of \(X_S\) do not have strong interactions with elements of \(X_C\)

We can estimate this expectation by

\[
\bar{f}_S(X_S) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n f(X_S, x_{iC})
\]

where \(\{ x_{1C}, \dots, x_{nC}\}\) are the values of \(X_C\) occurring in the training data

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  This can be computationally intensive to evaluate (but, for decision trees \(\bar{f}_S(X_S)\) can be computed efficiently from the tree itself).
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The interpretation of the partial dependence plots is that they represent the effect of \(X_S\) on \(f(X)\) after accounting for the average effects of the other variables \(X_C\) on \(f(X)\)
\item
  You can't assume that this present the effect of \(X_S\) \(f(X)\) \emph{ignoring} the effects of \(X_C\) (unless \(X_S \perp X_C\))
\end{itemize}

Partial dependence plots for the 2 most important variables, showing marginal effect of the variables on the response after integrating out the other variables (i.e., conditioned on other variables)

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{random-forests}{%
\chapter{Random Forests}\label{random-forests}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Similar to \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} since both are bootstrap-based procedure (based on committee of learning trees)
\item
  Different from \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} because the trees are effectively decorrelated

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    At each split in the tree-building process (for each bootstrap sample), we select the splitting rule from a randomly selected relatively small subset of the original input variables. Hence, different variables are used in the tree building process across the bootstrap sample. Thus, the trees are less dependent on each other (and dominant covariates), reducing the variance of the prediction
  \end{itemize}
\item
  This variance reduction is beneficial because trees are inherently noisy, but if grown fairly deep, have relatively low bias. Hence, averaging many low-biased independently distributed trees together gives relatively unbiased and low variance predictions/classification.
\end{itemize}

The variance of an average of correlated variables is larger than the average of the same number of independent variables (i.e,, the effective degrees of freedom are less for correlated data)

Hence, an average of \(B\) identically distributed random variables, each with variance \(\sigma^2\) that have pairwise correlation \(\rho\) is

\[
\rho \sigma^2 + \sigma^2 \frac{1 - \rho}{B}
\]

As \(B\) increases, there is still limiting variance \(\rho \sigma^2\)

If we reduce \(\rho\), then we could get a smaller variance for the average. Hence, random forests reduces the inherent dependence in \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging}

Intuitive steps:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Create bootstrapped datasets (with replacement)
\item
  Create a decision tree (1) using the bootstrapped dataset (2) using a random subset of variables at each step.
\item
  Repeat step 1 and 2
\item
  To make prediction, we run the data through all the created decision trees, and see the average, or majority vote (just like \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging}- using aggregated bootstrapped data )
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Procedure}

We randomly select a smaller number of input variables, \(m\), where \(m \le p\)

Typically, we use (rules of thumb, no scientific backing - simulation study):

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(m = \sqrt{p}\) for classification
\item
  \(m = \frac{p}{3}\) for regression
\end{itemize}

After \(B\) trees are grown, \(\{T(x, \Theta_b)\}^B_1\), the random forest regression predictor is

\[
\hat{f}^B_{rf} (x) = \frac{1}{B} \sum_{b=1}^B T(x; \Theta_b)
\]

where \(\Theta_b\) is the cut points associated with the b-th tree

\textbf{Algorithm for Random Forest} (both regression and classification) \citep[Alg 15.1]{hastie2001a}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  For \(b = 1\) to \(B\)

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    Draw a bootstrap sample \(\mathbf{Z}^*\) of size N from the training data
  \item
    Grow a random-forest tree \(T_b\) to the bootstrapped data, by recursively repeating the following steps for each terminal node of the tree, until the minimum node size \(n_{min}\) is reached:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumiii{\arabic{enumiii}.}
    \item
      Select \(m\) variables at random from the \(p\) variables
    \item
      Pick the best variable/split-point among the \(m\)
    \item
      Split the node into two daughter nodes
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Output the ensemble of trees \(\{T_b\}^B_1\)
\end{enumerate}

To predict a new point \(x\)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Regression: \(\hat{f}_{rf}^B(x) = \frac{1}{B}\sum_{b=1}^B T_b(x)\)
\item
  Classification: Let \(\hat{C}_b (x)\) be the class prediction of the b-th random-forest tree. Then \(\hat{C}_{rf}^B (x) = \text{majority vote } \{\hat{C}_b (x) \}^B_{1}\)
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  In general, \protect\hyperlink{gradient-boosting}{Gradient Boosting} is comparable \protect\hyperlink{random-forests}{Random Forests} and \protect\hyperlink{random-forests}{Random Forests} is usually better than \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging}. But in practice, this isn't always the case.
\item
  Best method is based on the type of structure in the process (e..g, degree of linearity/non-linearity, dependence in the covariates, relative importance of specific covariates, etc.)
\item
  To get out-of-bag (OOB) samples, for each observation pair \((x_i, y_i)\), its random forest predictor is the average of the trees corresponding to bootstrap samples in which this observation did \textbf{not} appear
\item
  OOB error (i.e., comparison between OOB prediction to the actual response) is effectively a cross-validation error estimate. Once this error stabilizes, then the model is trained.
\end{itemize}

Variable Importance

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Since random forests typically include more variables (due to the random selection), it's more likely to recognize additional ``important'' variables
\item
  Variable importance is determined by a measure of the important in the split-criterion fo reach variable as aggregated across each tree.
\item
  Variable importance can also be determined by the variable's prediction strength (based on OOB prediction). When the b-th tree is grown, the OOB samples are passed down the tree and the prediction accuracy is taken. Then, the value for the j-th variable are randomly permuted in the OOB samples, and the accuracy is computed. The decrease in accuracy is averaged over all trees and gives a measure of importance for variable j.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    This does not measure the effect on prediction if this variable was not available because the refitted model would likely contain surrogate variables to take its place
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Proximity Plots: indicate which observations are effectively close together in terms of the random forest classifier (typically only provide info on where the points lie relative to the decision boundary)
\item
  Overfitting: Random forests can be robust to overfitting if the number of relevant variables in \(X\) is large relative to \(p\)
\item
  Bias: The bias in random forests is the same as the bias of the individual tree, with only improvement in MSE due to variance reduction
\item
  Similar to Nearest Neighbor Classifiers (e.g., k-nearest neighbor)
\item
  Packages: \texttt{randomForest}, \texttt{cforest}, \texttt{ParallelForest}, \texttt{ranger}, \texttt{Rborist}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{random-forests}{Random Forests} are good with general nonlinear nonparametric prediction
\end{itemize}

Example by \href{https://github.com/StatQuest/random_forest_demo/blob/master/random_forest_demo.R}{Josh Starmer}

\FunctionTok{colnames}\NormalTok{(data) }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}
  \StringTok{"age"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"sex"}\NormalTok{,}\CommentTok{\# 0 = female, 1 = male}
  \StringTok{"cp"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# chest pain }
          \CommentTok{\# 1 = typical angina, }
          \CommentTok{\# 2 = atypical angina, }
          \CommentTok{\# 3 = non{-}anginal pain, }
          \CommentTok{\# 4 = asymptomatic}
  \StringTok{"trestbps"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# resting blood pressure (in mm Hg)}
  \StringTok{"chol"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# serum cholestoral in mg/dl}
  \StringTok{"fbs"}\NormalTok{,  }\CommentTok{\# fasting blood sugar if less than 120 mg/dl, 1 = TRUE, 0 = FALSE}
  \StringTok{"restecg"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# resting electrocardiographic results}
          \CommentTok{\# 1 = normal}
          \CommentTok{\# 2 = having ST{-}T wave abnormality}
          \CommentTok{\# 3 = showing probable or definite left ventricular hypertrophy}
  \StringTok{"thalach"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# maximum heart rate achieved}
  \StringTok{"exang"}\NormalTok{,   }\CommentTok{\# exercise induced angina, 1 = yes, 0 = no}
  \StringTok{"oldpeak"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# ST depression induced by exercise relative to rest}
  \StringTok{"slope"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# the slope of the peak exercise ST segment }
          \CommentTok{\# 1 = upsloping }
          \CommentTok{\# 2 = flat }
          \CommentTok{\# 3 = downsloping }
  \StringTok{"ca"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# number of major vessels (0{-}3) colored by fluoroscopy}
  \StringTok{"thal"}\NormalTok{, }\CommentTok{\# this is short of thalium heart scan}
          \CommentTok{\# 3 = normal (no cold spots)}
          \CommentTok{\# 6 = fixed defect (cold spots during rest and exercise)}
          \CommentTok{\# 7 = reversible defect (when cold spots only appear during exercise)}
  \StringTok{"hd"} \CommentTok{\# (the predicted attribute) {-} diagnosis of heart disease }
          \CommentTok{\# 0 if less than or equal to 50\% diameter narrowing}
          \CommentTok{\# 1 if greater than 50\% diameter narrowing}
\NormalTok{  )}

Now we are ready to build a random forest.

NOTE: For most machine learning methods, you need to divide the data manually into a ``training'' set and a ``test'' set. This allows you to train the method using the training data, and then test it on data it was not originally trained on.

In contrast, Random Forests split the data into ``training'' and ``test'' sets for you. This is because Random Forests use bootstrapped data, and thus, not every sample is used to build every tree. The ``training'' dataset is the bootstrapped data and the ``test'' dataset is the remaining samples. The remaining samples are called the ``Out-Of-Bag'' (OOB) data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{42}\NormalTok{)}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# impute any missing values in the training set using proximities}
\NormalTok{data.imputed }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rfImpute}\NormalTok{(hd }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ ., }\AttributeTok{data =}\NormalTok{ data, }\AttributeTok{iter=}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  17.49% 12.80% 23.02%
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  16.83% 14.02% 20.14%
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  17.82% 13.41% 23.02%
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  17.49% 14.02% 21.58%
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  17.16% 12.80% 22.30%
## ntree      OOB      1      2
##   300:  18.15% 14.63% 22.30%
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\DocumentationTok{\#\# }\AlertTok{NOTE}\DocumentationTok{: iter = the number of iterations to run. Breiman says 4 to 6 iterations}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# is usually good enough. With this dataset, when we set iter=6, OOB{-}error}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# bounces around between 17\% and 18\%. When we set iter=20,}
\CommentTok{\# set.seed(42)}
\CommentTok{\# data.imputed \textless{}{-} rfImpute(hd \textasciitilde{} ., data = data, iter=20)}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# we get values a little better and a little worse, so doing more}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# iterations doesn\textquotesingle{}t improve the situation.}
\DocumentationTok{\#\#}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# }\AlertTok{NOTE}\DocumentationTok{: If you really want to micromanage how rfImpute(),}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# you can change the number of trees it makes (the default is 300) and the}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# number of variables that it will consider at each step.}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# Now we are ready to build a random forest.}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# }\AlertTok{NOTE}\DocumentationTok{: If the thing we\textquotesingle{}re trying to predict (in this case it is}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# whether or not someone has heart disease) is a continuous number}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# (i.e. "weight" or "height"), then by default, randomForest() will set}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# "mtry", the number of variables to consider at each step,}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# to the total number of variables divided by 3 (rounded down), or to 1}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# (if the division results in a value less than 1).}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# If the thing we\textquotesingle{}re trying to predict is a "factor" (i.e. either "yes/no"}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# or "ranked"), then randomForest() will set mtry to}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# the square root of the number of variables (rounded down to the next}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# integer value).}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# In this example, "hd", the thing we are trying to predict, is a factor and}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# there are 13 variables. So by default, randomForest() will set}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# mtry = sqrt(13) = 3.6 rounded down = 3}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# Also, by default random forest generates 500 trees (}\AlertTok{NOTE}\DocumentationTok{: rfImpute() only}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# generates 300 tress by default)}
\NormalTok{model }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{randomForest}\NormalTok{(hd }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ ., }\AttributeTok{data=}\NormalTok{data.imputed, }\AttributeTok{proximity=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# RandomForest returns all kinds of things}
\NormalTok{model }\CommentTok{\# gives us an overview of the call, along with...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
## Call:
##  randomForest(formula = hd ~ ., data = data.imputed, proximity = TRUE) 
##                Type of random forest: classification
##                      Number of trees: 500
## No. of variables tried at each split: 3
## 
##         OOB estimate of  error rate: 16.83%
## Confusion matrix:
##           Healthy Unhealthy class.error
## Healthy       142        22   0.1341463
## Unhealthy      29       110   0.2086331
\end{verbatim}

      \CommentTok{\# 1) The OOB error rate for the forest with ntree trees. }
      \CommentTok{\#    In this case ntree=500 by default}

      \CommentTok{\#    The confusion matrix is laid out like this:}

     --------------------------------------------------------------

Healthy \textbar{} Number of healthy people \textbar{} Number of healthy people \textbar{}
\textbar{} correctly called ``healthy'' \textbar{} incorrectly called ``unhealthy'' \textbar{}
\textbar{} by the forest. \textbar{} by the forest \textbar{}
--------------------------------------------------------------
Unhealthy\textbar{} Number of unhealthy people \textbar{} Number of unhealthy people \textbar{}
\textbar{} incorrectly called \textbar{} correctly called ``unhealthy'' \textbar{}
\textbar{} ``healthy'' by the forest \textbar{} by the forest \textbar{}
--------------------------------------------------------------

\DocumentationTok{\#\# Now check to see if the random forest is actually big enough...}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# Up to a point, the more trees in the forest, the better. You can tell when}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# you\textquotesingle{}ve made enough when the OOB no longer improves.}
\NormalTok{oob.error.data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Trees=}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate), }\AttributeTok{times=}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Type=}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"OOB"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Healthy"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Unhealthy"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{each=}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate)),}
  \AttributeTok{Error=}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate[,}\StringTok{"OOB"}\NormalTok{], }
\NormalTok{    model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate[,}\StringTok{"Healthy"}\NormalTok{], }
\NormalTok{    model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate[,}\StringTok{"Unhealthy"}\NormalTok{]))}

\DocumentationTok{\#\# If we want to compare this random forest to others with different values for}
\DocumentationTok{\#\# mtry (to control how many variables are considered at each step)...}

\NormalTok{  oob.values[i] }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ temp.model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate[}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(temp.model}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{err.rate),}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{\}}
\NormalTok{oob.values}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 5
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\DocumentationTok{\#\# create a model for proximities using the best value for mtry}
\NormalTok{model }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{randomForest}\NormalTok{(hd }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ ., }
                      \AttributeTok{data=}\NormalTok{data.imputed,}
                      \AttributeTok{ntree=}\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, }
                      \AttributeTok{proximity=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }
                      \AttributeTok{mtry=}\FunctionTok{which}\NormalTok{(oob.values }\SpecialCharTok{==} \FunctionTok{min}\NormalTok{(oob.values)))}

\hypertarget{bayesian-additive-regression-trees}{%
\chapter{Bayesian Additive Regression Trees}\label{bayesian-additive-regression-trees}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  also known as BART \citep{sparapani2021}
\item
  is related to \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} and \protect\hyperlink{random-forests}{Random Forests}, and \protect\hyperlink{boosting}{Boosting} because

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Each tree is constructed in a random manner (similar to \protect\hyperlink{bagging}{Bagging} and \protect\hyperlink{random-forests}{Random Forests})
  \item
    Each tree tries to capture signal not yet accounted for by the current model (similar to \protect\hyperlink{boosting}{Boosting})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Let \(K\) be the number of regression trees, \(B\) the number of (MCMC) iterations for which the BART algorithm will be run

Let \(\hat{f}_k^b(x)\) represent the prediction at \(x\) for the k-th regression tree used in the b-th iteration.

Then, the \(K\) trees from that iteration will be summed as

\[
\hat{f}^b(x) = \sum_{k=1}^K \hat{f}_k^b(x), b = 1, \dots, B
\]

In the first iteration, all trees are initialized to have a single root node with \(\hat{f}_k^1(x) = (1/nK)\sum_{i=1}^n y_i\), which is the mean of the response values divided by the total number of trees.

Thus,

\[
\hat{f}^1(x) = \sum_{k=1}^K \hat{f}_k^1 (x) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n y_i \text{ (mean response)}
\]

In each later iteration, BART updates each of the \(K\) trees, one at a time

In the b-th iteration, to update the k-th tree, we subtract from each response value the predictions from all but the k-th tree to obtain \textbf{a partial residual}:

\[
r_i = y_i - \sum_{k'<k} \hat{f}_{k'}^b (x_i) - \sum_{k'>k} f_{k'}^{b-1}(x_i), i = 1, \dots, n
\]

Instead of fitting a new tree to this partial residual, BART randomly chooses a perturbation to the tree in the previous iteration (\(\hat{f}_k^{b-1}\)) from a set of possible perturbations (this is where MCMC comes in), favoring ones that improve the fit to the partial residual. There are 2 components to this perturbation:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  change the structure of the tree by adding or pruning branches
\item
  change the prediction in each terminal node of the tree
\end{itemize}

The output of BART is a collection of prediction models:

\[
\hat{f}^b(x) = \sum_{k =1}^K \hat{f}_k^b (x) , b = 1, \dots, B
\]

where we don't include the results from the iterations in the MCMC burn-in period (say \(L\) burn-in samples):

\[
\hat{f}^b(x) = \frac{1}{B - L} \sum_{b = L +1}^B \hat{f}_k^b (x) 
\]

Since this method is under the Bayesian paradigm, we can use the posterior samples to get distributional estimates of the predictions

BART algorithm \citep{james2013} Alg 8.3

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Let \(\hat{f}_1^1(x) = f^1_2(x) = \dots = \hat{f}_K^1(x) = \frac{1}{nK} \sum_{i=1}^n y_i\)
\item
  Compute \(\hat{f}^1 (x) = \sum_{k=1}^K \hat{f}_k^1 (x) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n y_i\)
\item
  For \(b = 2, \dots, B\)

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    For \(k = 1, \dots, K\)

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumiii{\arabic{enumiii}.}
    \item
      For \(i = 1, \dots , n\), compute the current partial residual (\(r_i\)
    \item
      Fit a new tree \(\hat{f}_k^b(x)\) to \(r_i\) by randomly perturbing the k-th tree from the previous iteration, \(\hat{f}_k^{b-1}(x)\) (favor perturbations that improve the fit)
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    Compute \(\hat{f}^b (x) = \sum_{k=1}^K \hat{f}^b_k (x)\)
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Compute the mean after \(L\) burn-in samples \(\hat{f}(x) = \frac{1}{B - L} \sum_{b = L +1}^B \hat{f}^b (x)\)
\end{enumerate}

Notes

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Step 3.1.2 prevents overfitting since it limits how "hard we fit the data in each iteration
\item
  The individual trees are quite small. we limit the tree size to avoid overfitting the data (especially when we grew very large trees)
\item
  Each time we randomly perturb a tree to fit the residuals, we are drawing a new tree form a posterior distribution
\item
  When we use BART, we have to specify

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    the number trees \(K\)
  \item
    the number of interactions \(B\)
  \item
    the number of burn-in interactions \(L\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  We typically choose large values for \(B = 1000\) and \(K =200\), and moderate value for \(L=100\)
\item
  BART is a good out-of-box tool (i.e., minimal tuning)
\end{itemize}

Example by \citep[Ch 8.3]{james2013}
