\hypertarget{part-4.-network-based---deep-learning}{%
\part{4. NETWORK BASED - DEEP LEARNING}\label{part-4.-network-based---deep-learning}}

\hypertarget{neural-networks}{%
\chapter{Neural Networks}\label{neural-networks}}

Readings:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \citep[Ch 10]{james2013} with \href{https://www.statlearning.com/}{(statlearning.com)} and \href{https://www.dataschool.io/15-hours-of-expert-machine-learning-videos/}{online course}
\item
  \citep[Ch 11]{hastie2001a}
\item
  \href{https://www.deeplearningbook.org/}{Deep Learning} \citep{Goodfellow-et-al-2016}
\item
  Deep Learning with R \citep{alma991043601650103276}
\item
  \citet{abdar2021}
\item
  \citet{samek2021a}
\end{itemize}

\includegraphics{images/architecture.jpeg}

\citep[p.~321]{jiao2020}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The basic idea behind neural networks (NN) is to find nonlinear functions of linear combinations of inputs to model a response.
\item
  NN include a large class of models. Even the simple version of these models is a form of ``universal approximator'' (i.e., can represent any function)
\item
  NN is a multi-stage regression or classification model that can be represented by a network diagram

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    In the artificial intelligence community, they want to come up with models that mimic the way neurons and synapses create intelligence in the human brain
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Refresher on Non-linear regression

A model with the form of

\[
Y_i = f(\mathbf{x}_i ; \mathbf{\theta}) + \eta_i, i = 1, \dots, n
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(f(\mathbf{x}_i , \mathbf{\theta})\) is a nonlinear function (known up to parameters, \(\mathbf{\theta}\)) relating to \(E(Y_i)\) to the independent variables \(\mathbf{x}_i\)
\item
  \(\mathbf{x}_i\) is a \(p \times 1\) vector of independent variables (fixed)
\item
  \(\mathbf{\theta}\) is a \(q \times 1\) vector of parameters
\item
  \(\eta_i\)'s are iid variables mean 0 and variance \(\sigma^2\)
\end{itemize}

The objective function that we week to minimize is a loss function

Ex: for continuous data, we can use squared-error loss (e.g., residual sums of squares)

\[
Q(f(\mathbf{x});y; \mathbf{\theta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \{ y_i - f(\mathbf{x}_i; \mathbf{\theta})\}^2
\]

and

\[
\hat{\mathbf{\theta}} = \underset{\mathbf{\theta}}{\operatorname{argmin}} ( Q(f(\mathbf{x}); y; \mathbf{\theta})
\]

Estimation

To minimize numerically based on a Taylor expansion and iterative algorithm of the form

\[
\hat{\mathbf{\theta}}^{(j + 1)} = \hat{\mathbf{\theta}}^{(j)} - \epsilon_j \mathbf{A}_j \frac{\partial Q (\hat{\theta}^{(j)})}{\partial \mathbf{\theta}}
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\mathbf{A}_j\) = some positive definite matrix
\item
  \(\frac{\partial Q (\hat{\theta}^{(j)})}{\partial \mathbf{\theta}}\) (i.e., \(\nabla_\theta Q(\hat{\mathbf{\theta}}^{(j)})\) ) is the gradient of the objective function \(Q(\mathbf{\theta})\) at \(\hat{\theta}\)
\item
  \(\epsilon_j\) = learning rate
\end{itemize}

Estimation via Optimization (this matrix controls the gradient - control all parameters at the same time and stop the model from over shooting, but it is very expensive to calculate; hence, it modern machine learning we usually just control for the learning rate)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Gradient Descent: \(\mathbf{A}_j \equiv \mathbf{I}_p\)
\item
  Gauss-Newton: \(\mathbf{A}_j \equiv [\mathbf{F}( \hat{\theta}^{(j)})' \mathbf{F}( \hat{\theta}^{(j)})]^{-1}\) where \(\mathbf{F} (\theta) \equiv \frac{\partial f(\mathbf{x}_i; \mathbf{\theta})}{\partial \theta_j}\) (\(n \times p\) Jacobian matrix)
\item
  Levenberg-Marquardt: \(\mathbf{A}_j \equiv [\mathbf{F}( \hat{\theta}^{(j)})' \mathbf{F}( \hat{\theta}^{(j)}) + \tau \mathbf{I}]^{-1}\) (Ridge penalty: \(L_2\) regularization)
\item
  Newton-Raphson: \(\mathbf{A}_j \equiv [\frac{\partial^2 Q (\hat{\mathbf{\theta}}^{(j)})}{\partial \theta \partial \mathbf{\theta}'}]^{-1}\) (\(p \times p\) Hessian matrix)
\end{itemize}

In general, optimization algorithms use an objective function (e.g., loss function) that consists of a sum over the training data

In ``big data'' problems (large n) and \textbf{computation of gradient} can can be taxing (i.e., a large number of floating point operations that must be used for gradient calculation). Plus, we can also have problem with \textbf{redundant data} in the training sample.

\hypertarget{stochastic-gradient-descent}{%
\section{Stochastic Gradient Descent}\label{stochastic-gradient-descent}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  It's an optimization technique denoted as SGD
\item
  SGD can fix the "big data' problem by considering minimizing the expected loss, which requires estimates of the expected loss.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    This can be done with averages of small samples (min-batches) of the training data (assuming the input \(\mathbf{x}_i\) come from the same underlying distribution)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Considering the expectation of the gradient by randomly sampling a small number of samples from the training set (without replacement) and taking the average turns out to be a very effective solution to this problem. Thus, the method, stochastic gradient descent is ``stochastic'' because it is based on samples and expectation, but it can also be used in an \emph{online scenario} where data are received \textbf{sequentially}.
\item
  Smaller batch sizes offer more regularization and more flexibility (generalization) outside the training sample
\end{itemize}

Basic Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) Algorithm (Goodfellow et al.~2016, Ch 18)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Require}: Learning rate \(\epsilon\)
\item
  \textbf{Require}: Initial parameters \(\mathbf{\theta}^{(j)}\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{while} Stopping criterion not met \textbf{do}

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      Sample a mini-batch of \(m\) inputs \(\{ \mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_m \}\) and targets \(\{ \mathbf{y}_1, \dots, \mathbf{y}_m \}\)
    \item
      Set \(\mathbf{g} = 0\)
    \item
      \textbf{for} \(i = 1\) to \(m\) \textbf{do}

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        Compute gradient estimate: \(\mathbf{g} \leftarrow \mathbf{g} + \frac{1}{m} \nabla_\theta Q(f(\mathbf{x}_i; \theta), \mathbf{y}_i)\)
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      \textbf{end for}
    \item
      Apply update: \(\mathbf{\theta}^{(j +1)} \leftarrow \mathbf{\theta}^{(j)} - \epsilon \mathbf{g}\)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{end while}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Note: one cycle of several min-batches that correspond to all training observations is called an epoch. Hence, the number of epochs is the number of complete passes through the training data.

Using previous update (direction nd magnitude) to make the new update a convex combination of the previous update and the current gradient, it gives a more stable update, which is known as momentum

Basic Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) with Momentum Algorithm (Goodfellow et al.~2016, Ch 8)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Require}: Learning rate \(\epsilon\), momentum parameter \(\nu\)
\item
  \textbf{Require}: Initial parameters \(\mathbf{\theta}^{(j)}\) and ``velocity'' \(\mathbf{v}\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{while} Stopping criterion not met \textbf{do}

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      Sample a mini-batch of \(m\) inputs \(\{ \mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_m \}\) and targets \(\{ \mathbf{y}_1, \dots, \mathbf{y}_m \}\)
    \item
      Set \(\mathbf{g} = 0\)
    \item
      \textbf{for} \(i = 1\) to \(m\) \textbf{do}

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        Compute gradient estimate: \(\mathbf{g} \leftarrow \mathbf{g} + \frac{1}{m} \nabla_\theta Q(f(\mathbf{x}_i; \theta), \mathbf{y}_i)\)
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      \textbf{end for}
    \item
      Compute velocity update: \(\mathbf{v} \leftarrow \nu \mathbf{v} - \epsilon \mathbf{g}\)
    \item
      Apply update: \(\mathbf{\theta}^{(j +1)} \leftarrow \mathbf{\theta}^{(j)} - \mathbf{v}\)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{end while}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

The learning rate parameter is the most important tuning parameter in SGD.

Ways to adapt the learning rate (e.g., Goodfellow et al.~2016, Chp. 8):

\begin{itemize}
\item
  AdaGrad: adapts the learning rates for each parameter by scaling them inversely proportional to the sum of all historical squared
\item
  RMSProp: modifies AdaGrad by changing the squared gradient accumulation into an exponentially weighted moving average of squared gradients (2nd moments)
\item
  Adam: combination of RMSProp and momentum that updates both first and second-order moments with exponential weighting and performs a bias correction.
\end{itemize}

Challenges and modifications (Gradient Descent Algorithms):

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Convergence (failure to converge: convergence to local minima)
\item
  Starting values
\item
  Regularization if there are many parameters (shrink \(\hat{\theta}\) towards 0)

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \(L_2\) penalty (\protect\hyperlink{ridge-regression}{Ridge regression}): add \(\lambda \sum_{j=1}^q \theta_i^2\) to the objective function, \(Q(\theta)\)
  \item
    \(L_1\) penalty (\protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso regression}): add \(\lambda \sum_{j=1}^q |\theta_i|\) to objective function, \(Q(\theta)\)
  \item
    Others
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Recall a neural network is a mutli-stage nonlinear regression or classification model that can be represented by a network diagram
\item
  Essentially, we get parameter estimates like we would with nonlinear regression (there are just a LOT more to estimate and we call them ``weights''). But in this case, we can make it more efficient
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{simple-single-layer-neural-network}{%
\section{Simple Single Layer Neural Network}\label{simple-single-layer-neural-network}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A simple type of neural network is a ``single hidden layer feed forward network (FNN)'' or ``single layer perceptron.''

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Input \(\mathbf{x}\) (p-dimensional)
  \item
    Output \(\mathbf{y}\) (k-dimensional) (typically, \(k = 1\) in most nonlinear regression and binary classification)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{hidden-layer}{%
\subsection{Hidden Layer}\label{hidden-layer}}

\[
z_j = g( \alpha_{j0} + \sum_{i = 1}^p \alpha_{ji} x_i), j = 1, \dots, J
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(z_j\) is the hidden variable
\item
  \(\alpha_{j0}\) is the ``bias'' (offset) parameter
\item
  \(\{\alpha_{ji}\}\) are the "weight parameters
\item
  \(g(.)\) is the activation function
\end{itemize}

A classic activation function is the \textbf{sigmoid} (logistic) function:

\[
g(a) = \frac{1}{1 + \exp(-a)}
\]

Other options include:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Hyperbolic tangent
\item
  Radial basis function
\item
  Rectified linear unit (universal use nowadays)
\item
  Softmax
\item
  Identity
\end{itemize}

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The hidden layer is just transformations of the input variables - similar to \protect\hyperlink{basis-function-representations}{Basis Function Representations}
\item
  These transformations are crucial since the format is very flexible and we ``learn'' the most useful ones
\end{itemize}

It simplifies notation to assume \(x_0 = 1\) then

\[
z_j = g(\sum_{i=1}^p \alpha_{ji}x_i), j = 1, \dots, J
\]

Typical activation functions include

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Sigmoid (logistic)
\end{itemize}

\[
\sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + \exp(-z)}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Hyberbolic Tangent
\end{itemize}

\[
\tanh (z) = \frac{ \exp(z) - \exp(-z)}{ \exp(z) + \exp(-z)}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Rectified Linear Unit (universal use nowadays because it enforces sparse activations and yields a simple gradient (e.g., it can be computed and stored more efficiently), the offset parameter in \(z\) can shift in the inflection point away from zero)
\end{itemize}

\[
ReLU(z) = \max(0,z)
\]

\hypertarget{output-layer}{%
\subsection{Output Layer}\label{output-layer}}

\[
y_k = h(\beta_{k0} + \sum_{j=1}^J \beta_{kj} z_j) , k = 1, \dots, K
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(y_k\) is the output variable
\item
  \(h(.)\) is another activation function (typically, the identity function for nonlinear regression or a sigmoid or softmax function for classification problems)
\end{itemize}

As with the \protect\hyperlink{hidden-layer}{Hidden Layer}, we can write

\[
y_k = h(\sum_{j=0}^J \beta_{kj} z_j), k = 1, \dots, K
\]

In summary, the single layer feed-forward NN can be written as (combining \protect\hyperlink{hidden-layer}{Hidden Layer} and \protect\hyperlink{output-layer}{Output Layer}):

\[
y_k(\mathbf{x}; \mathbf{\beta}, \mathbf{\alpha}) = h(\sum_{j=0}^J \beta_{kj} g(\sum_{i=0}^p \alpha_{ji} x_i))
\]

Similar to traditional nonlinear regression, to estimate the parameters (``train the network'') we select an objective function \(Q(\alpha, \beta)\) (e.g., squared error, cross-entropy) and then seek a gradient-based approach to obtain parameter estimates of the \(\alpha, \beta\)

Hence, NN is just a complicated nonlinear transformation of the inputs

\hypertarget{discrimination-problem}{%
\subsection{Discrimination Problem}\label{discrimination-problem}}

\hypertarget{two-class-discrimination}{%
\subsubsection{Two Class Discrimination}\label{two-class-discrimination}}

When the output has \((0,1)\), the standard neural net implementation includes one hidden layer and ont output unit with a sigmoidal transformation, \(h(.)\):

\[
y_l = h(\beta_0 + \sum_{j = 1}^J \beta_j z_{jl}) = h(\sum_{j=0}^J \beta_j z_{jl})
\]

The hidden layer is the same, but the objective function can be changed to \textbf{cross-entropy loss} (the negative binomial log-likelihood), where \(\tilde{y}\) is the observed response:

\[
Q(\mathbf{\alpha}, \mathbf{\beta}, \{ \mathbf{x}_l , \tilde{y}_l \}) = - \sum_l \tilde{y}_l + ( 1 - \tilde{y}_l) \log(1 - y_l)
\]

\hypertarget{multiclass-discrimination}{%
\subsubsection{Multiclass Discrimination}\label{multiclass-discrimination}}

When \(K > 2\) category classification, one has \(K\) output functions:

\[
o_{kl} = \sum_{j = 0}^{J} \beta_{kj} z_{jl}, k = 1, \dots, K
\]

softmax function is used to transform these to the classification output (the same transformation in multinomial logistic regression);

\[
y_{kl} = h(o_{kl}) = \frac{ \exp(o_{kl})}{\sum_{k'} \exp( o_{k'l})}
\]

The objective function is given by cross-entropy (negative multinomial log-likelihood):

\[
Q(.) = - \sum_l \sum_k \tilde{y}_{kl} \log(y_{kl})
\]

Example by \citep[ Ch. 10]{james2013}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ISLR2)}
\NormalTok{Gitters }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{na.omit}\NormalTok{(Hitters)}
\NormalTok{n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(Gitters)}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ntest }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{trunc}\NormalTok{(n}\SpecialCharTok{/}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{testid }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sample}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\NormalTok{n, ntest)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Get prediction error of the linear model

\begin{Shaded}

\hypertarget{deep-neural-networks}{%
\section{Deep Neural Networks}\label{deep-neural-networks}}

\hypertarget{backpropagation}{%
\subsection{Backpropagation}\label{backpropagation}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  used in the \protect\hyperlink{stochastic-gradient-descent}{Stochastic Gradient Descent} to train the parameters
\item
  It applies the chain rule to calculate the gradient (thank to the hierarchical/compositional nature of the model)
\end{itemize}

\[
\frac{\partial Q}{\partial \alpha_{ji}} = \frac{\partial Q}{\partial y_k} \frac{\partial y_k}{\partial z_j} \frac{\partial z_j}{\partial \alpha_{ji}} , j = 1, \dots, J; i = 0, \dots, p
\]

\[
\frac{\partial Q}{\partial \beta_{kj}} = \frac{\partial Q}{\partial y_k} \frac{\partial y_k}{\partial \beta_{kj}} , k = 1, \dots, K; j = 0, \dots, J
\]

Example:

Nonlinear regression with a single output

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(h(.)\) the identity function
\item
  \(g(.)\) the sigmoid function
\item
  squared error loss
\end{itemize}

Let \(\{ \tilde{y}_{(l)}, \mathbf{x}_{(l)} \}\) correspond to the observed input/output pair for the \(l\)-th sample (in the minbatch)

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:1}
y_{(l)} = \sum_{j=1}^J \beta_j z_{j(l)}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:2}
z_{j(l)} = g(\sum_{i=0}^p \alpha_{ji} x_{i(l)})
\end{equation}

Squared-error objective function

\[
Q(\alpha, \beta, \{ \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{\tilde{y}}) \}) = \frac{1}{2} \sum_l (\tilde{y}_{(l)} - y_{(l)})^2
\]

Then, the gradients are

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:3}
\frac{\partial Q}{\partial \beta_{kj}} = \frac{\partial Q}{\partial y_k} \frac{\partial y_k}{\partial \beta_{kj}} = - \sum_l (\tilde{y}_{(l)} - y_{(l)}) z_{j(l)}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:4}
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\partial Q}{\partial \alpha} &= \sum_l \frac{\partial Q}{\partial y_k} \frac{\partial y_k}{\partial z_j} \frac{\partial z_j}{\partial \alpha_{ji}} \\
&= \sum_l \{- (\tilde{y}_{(l)} - y_{(l)}) \} \{\beta_j\} \{ z_{j(l)} (1 - z_{j(l)}) x_{i(l)} \}
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}

The \((\tilde{y}_{(l)} - y_{(l)})\) term in the equations \eqref{eq:3} and \eqref{eq:4} are residuals.

The other terms indicate what fraction of the residual gets associated with the parameters in the hidden and output layers

Note: \(\partial g(ab) / \partial a = g(a) (1 - g(a))b\) when \(g(.)\) is a sigmoid function

Then, basic SGD algorithm updates parameters

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:5}
\beta_{j}^{(r+1)} = \beta_j^{(r)} - \epsilon^{(r)} \frac{1}{L} \sum_l \frac{\partial Q}{\partial \beta_j}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:6}
\alpha_{ji}^{(r+1)} = \alpha_{ji}^{(r)} - \epsilon^{(r)} \frac{1}{L} \sum_l \frac{\partial Q}{\partial \alpha_{ji}}
\end{equation}

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\epsilon^{(r)}\) is the learning rate parameter
\item
  \(L\) is the number of samples in the summation
\end{itemize}

Updates are done in a two-pass algorithm (forward and backward - hence the name backpropagation)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  In the forward pass, the current parameter values are fixed at the r-th values and \(z_{j(l)}\) and \(y_{(l)}\) are calculated using \eqref{eq:1} and \eqref{eq:2}
\item
  In the backward pass, the gradients are then calcualted from \eqref{eq:3} and \eqref{eq:4} using the r-th updates; then, the parameters are updated using \eqref{eq:5} and \eqref{eq:6}
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Under backpropagation algorithms, each hidden unit passes and receives info only to and from units that share a connection (i.e., ``local'' algorithm)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    which facilitates computation in a parallel computing environment, which is beneficial to process large datasets (this is why deep learning took off).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  We can also add multiple outputs, \(y_{k(l)}, k = 1, \dots, K\)
\item
  Surprisingly, the chain rule gives the same general form for the backpropagation gradient terms for cross-entropy loss (both 2-class and multi-class cases) as for squared error loss.
\item
  In real real, we never calculate derivatives by-hand because now we have automatic differentiation that can symbolically/algorithmically get the derivatives
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{deep-feedforward-networks}{%
\subsection{Deep Feedforward Networks}\label{deep-feedforward-networks}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  also known as Multilayer Perceptrons
\item
  Problems like acoustic processing, image processing, and natural language processing, have complex structure that requires the use of ``deep learning'' algorithms
\item
  These are neural networks with \textbf{many hidden layers}, with outputs from one layer becoming the input of the next.
\item
  The number of units in each layer is called \textbf{width}
\item
  The number of layers is the \textbf{depth} of the network
\item
  The usual challenge with deep models is that they can be severely over-parameterized, unless we use regularization.
\end{itemize}

Example of 2-Layer

Model characteristics:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Two hidden layers
\item
  One output layer for nonlinear regression
\end{itemize}

\[
z_{1j(l)} = g(\sum_{i=0}^p \alpha_{1ji} x_{i(l)}), j = 1, \dots, J_1
\]

\[
z_{2j'(l)} = g(\sum_{j=1}^{J_1} \alpha_{2j'j}z_{1j(l)}), j' = 1, \dots, J_2
\]

\[
y_{(l)} = \sum_{j'=0}^{J_2} \beta_{j'} z_{2j'(l)}
\]

Similar to \protect\hyperlink{backpropagation}{Backpropagation}, as the depth increases the more number of parameters you have to estimate (this is the problem)

It helps to have multiple hidden layers in a deep learning model because the chain of transformations allow the network to learn complex transformation of the input

For example, when applied to images (e..g, in a convolutional neural network), different scales in images are identified at different levels).

\hypertarget{implementation}{%
\subsection{Implementation}\label{implementation}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Preprocessing of the Data: Two choices:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Feature normalization per sample: it can help to center each input \(\mathbf{x}_m\) (demean)
  \item
    Global feature standardization (more commonly used): subtract the average form all the elements of each feature element across the entire training set, and divides by the associated standard deviation (we also have to apply this to the test set, but using the means and standard deviation from the training set).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Model Initialization

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Because neural models are highly nonlinear, they can be very sensitive to the initial parameter values. But we can follow the following general heuristic ``principles'':

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      Choose parameters such that each hidden variable is operating tin the linear range of its activation functions. Because this depends on weights and the inputs, that's why we standardize the inputs
    \item
      Choose parameters randomly (since hidden units are interchangeable, we need them to find different features, so it's important that they don't have the same weights)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Exemptions: \textbf{pretraining}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Regularization

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Because neural networks tend to overfit, we have to use regularization
  \item
    \textbf{Weight Decay} (commonly used): which is \(L_2\) (\protect\hyperlink{ridge-regression}{ridge}) penalty applied to the objective function of the form (e.g., \(\lambda(\sum_{ji} \alpha_{ji}^2 + \sum_{kj} \beta_{kj}^2)\) where \(\lambda \ge 0\) is the tuning parameter. It will add terms \(2 \lambda \beta_{kj}\) and \(2 \lambda \alpha_{ji}\) to the gradient expressions
  \item
    \textbf{Weight Elimination}: is the same thing as \(L_1\) (\protect\hyperlink{lasso-regression}{Lasso}) penalties, in which small parameters are set to 0.
  \item
    \textbf{Dropout}: randomly omit a certain (relatively small) percentage (say, \(\gamma\)) of the hidden units in each hidden layer for each minbatch of samples during the backpropagation training. This can help break up the dependence in the hidden units, and prevents overfitting (improving generalization). To implement, set the activation to 0 (no information propagates through that unit). Then, we re-weight those units that remain in the model to account for those that were removed. Dropout can estimate uncertainty for model predictions).
  \item
    \textbf{Early Stopping} (simplest): rarely does the best generalization of a deep model occur at the local minimum obtained by the gradient descent algorithm. People come up with early stopping, which means stopping the gradient descent algorithm before it has ``converged.'' The choice of the iteration to stop is a hyperparameter that must be chosen via a validations et or by trail and error. It can be used with other forms of regularization
  \item
    \textbf{Weight Sharing}: In some deep model, it makes sense that weights are similar (e..g, imagine weights connected to components of an image or a group of words). Then, we can force these weights to be identical, or add a penalty term that favors them being similar (benefit is that we can estimate less parameters).
  \item
    \textbf{Data Augmentation}: since deep models requires a lot of data to train, when there are too few samples, it's hard for the model to generalize to new data. For models like CNNs, we can use data augmentation to generate more training data (from existing training samples by transforming inputs to produce new input samples that are similar, but different to the training data). We can't do it in inference problem, but since we are doing prediction problem, this method is fine.
  \item
    \textbf{Pretraining}: we use a pretrained network as starting values in backpropagation algorithm (or in cases that you have a huge sample has been used to train a network, just use the pretrained model directly). We typically use this method in image classification because many architectures have been trained on ImageNet already (about 1k categories, and 1.4 mil images). In the early days of deep models, other form of unsupervised generative pre training were used (e..g, layered restricted Boltzmann machines, autoencoders).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Batch Size Selection:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Minibatches in stochastic gradient descent is advantageous computationally. In deep learning training samples are divided by the \textbf{batch size} and then we cycle through all training data. Each iteration over the training data is called an \textbf{epoch}. At the beginning of the optimization, it's preferable to have large gradient variance so the solution can jump out of local minima, which suggests that the minibatch sample should be smaller initially. Then, we adjust it to have larger minibatches in alter stages of the optimization (when we don't want to ump around as much). Thus, we can vary minibatch size through the iterations.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Metrics: to monitor the progress of the training (i.e., at the end of each epoch, we evaluate model performance). Examples

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    An estimate of the loss from the training and validation data
  \item
    Classification accuracy (error rate)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Momentum: we usually include a ``momentum'' term in the \protect\hyperlink{stochastic-gradient-descent}{Stochastic Gradient Descent} algorithm to take into account the previous gradients (i.e., we take a convex combination of the previous gradient and the current gradient estimates). The momentum value is typically chosen between \(0.9 \to 0.99\)
\item
  Learning Rate: the actual learning rate is a function of both the ``learning rate parameter'' (\(\epsilon\)) and the minibatch size \(M_b\). Hence, we adjust the ratio \(\epsilon^* = \epsilon / M_b\) to find a good learning rate. In cases that you vary minibatch size, the learning rate is changed as well.
\item
  Network Architecture: The number of layers and the number of hidden units at each layer are parameters as well. We need enough hidden units to capture features (these are really just feature extraction models), and we don't want ``too many'' (i.e., overfitting). Hence, this is where the ``art'' comes into deep models. In general, wide/shallow models are easier to overfit and narrow/deep models are easier to underfit. It's recommended that the lower levels have more hidden units.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{convolutional-neural-networks}{%
\section{Convolutional Neural Networks}\label{convolutional-neural-networks}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A success story of multi-level neural networks
\item
  The patterns they learn are translation invariant and they also learn spatial hierarchies of patterns (i.e., whereas densely connected network learn global patterns in their input space, convolution layers learn local patterns.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{convolution}{%
\subsection{Convolution}\label{convolution}}

\[
(f \times g) (t) = \int_{R^d} f(\tau) g(t - \tau) d \tau = \int_{R^d} f(t - \tau) g(\tau) d\tau
\]

Discrete convolution (e.g., \(d = 2\))

\[
f[x, y ] \times g [x, y] = \sum_{i = - \infty}^\infty \sum_{j = - \infty}^\infty f[i,j] g[x-i, y - j]
\]

where \(f[]\) is a ``kernel'' weight function that is applied to elements of the spatial image \(g[]\) (similar to \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing})

The difference is that \protect\hyperlink{kernel-smoothing}{Kernel Smoothing} integrates to 1 (or sum to 1 with discrete data), while \protect\hyperlink{convolution}{Convolution} we don't have to satisfy this constraint. Hence, sometimes people can refer to \(f[]\) as ``kernels'' or ``filters''

\textbf{Notes}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Due to the edge effect (less data on edges), the convolved output is smaller. Hence, we can mitigate it by \textbf{padding} the original input space with 0 around edges (so that the convolution can be centered on every pixel value, thus giving a convolved feature map that is the same size as the original image).
\item
  Ideally, we learn these kernels (filters) instead of specifying them (even though we have some standard specifications). Each of these filters convolved with the input space produces a new \textbf{feature map}. Typically, they are \(3 \times 3\) or \(5 \times 5\)
\item
  CNN reduces the parameter space by assuming there is \textbf{only one set of weights for each convolution}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Since weights are shared, we can benefit from dimension reduction in the parameter space
  \item
    After learning the weights, we will use ``pooling layer'' (or ``subsamplng'' or ``down sampling'').
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Tensors}: In practice, input (images) are often tensors. Hence, there is \textbf{a multivariate value} at each spatial location (pixel).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Example: in color images in RGB format, there are three values that define the pixel color. Then, CNNs consider a tensor convolution where the kernel averaging is over \textbf{location and the variables} (or, ``channels'' such as red, green, blue color value). Each layer of a CNN has ``height, width, and depth'' (i.e., 3-d). For black and white images, each pixel has one value, so the ``depth'' is 1.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Hidden layers in the CNN will always have depth \textgreater1 (in practice, this depth is way larger). The filter operates on a relatively small part of the image (in x and y), but across the entire depth; so the weights (in 3-d) form a tensor.
\item
  \textbf{Stride}: When we skip pixels in convolution, it's called strides (less weights to understand and computation)
\item
  Bach normalization: Since there are multiple filters for each layer, it is beneficial to normalize the convolved pixels across these multiple filters (for a given layer), or a subset of those filters, which should be applied immediately after the ReLU step. This can also help with gradient propagation and allow for deeper networks in general
\item
  Data Augmentation: augmenting the samples by a number of random transformations that yield believable images
\item
  Pretrained networks: using a saved network that was previously trained on a large dataset (e.g., ImageNet): the spatial hierarchy of features learned by the pretrained network can effectively act as a generic model of the visual world. To implement, you can either use it as

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    feature extraction: use the convolution base layer (which is ``frozen'') from the pre-trained model, and only train the flattened fully connected last layer(s)
  \item
    fine-tuning: unfreezing the few top layers of the pre-trained model and jointly training both the newly added part of the model (the last fully connected layer) and the top layers. However, you can only fine-tune the top layers after the top level is already trained.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Convolution calculation

\begin{itemize}
\item
  very expensive
\item
  Traditional approach used the fast Fourier transform (FFT)
\end{itemize}

Let \(F(f), F(g)\) be the Fourier transforms of f and g. Then

\[
F(f \times g) = F(f)\cdot F(g) \\
f \times g = F^{-1}(F(g) \cdot F(g))
\]

Thus, we do element-wise multiplication in Fourier space, which is way faster.

\hypertarget{pooling}{%
\subsection{Pooling}\label{pooling}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The pooling layer considers a small rectangular block from the convolutional step and subsamples it to produce a single output.
\item
  Ways to do pooling

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Block maximum (``max pooling'') (favored): usually done with \(2 \times 2\) windows and a stride of 2
  \item
    BLock average (mean)
  \item
    BLock weighted average
  \item
    Downsampling (systemically subsampling one element per block)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Pooling helps \textbf{make the network invariant to translations of the input}, especially when structure is more important than location
\item
  \textbf{Rectification}: Convolved images typically go through a ReLU rectification (\(ReLU(x) = max(0,x)\)) before pooling
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{structure-and-training}{%
\subsection{Structure and training}\label{structure-and-training}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Convolution step and the activations for the flattened layers requires training , while activation and pooling layers are typically specified (don't learn)
\item
  The parameters for the convolutions for any given filter are the same for the entire feature map, which allows the presence of a shape in any part of the feature map to be processed the same way, regardless of its special location (i.e., translation invariant)
\item
  Different from the convolution operator, the pooling operator applies to each feature map separately (i.e., don't pool across the depth dimension).
\item
  Each feature in the final spatial layer is connected to each hidden state in the first fully connected layer, which works like the layers in the deep FNN. There can be multiple fully connected layers (but remember it can be expensive). Most of the parameters int the CNN correspond to these layers.
\item
  In practice, the backpropagation algorithm can be implemented using matrix and vector multiplications.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{interpretation}{%
\subsection{Interpretation}\label{interpretation}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  To visualize what CNNs learn (e.g., explainable AI), we can visualize

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    intermediate outputs (activations)
  \item
    the intermediate filters
  \item
    ``heatmaps'' of class activation on an image (related to ``layer-wise relevance propagation'', which operates by propagating the prediction backward in the neural network, using a set of purposely designed propagation rules),
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{recurrent-neural-networks}{%
\section{Recurrent Neural Networks}\label{recurrent-neural-networks}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Traditional neural networks (\protect\hyperlink{convolutional-neural-networks}{CNNs}) don't account for the sequential dependence of inputs and outputs, such as

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    document and time series classification
  \item
    time series comparisons
  \item
    sequence-to-sequence learning (translation)
  \item
    sentiment analysis in text
  \item
    time series forecasting
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Statisticians considered sequential dependence long before in time-series and spatio-temporal modeling, but these model can still be limited when it comes to complex nonlinear or non-Markovian dependence
\item
  RNNs were developed to handle feedback models for sequence data. Now, they become a successful deep learning method, particularly for language processing applications.
\item
  RNNs are closely related to multivariate state-space models for dynamical systems (e.g., time series, econometric, or spatio-temporal statistics).
\end{itemize}

Consider a classical dynamical system

\[
\mathbf{s}_t = f(\mathbf{s}_{t-1} ; \mathbf{\theta})
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\mathbf{s}_t\) represents the state of the system at time \(t\)
\item
  This is called ``recurrent'' because the state at the time t is a function of the state at time \(t-1\)
\end{itemize}

In ``unfolded'' form

\[
\begin{aligned}
\mathbf{s}_t &= f(\mathbf{s}_{t-1}; \mathbf{\theta}) \\
&= f(f(\mathbf{s}_{t-2}; \mathbf{\theta}); \mathbf{\theta}) \\
&= f(f(f(\mathbf{s}_{t-3}; \mathbf{\theta}); \mathbf{\theta}); \mathbf{\theta}) \\
&= \dots
\end{aligned}
\]

Where parameters \(\mathbf{\theta}\) are shared across all states

Similar to traditional state space model, the states are considered ``hidden'' (denoted as \(\mathbf{h}_t\)) and add external inputs \(\mathbf{x}_t\)

\[
\mathbf{h}_t = f(\mathbf{h}_{t-1}, \mathbf{x}_t; \mathbf{\theta})
\]

Hence, sequences are processed by iterating through the sequence elements and maintaining a state (containing info relative to what it has seen so far via an internal feedback loop)

\includegraphics{images/unfold.png}

(picture by \citet{Goodfellow-et-al-2016} Ch. 10)

Incorporating RNN with output

\includegraphics{images/unfold2.png}

(picture by \citet{Goodfellow-et-al-2016} Ch. 10)

A basic RNN setup is

\[
\mathbf{y}_t = g_o (\mathbf{o}_t) \\
\mathbf{o}_t = \mathbf{W}_{hy} \mathbf{h}_t \\
\mathbf{h}_t = g_h (\mathbf{W}_{hh} \mathbf{h}_{t-1} + \mathbf{W}_{xh} \mathbf{x}_t)
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(g_o(.), g_h(.)\) are activation functions
\item
  \(\mathbf{W}_{hy}, \mathbf{W}_{hh}, \mathbf{W}_{xh}\) are weight matrices (typically contain bias terms as well).
\end{itemize}

To estimate these parameters, we still define loss function and optimize by backpropagation. However, with common parameters across time, they introduce some complications. To solve this,

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Traditionally, the \textbf{backpropagation through time} (BPTT) algorithm via stochastic gradient descent can handle. But it introduces the problem of \textbf{vanishing gradient/exploding gradient}: when the gradients get propagated over many times they tend to either (1) vanish (most of the time) or (2) explode

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Even though there are ways to mitigate exploding gradient (to ensure stability), the series of dependencies through time can still lead to exponentially smaller weights
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Or we add ``gates'' that selectively allow info from the past to influence the present. These gates serve to save info for later, breaking the gradient
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{long-short-term-memory-lstm-rnns}{%
\subsection{Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) RNNs}\label{long-short-term-memory-lstm-rnns}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  This is the most common gated RNN.
\item
  It creates time paths that have gradients that don't vanish or explode
\end{itemize}

Setup

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Name\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
Formula\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Output\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{y}_t = g_o(\mathbf{Vh}_t)\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Hidden State\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{h}_t = \tanh (\mathbf{c}_t) \odot \mathbf{o}\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Internal Memory\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{c}_{t-1} \odot \mathbf{f} + \mathbf{g} \odot \mathbf{i}\)

(first part = forgetting, second part =new info)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Candidate Hidden State\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{g} = \tanh (\mathbf{U}^g \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^g \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Output Gate\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{o} = g(\mathbf{U}^o \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^o \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Forget Gate\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{f} = g(\mathbf{U}^f \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^f \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.23}}\raggedright
Input Gate\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.77}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{i} = g(\mathbf{U}^i \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^i \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

where \(g(.)\) is typically a sigmoid function and \(\bigodot\) is Hadamard product (component-wise multiplication for matrices).

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The input gate selects hidden units that get input to time \(t\)
\item
  The forget gate selects the hidden states at the previous time to reset to 0 at time \(t\)
\item
  The output gate selects the states that will be related to the response
\item
  The memory units indicate when to remember or forget previous hidden states, which will mitigate the vanishing/exploding gradient problem. And its assumption is also plausible because many processes that events in the distant past can influence presence irrespective of the intervening states.
\end{itemize}

Application:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Classification /Segmentation
\item
  Object Detection/Localization
\item
  Similarity learning
\item
  Generative models
\item
  Video analysis
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{gated-recurrent-units}{%
\subsection{Gated Recurrent Units}\label{gated-recurrent-units}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Name\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
Formula\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Output\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{y}_t = g_o (\mathbf{Vh}_t)\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Hidden state\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{h}_t = (1- \mathbf{z}) \circ\mathbf{s}+\mathbf{z} \odot \mathbf{h}_{t-1}\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Candidate Hidden State\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{s} = \tanh (\mathbf{U}^s \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^s (\mathbf{r} \odot \mathbf{h}_{t-1}))\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Reset Gate\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{r} = g(\mathbf{U}^r \mathbf{x}_t+ \mathbf{W}^r \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}\raggedright
Update Gate\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 1\tabcolsep) * \real{0.81}}\raggedright
\(\mathbf{z} = g(\mathbf{U}^z \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}^z \mathbf{h}_{t-1})\)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

If \(\mathbf{r} = 1, \mathbf{z} = 0\), then it is the vanilla RNN

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{long-short-term-memory-lstm-rnns}{Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) RNNs} and \protect\hyperlink{gated-recurrent-units}{Gated Recurrent Units} are computationally intensive to fit, and require parallelized implementation
\item
  Similar to the standard \protect\hyperlink{deep-neural-networks}{Deep Neural Networks} and \protect\hyperlink{convolutional-neural-networks}{Convolutional Neural Networks} require a lot of data
\item
  RNNs are more ``black boxes'' given their complexity, which has the benefit of making them modular and connectable, hence much deeper
\item
  \textbf{Recurrent Dropout}: helps with overfitting. But it should not be applied before a recurrent layer as it measures information in the flow. Instead of allowing the dropout pattern to vary randomly with each time step, we use \emph{the same pattern of dropout for every time step within the recurrent layer} (known as \textbf{recurrent dropout}). We can still use regular dropout for this input layers
\item
  \textbf{Stacking Recurrent Layers}: Similar to \protect\hyperlink{deep-neural-networks}{Deep Neural Networks} and \protect\hyperlink{convolutional-neural-networks}{Convolutional Neural Networks}, we can also improve learning and build more powerful RNNs by stacking the recurrent layers, but it can increase dramatically the cost of implementation.
\item
  \textbf{Recurrent Attention}: \protect\hyperlink{recurrent-neural-networks}{Recurrent Neural Networks} can't localize in certain regions of the sequence. Generally, a \textbf{neural attention mechanism} equips a neural network with the ability to focus on a subset of its inputs (\href{http://akosiorek.github.io/ml/2017/10/14/visual-attention.html}{more detail}). \href{https://arxiv.org/abs/1810.12754}{Recurrent Attention Unit} was described, which integrates an attention mechanisms into the interior of RNN by adding an attention gate. The attention gate enhances RNN's ability to remember long-term memory and help memory cells quickly discard unimportant content \citet{zhong2020}
\item
  We can also use one-dimensional (1D) \protect\hyperlink{convolutional-neural-networks}{CNNs} for sequence data. This method can be cheaper to implement, but not as good when \textbf{global order matters} (e.g., time series where the recent past has more info). However, it's not necessarily true for text data, 1D CNNs can outperform RNNs. People also combine 1D CNN layers and RNN layers (usually the CNN layer is first to reduce dimensionality) \citep{alma991043601650103276}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{artificial-intelligence}{%
\chapter{Artificial Intelligence}\label{artificial-intelligence}}

\hypertarget{explainable-ai}{%
\section{Explainable AI}\label{explainable-ai}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  denoted as XAI
\item
  Historical criticisms for deep learning

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    lack of uncertainty quantification
  \item
    inability to do inference (i.e., unknown important inputs)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  XAI tries to solve these problems (and fairness and transparency)
\item
  Review papers

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \citet{samek2021a}
  \item
    \citet{abdar2021}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Uncertainty Quantification}

Four general topics:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Variational (Bayesian) inference
\item
  Monte Carlo Dropout: justifies dropout as a UQ procedure from a perspective of deep \protect\hyperlink{gaussian-process-regression}{Gaussian Processes}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{mixture-density-networks}{Mixture Density Networks}: this model could predict a mixture density for the output of a NN (instead of the mean response) (Pattern Recognition Bishop 2006's book)
\item
  Ensemble techniques: UQ by generating multiple realization (ensembles) of a deep model
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Explainability}

There are 4 primary approaches \citep{samek2021a}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Interpretable Local Surrogates (e.g., LIME)
\item
  Occlusion Analysis (e.g., Shapley values, SHAP, Kernel SHAP, meaningful perturbation)
\item
  Integrated Gradients (e.g., SmoothGrad)
\item
  Layerwise Relevance Propagation
\end{itemize}

Additionally,

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Self-explainable model (e.g., attention mechanisms)
\item
  Specialized models (e.g., graph neural networks)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Fail}: People can modify the ML such that we can have the same predictions, but it matches up better with stories that they like to tell. (so be careful!!!).

\hypertarget{mixture-density-networks}{%
\subsection{Mixture Density Networks}\label{mixture-density-networks}}

It's theoretically possible/plausible to model any distributions as a mixture of Gaussians

We can model any distribution as a mixture of Gaussian

\[
p(y|\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{k=1}^K \alpha_k (\mathbf{x}) \phi(\mu_k(\mathbf{x}), \sigma_k (\mathbf{x}))
\]

where \(\alpha_k(\mathbf{x})\) are mixture coefficients associated with the Gaussian distribution defined by mean and standard deviation \(\phi(\mu_k(\mathbf{x}), \sigma_k (\mathbf{x})\) and all of these parameters depend on the inputs \(\mathbf{x}\)

Instead of training the neural network to estimate the conditional mean, we estimate all the mixture parameters \(\{ \alpha_k(\mathbf{x}), \mu_k (\mathbf{x}), \sigma_k (\mathbf{x})\}\) (i.e., these parameters are the output of the NN)

This method can use mixtures of other distributions as well

\hypertarget{local-interpretable-model-agnostic-explanations}{%
\subsection{Local Interpretable Model-Agnostic Explanations}\label{local-interpretable-model-agnostic-explanations}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  denoted as LIME \citep{ribeiro2016}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Local}: explain individual predictions (local surrogate models - a model within model)
  \item
    \textbf{Interpretable}: Understandable
  \item
    \textbf{Model}-\textbf{agnostic}: explain prediction for any type of model
  \end{itemize}
\item
  It's hard to find the variable importance for predicting certain output.
\item
  The attribution to a base feature (inputs, instead of parameters) can be interpreted as the importance of the feature to the prediction
\item
  Treat all models as black boxes and understand why they make each individual prediction
\item
  Undersigning why a model makes each individual prediction will help the user determine whether to trust the model
\item
  \textbf{Local surrogate models}: approximate the predictions of black-box models locally
\item
  Instead of training a global surrogate model, LIME trains many local surrogate models to explain individual prediction
\item
  These local surrogate models must behave similarly to how the black-box model does in the neighborhood of each instance being explained (local faithfulness)
\end{itemize}

Steps to train local surrogate models

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Select a prediction from the black-box model that you want to explain
\item
  Perturb the dataset and get the black-box predictions for these new points
\item
  Weight the new samples based on their proximity to the point of interest (i.e., your chosen prediction)
\item
  train a weighted, interpretable model (e.g., regression) on the perturbed dataset, using the black-box predictions as the response
\item
  Explain the prediction by interpreting the local model
\end{enumerate}

LIME produces an explanation for a given instance of \((\mathbf{y,x})\)

We want to know which of the \(\mathbf{x}\) features are most responsible for a given prediction \(\mathbf{\hat{y}}\)

\[
\text{explanation}(\mathbf{x}) = \underset{g \in G}{\operatorname{argmax}} L (f, g, \pi_x) + \Omega(g)
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(f\): the original black-box model
\item
  \(g\) the explanation model for the instance
\item
  \(G\) the family of possible explanation models (e.g., linear regression models)
\item
  \(\pi_x\) proximity measure that determines how large the neighborhood is around \(\mathbf{x}\)
\item
  \(\Omega(g)\): model complexity (want to be small - fewer features in explanation)
\end{itemize}

To define these neighborhoods

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\pi_x\) is an exponential smoothing kernel
\item
  Small kernel width means that observation must be very close to influence the local model
\item
  Large kernel width means that observations that are very different from the instance we are seeking to explain can have influence on the local model
\item
  kernel widths are very subjective (have to experiment and see which ones result in meaningful explanations)
\end{itemize}

Notes:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  LIME is easy to use in R (\texttt{lime} or \texttt{iml} package)
\item
  The difficult part is evaluating how good the local surrogate model is at estimating the complex model locally, which can affect the interpretability
\item
  Heat map can show how all the variables have an influence on the predictions
\end{itemize}

\hfill\break
References for LIME

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \url{https://www.oreilly.com/content/introduction-to-local-interpretable-model-agnostic-explanations-lime/}
\item
  \url{https://homes.cs.washington.edu/~marcotcr/blog/lime/}
\item
  \citet{ribeiro2016}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{shapley-values}{%
\subsection{Shapley Values}\label{shapley-values}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Shapley values are a game theoretic approach to explain the output of any machine learning model (migrated from econ to stat)
\item
  These values can also combined with other approaches such as LIME.
\item
  Similar to LIME, the problem is distributing the prediction score of a model for a specific input to its base features
\item
  Game theory inspiration:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Introduced in 1951 by Lloyd Shapley as a method to value individual play contributions in a cooperative game (which won him a Nobel Prize in Econ)
  \item
    Example

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      Determine value of players individually and in subsets
    \item
      Determine marginal values fro players from all possible orderings
    \item
      Find the Shapley value by taking average of marginal values
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Formally, for a game with \(N\) players, the characteristics (value) function \(\nu\) amps subsets \(S \in \{0, 1,\dots, N\}\) to a real value \(\nu(S)\), the value to be gained from player cooperation

The marginal contribution for each play is

\[
\Delta_\nu (i,S) = \nu (S \cup i) - \nu(S)
\]

The Shapley value (contribution to the ``grand coalition'') for player \(i\) is

\[
\phi_i (\nu) = \frac{1}{N} \sum_{S \subseteq N_{\{i\}}} 
\left(
\begin{array}
{c}
n-1 \\
|S|
\end{array}
\right) ^{-1}
\Delta_\nu (i,S)
\]

which means

\[
\varphi (\nu) = \frac{1}{\text{number of players}} \sum_{\text{coalitions excluding i}} \frac{\text{marginal contribution of i to coalition}}{\text{number of coalitions excluding i of this size}}
\]

where \(x_S\) has missing values for the features not in set \(S\)

Desirable properties of Shapley Values

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Unique
\item
  Symmetry: if two players have equal marginal contribution, they will have equal Shapley values
\item
  Dummy: if the marginal contribution is 0, the Shapley value will be 0
\item
  Additivity: for a single player, \(i\), with two value functions, \(\nu\) and \(w\),
\end{itemize}

\[
\phi_i(\nu) + \phi_i (w) = \phi_i (\nu + w)
\]

They are useful to ensure that the feature attribution correspondence to human understanding.

Application to predictive modeling

If the game is a single outcome, with players as features \(x_1, \dots, x_p\) with a value function \(\nu_f\) with respect to the model \(f\), then the Shapley value for feature \(i\) can be viewed as the influence of that feature on the outcome

The key is then how one defines the value function, \(\nu_f\)

Possible approaches:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Conditional Expectation variants

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Shapley regression values \citep{lipovetsky2001, lipovetsky2001a}
  \item
    Shapley sampling values \citep{trumbelj2013}
  \item
    SHAP \citep{lundberg2017unified}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Intervention variants

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Quantitative input influence \citep{datta2016}
  \item
    Formulate, Approximate, Explain framework \citep{merrick2020}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{SH}apley \textbf{A}dditive ex\textbf{P}lanation (SHAP) values

The value function is defined as

\[
\nu_{f,x}(S) = f(x_s) = E[f(x)|x_s] = E_{x_{\bar{S}}|x_S}[f(x)] \\
\text{assume feature independence} \approx E_{x_{\bar{S}}}[f(x)] \\
\text{assume model linearity} \approx f([x_s, E[x_{\bar{S}}]])
\]

where \(x_S\) has missing values fro the features not in set , \(S\)

Exact computation is challenging, but there are numerous model -agnostic approximation methods and model-type-specific approximation methods

these have varying assumptions, such as feature independence and model linearity (as in the equation above)

Approximation Methods

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Model-agnostic

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    If we assume feature independence, we can use sampling approximation methods from \citep{trumbelj2013} and \citep{datta2016}; reasonable to compute fro small number of inputs only
  \item
    Kernel SHAP (LIME + Shapley): reformulate as a weighted linear regression problem to jointly esitamte all SHAP values
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Model-type-sepcific

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    LInear SHAP
  \item
    Low-order SHAP
  \item
    Max SHAP
  \item
    Deep SHAP (DeepLift + Shapley)
  \item
    Tree SHAP \citep{lundberg2018consistent}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Implementation

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Python: \texttt{shap}: developed by Lundberg provides Kernel SHAP + model-type-specific SHAP algorithm; support for TensorFLow/Keras/PyTorch models
\item
  R: \texttt{shapr} provides Kernel SHAP + \citet{aas2021} method for accounting for feature dependence; support for \texttt{gam}, \texttt{lm}, \texttt{glm}, \texttt{xgb.Booster}, \texttt{ranger,}shapper: port for Python library \texttt{shap}
\item
  Others: \texttt{fastshap}, \texttt{iml}, shap-value, \texttt{DALEX}, \texttt{shapleyR}
\end{itemize}

Critiques

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Mathematical Issues

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Indirect influence: a feature which may have no effect on the response but is predictive of other features may attribute influence
  \item
    How should statistically-related features be handled? SHould they be considered a ``single player''?
  \item
    Intervention methods rely on out-of-distribution sampling and extrapolate outside of feature space
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Human Interpretability Issues

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Shapley value answer question: ``Why \(f(x)\) instead of \(E[f(x)]\)?, while we may not actually observe data with the outcome \(E[f(x)]\)''
  \item
    Does not provide guidance on changing outcomes
  \item
    Prone to misinterpretation
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

References for Shapley

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \citet{datta2016}
\item
  \citet{kumar2020problems}
\item
  \citet{lipovetsky2001a}
\item
  \citet{lundberg2018consistent}
\item
  \citet{lundberg2017unified}
\item
  \citet{merrick2020explanation}
\item
  \citet{trumbelj2013}
\item
  \citet{sundararajan2020many}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{part-5.-others}{%
\part{5. OTHERS}\label{part-5.-others}}

\hypertarget{support-vector}{%
\chapter{Support Vector}\label{support-vector}}

\hypertarget{maximal-margin-classifier}{%
\section{Maximal Margin Classifier}\label{maximal-margin-classifier}}

We have n pairs of training data, \((x_1, y_1), \dots, (x_n, y_n)\), where \(x_i \in R^p\) and \(y_i \in \{-1, 1\}\), corresponding to two possible classes

Hyperplane in p-dimensional space is a ``flat'' affine subspace of dimension \(p-1\)

If \(p = 2\), the hyperplane is a line

If \(p = 3\), the hyperplane is a plane

In two dimensions, a hyperplane is defined by the equation

\[
\beta_0 + \beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2 = 0
\]

so that any point \(X_1, X_2\) that satisfies this equation is on the hyperplane

Then, in \(p\)-dimensions, the hyperplane is defined by

\[
\beta_0 + \beta_1 X_1 + \dots + \beta_p X_p = 0
\]

or equivalently

\[
\beta_0 + \mathbf{X}^T \beta = 0
\]

If the point \(X = (X_1, \dots, X_p)^T\) does not lie on the hyperplane, it lies on one side or the other

\[
\beta_0 + X^T \beta >0 \\
\beta_0 + X^T \beta <0
\]

Hence, the hyperplane divides the p-dimensional space in two sides to determine which side the point is on, we can find the sign of \(\beta_0 + X^T \beta\) (i.e., \(G(x) = sign [\beta_0 + X^T \beta]\)

We want to classify, \(x^* = (x_1^*, \dots, x^*_p)^T\) from the separating hyperplane

We start by assuming that it's possible to construct a hyperplane that separates the observations perfectly

Thus, our classification of \((x_1, y_i)\) is

\[
\begin{cases}
\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta > 0 & \text{if } y_i = 1 \\
\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta <0 & \text{if } y_i = -1
\end{cases}
\]

This is equivalent to noting that the separating hyperplane has the property

\[
y_i (\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta) >0
\]

for all \(i = 1, \dots, n\)

Thus, our classifier classifies \(x^*\) into the first group if \(f(x^*) = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_1^* + \dots + \beta_p x_p^*\) is positive and

into the second group if this is negative (i.e., this \(G(x)\) defined above)

The magnitude of \(f(x^*)\) tells us how far \(x^*\) is from the hyperplane

Clearly, \textbf{the separating hyperplane is just a linear decision boundary}

We can have an infinite number of such separating hyperplanes.

We can define an \textbf{optimal separating hyperplane} as \textbf{the maximal margin hyperplane}, which is just the hyperplane that is farthest from the training observations, which can be done by considering the perpendicular distance between each observation and hyperplane;

The smallest such distance is the minimal distance from the observations to the hyperplane, and is known as the \textbf{margin}

The \textbf{maximal margin hyperplane} is then the separating hyperplane that has the largest margin (i.e., the one that has the farthest minimum distance to the training observations).

The classifier built with this hyperplane is then known as \textbf{the maximal margin classifier}

The maximal margin hyperplane presents the mid-line between the two classes with the widest ``slab'' that we can insert between the classes

Points that are used to make the hyperplane are examples of \textbf{support vectors} (i.e., they are vectors in p-dimensional space that ``support'' the maximal margin hyperplane in that if they were located at different locations, the maximal margin hyperplane would move as well)

All of the other points do not affect the maximal margin hyperplane (i.e., if they move, it doesn't change the location of the maximal margin hyperplane - so long as they don't move across the existing margin boundaries).

This property of support vectors is important in the construction of support vector classifiers and supper vector machines

We can find the maximal margin hyperplane by solving the following convex optimization problem

\[
\underset{\beta_0,\dots, \beta_p}{\operatorname{max}} M
\]

subject to

\[
\sum_{j = 1}^p \beta_j^2 = 1
\]

i.e., \(||\beta|| = 1\)

and

\[
y_i (\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta) \ge M,\forall i = 1, \dots, n
\]

The last constraint guarantees that each observation will be on the correct side of the hyperplane, provided \(M\) is positive, with a cushion \(M\)

The first constraint identifies the parameters such that the perpendicular distance between the i-th observation and the hyperplane is given by \(y_i (\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta)\). This ensures that each observation is at least a distance \(M\) from the hyperplane - hence, \(M\) is the margin

However, this method would not work fi no separating hyperplane exists (i.e., if no hyperplane can separate the two classes)

Two cases where a separating hyperplane may not be desirable even if possible

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  If it has a very small margin
\item
  it very sensitive to one or two observations, it will most likely overfit
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{support-vector-classifiers}{%
\section{Support Vector Classifiers}\label{support-vector-classifiers}}

Due to the previous two problems, we seek a classifier that is based on a hyperplane that does not perfectly separate the classes to give

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  greater robustness to individual observation
\item
  better classification of most of the training observation
\end{enumerate}

Such a \textbf{soft margin classifier} is the support vector classifier, which is constructed similarly to the maximal margin classifier but we allow some observations to be on the incorrect side of the margin or even the incorrect side of the hyperplane

The procedure for implementing a support vector classifier is also an optimization problem with a modification of one of the constraints and the addition of another

The support vector classifier is given by the solution to the following optimization problem

\[
\underset{\beta_0,\dots, \beta_p, \epsilon_1, \dots, \epsilon_n}{\operatorname{max}} M
\]

subject to

\[
\sum_{j=1}^p \beta_j^2 = 1
\]

(i.e., \(||\beta|| = 1\)) and

\[
y_i (\beta_0 + x_i^T \beta) \ge M(1 - \epsilon_i), \forall i = 1, \dots, n
\]

\[
\epsilon_i \ge 0, \sum_{i=1}^n \epsilon_i \le C
\]

where \(C\) is a non-negative tuning parameter

\(M\) is the margin

we have \(\epsilon_i, i = 1, \dots, n\), which are called \textbf{slack variables} that provide the flexibility to be on the wrong side of the margin or hyperplane

Then, we classify a new observation \(x^*\) via the sign of \(f(x^*)\) as before

The \(\epsilon_i\) parameters give an indication of where the observation is located relative to the margin

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\epsilon_i = 0\) means it's on the correct side of the margin
\item
  \(\epsilon_i >0\) means it's on the wrong side of the margin
\item
  \(\epsilon_i >1\) means it's on the wrong side of the hyperplane
\end{itemize}

Since \(C\) is the upper bound of the sum fo the \(\epsilon_i\), it determines the number of severity of the violations to the margin (and hyperplane) that we can tolerate, which is like a budget for the amount that the margin can be violated by n observations \citep[p.~376]{james2013}

If \(C = 0\), then there is no budget for violations and \(\epsilon_i = 0, i = 1, \dots, n\) (which is just the {[}Maximal Marginal Classifier{]} if the classes are separable)

If \(C >0\), no more than \(C\) observations can be on the wrong side of the hyperplane (recall, \(\epsilon_i > 1\) if the observation is misclassified)

As \(C\) increases, we become more tolerant to violations of the margin, so the margin widens

\(C\) acts as a tuning parameter that controls the variance-bias trade-off and can be chosen by cross-validation

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(C\) small: narrow margins, few margin violations, low bias, high variance, fewer support vectors
\item
  \(C\) large: wide margins, more violations, more bias, but less variance, many support vectors
\end{itemize}

Again, support vectors are those observations that are on or between the margins

The fact that the support vector decision rule is based on a relative few observations (support vectors) means that it's relative \textbf{robust to the behavior of observations far away from the hyperplane}, which is different from the linear discrimination rules

\hypertarget{nonlinear-decision-boundaries}{%
\section{Nonlinear Decision Boundaries}\label{nonlinear-decision-boundaries}}

Instead of fitting a support vector classifier using the \(p\) features, \(X_1, \dots, X_p\), we consider \(2p\) features (linear and quadratic)

\[
X_1, X_1^2, \dots, X_p, X^2_p
\]

Then, the optimization problem become

\[
\underset{\beta_0,\dots, \beta_p, \epsilon_1, \dots, \epsilon_n}{\operatorname{max}}
\]

subject to

\[
y_i (\beta_0 + \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_{j1}x_{1j} + \sum_{j=1}^p \beta_{j2} x_{ij}^2) \ge M (1 - \epsilon_i), \forall i = 1, \dots, n
\]

\[
\epsilon_i \ge 0 , \sum_{i=1}^n \epsilon_i \le C, \sum_{j = 1}^p \sum_{k = 1}^2 \beta_{jk}^2 = 1
\]

Although this is nonlinear (quadratic) in the original feature space, it's linear in the expanded (2p-dimensional) space

Then, we could extend the feature space to be beyond quadratic terms, such as polynomials and interactions, or other functions of the predictors (e.g., basis)

There are an infinite number of choices for such terms.\\
The support vector machine (svm) provides a principled way to enlarge the feature space used in a support vector classifier in a way that facilitates computation

There are two things that make this tractable

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Only the support vectors are relevant for determining the classification boundary
\item
  A kernel representation that is easily generalizable
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{support-vector-machines}{%
\section{Support Vector Machines}\label{support-vector-machines}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  is an extension of the \protect\hyperlink{support-vector-classifiers}{Support Vector Classifiers} that results from enlarging the feature space to include transformations of the inputs using kernels
\end{itemize}

Consider the collection of \(m\) basis functions int terms of the features, \(x\)

\[
h(x_i) = (h_1(x_i), h_2(x_i), \dots, h_m (x_i))^T, i = 1, \dots, n
\]

Then,w e could use the \protect\hyperlink{support-vector-classifiers}{Support Vector Classifiers} to find the parameters \(\hat{\beta}\) that minimize \(f(x) = \beta_0 + h(x)^T \beta\)

Then, classification can be accomplished by \(\hat{G}(x) = sign (\hat{f}(x))\)

Then, we can we choose the basis functions?

We don't need to select basis function, but rather it's sufficient to choose a positive definite kernel function \(K(x_i, x_{i'})\) which will imply a finite collection of basis functions

\textbf{Reminder}

Inner Products

The inner product of two r-dimensional vectors a and b is defined as \(<a,b> = \sum_{j=1}^r a_j b_j\)

Then, the inner product of two inputs \(x_i\) and \(x_i'\) is

\[
<x_i, x_i'> = \sum_{j= 1}^p x_{ij} x_{i'j}
\]

Importantly, we can represent a positive definite kernel in terms of the inner product of some set of basis functions:

\[
K(x_i, x_{i'}) = < h(x_i), h(x_{i'})>
\]

Example with a second-degree polynomial kernel

\[
K(x, \tilde{x}) = (1 + <x, \tilde{x}>)^2
\]

and \(p = 2\); e.g., \(x = (x_1, x_2), \tilde{x} = (\tilde{x}_1, \tilde{x}_2)\)

Then,

\[
\begin{aligned}
K(x, \tilde{x}) &= (1 + < x, \tilde{x}>)^2 \\
& = (1 + x_1 \tilde{x}_1 + x_2  \tilde{x}_2)^2 \\
&= 1 + 2 x_1 \tilde{x}_1 + 2 x_2 \tilde{x}_2 + (x_1 \tilde{x}_1)^2 + (x_2 \tilde{x}_2)^2 + 2 x_1 \tilde{x}_1 x_2 \tilde{x}_2
\end{aligned}
\]

If we write \(K(x, \tilde{x}) = <h(x), h(\tilde{x})>\), we have \(m = 6\) basis function with

\[
h_1(x) = 1 \\
h_2(x) = \sqrt{2}x_1 \\
h_3(x) = \sqrt{2}x_2 \\
h_4 (x) = x_1^2 \\
h_5 (x) = x_2^2 \\
h_6(x) = \sqrt{2} x_1 x_2
\]

This is a powerful tool in regularized (penalized) modeling, known as \textbf{Reproducing Kernel Hilbert Spaces} (RKHS)

\hypertarget{regularization-by-rkhs}{%
\subsection{Regularization by RKHS}\label{regularization-by-rkhs}}

\citep[5.8]{hastie2001a}

In general, the regularization (penalized loss) problem is

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg1}
\underset{f \in H}{\operatorname{min}} [ \sum_{i = 1}^n L(y_i, f(x_i)) + \lambda J(f)]
\end{equation}

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(L(y_i, f(x_i))\) is the loss function
\item
  \(J(f)\) is a penalty function
\item
  \(H\) is a space of functions on which \(J(f)\) is defined
\end{itemize}

Consider a \textbf{positive define kernel} \(K(x, \tilde{x})\) for \(x, \tilde{x} \in R^p\), that has an eigen-expansion given by

\[
K(x, \tilde{x}) = \sum_{j=1}^\infty \delta_j \phi_j(x) \phi_j (\tilde{x})
\]

with eigenvalue \(\delta_j >0, \sum_{j = 1}^\infty \delta^2_j < \infty\) and eigenfunctions \(\{ \phi_j (.)\}\)

The idea is to use eigenfunctions as basis functions

We seek functions \(f(x)\) in terms of these eigenfunctions,

\[
f(x) = \sum_{j=1}^\infty c_j \phi_j (x)
\]

that satisfy the constraint that \(J(f) = ||f||^2_{H_K} \equiv \sum_{j=1}^\infty c_j^2/\delta_j < \infty\)

This function space, \(H_K\), is called a reproducing kernel Hilbert space because the inner product of K with itself gives \(K\) (i.e., \(<K(.,x_i), K(x,x_{i'})> = K(x_i, x_{i'})\) it reproduces itself)

Then, we can rewrite the regularization problem as

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg2}
\underset{\{c_j\}^\infty_1}{\operatorname{min}} [\sum_{i=1}^nL(y_i, \sum_{j = 1}^\infty c_j \phi_j (x_i)) + \lambda \sum_{j = 1}^\infty \frac{c_j^2}{\delta_j} ]
\end{equation}

Even though we might think that we have \textbf{infinite} number of choices for \(\phi_j\), the solution to the regularization problem here can be shown to be given by (\textbf{finite})

\[
f(x) = \sum_{i=1}^n \alpha_i K(x, x_i)
\]

where (\textbf{finite})

\[
J(f) = \sum_{i=1}^n \sum_{j=1}^n K(x_i, x_j) \alpha_i \alpha_j
\]

Critically, we note that \textbf{although the optimization function is infinite dimensional, this solution is finite-dimensional (this is the kernel trick in machine learning)}

In fact, the infinite-dimensional optimization problem reduces to the following finite dimensional optimization problem

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg3}
\underset{\alpha}{\operatorname{min}} [ L(\mathbf{y}, \mathbf{K\alpha})+ \lambda \mathbf{\alpha}^T \mathbf{K \alpha}]
\end{equation}

where \(\mathbf{K}\) is a matrix with \((i,j)\)th entry given by \(K(x_i, x_j)\)

This is powerful because it works for \textbf{any} (convex) loss function and \textbf{any} (positive definite) kernel function (from \eqref{eq:reg1} to \eqref{eq:reg2} to \eqref{eq:reg3} )

This idea relates back to \protect\hyperlink{support-vector-machines}{Support Vector Machines} because all we need is the loss function associated with SVMs and to choose a positive definite kernel.

It has been worked out that the loss function associated with the support vector classifier is given by \([1 - y_i f(x_i)]_+\) (where the ``+'' indicates the positive portion of the function in the brackets), which is called ``hinge loss.''

Then the solution of the following constrained minimization problem gives the same estimates for \(\beta\)

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg4}
\underset{\beta_0, \beta}{\operatorname{min}} [ \sum_{i=1}^n [1 - y_i f(x_i)]_+ + \frac{\lambda}{2}||\beta||^2]
\end{equation}

where \(\lambda\) is proportional to \(C\) as before

Suppose a basis set \(\{ h_j(x) \}\) comes from the eigen-decomposition of \(K(x, \tilde{x}) = \sum_{j = 1}^\infty \delta_j \phi_j (x) \phi_j (\tilde{x})\); hence \(h_j(x) = \sqrt{\delta_j} \phi_j(x)\) Let \(\delta_j \equiv \sqrt{\delta_j} \beta_j\), (recall that the desired function is an expansion in these basis functions), the minimization above can be written

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg5}
\underset{\beta_0, \theta}{\operatorname{min}} [ \sum_{i=1}^n [1 - y_i (\beta_0 + \sum_{j=1}^\infty \theta_j \phi_j(x_i))]_+ + \frac{\lambda}{2} \sum_{j=1}^\infty \frac{\theta_j^2}{\delta_j}]
\end{equation}

One of the strengths of the RKHS approach to the SVM problem is that the penalized loss approach suggests that it's fairly simple to consider any convex loss function. For example, using logistic regression as a classifier is similar to the SVM because the binomial deviance loss is similar to the hinge loss

Then, the RKHS theory that we discussed gives a finite-dimensional solution ( \eqref{eq:reg3}) \(f(x) = \beta_0 + \sum_{i=1}^n \alpha_i K(x, x_i)\), that equivalently minimizes

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:reg6}
\underset{\beta_0, \alpha}{\operatorname{min}} [ [1 - y_i f(x_i)]_+ + \frac{\lambda}{2} \mathbf{\alpha}^T \mathbf{K \alpha}] 
\end{equation}

For example, we chose a linear kernel, \(K(x, x_i) = <x, x_i>\) (which is essentially the Pearson correlation), then we obtain the solution \(f(x) = \beta_0 + \sum_{i=1}^n \alpha_i <x, x_i>\), which is just the \protect\hyperlink{support-vector-classifiers}{Support Vector Classifiers}

For SVM analysis, we typically select a more complicated kernel to allow flexible classification boundaries. Common choices are:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  d-th degree polynomial kernel: \(K(x, \tilde{x}) = (1 + <x, \tilde{x}>)^d\)
\item
  radial basis kernel (effectively is the Gaussian): \(K(x, \tilde{x}) = \exp(-\gamma ||x - \tilde{x}||^2 )\)
\item
  neural network kernel: \(K(x, \tilde{x}) = \tanh (\kappa_1 <x, \tilde{x}>+ \kappa_2)\)
\end{itemize}

The drawback is that you don't know which one to pick.

An advantage of being able to use finite-dimensional kernel representation is that we only need to consider the \(\binom{n}{2}\) distinct pairs of \(x_i\) and \(x_{i'}\) in the kernel matrix. Even though it can still be a large number of kernel, but it's certainly better than infinite.

Moreover, \(\alpha_i = 0\) if the observed feature vector \(x_i\) is not a support vector, because the hinge loss function is 0 for observations for which \(y_i f(x_i) \ge 1\), which are those on the correct side of the margin. This simplifies the calculation in the procedure and we write

\[
f(x) = \beta + \sum_{i \in S} \alpha_i K(x, x_i)
\]

where \(S\) is the set of support vectors.

It can be shown that for a given \(\lambda\) that \(\beta = (1/\lambda) \sum_{i \in S} \alpha_i y_i x_i\)

\textbf{Alternative Loss Functions}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Huberized loss}, which has some of the attractive features of the hinge loss (support points) and logistic regression (smooth loss function for which one can estimate probabilities probabilities) - good with outliers
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Extensions to Multiple Classes}

It isn't clear how we should extend the SVM machinery from the binary case to the multiple case The notion of a separating hyperplane is hard to generalize to more than two classes There are few possible approaches: (1) one-versus-one or (2) one-versus-all approaches

The \textbf{one-versus-on approach} for \(K > 2\) classes finds \(\binom{K}{2}\) SVMs for each pair of classes. Then, given a new observation, each SVM classifier is used and one keeps track of the number of times the test observation was assigned to each class. The one that is assigned most frequently is chosen

The \textbf{one-versus-all} (preferable) approach fits K different SVMs comparing each class to all of the others, thus getting K functions, \(f_k(x), k = 1, \dots, K\). Given test observation \(x^*\), one selects the class, k for which \(f_k(x^*)\) is largest

GIven that logistic regression gives similar results to SVMs for binary classes, a multinomial-logistic classifier (i.e., multinomial loss function) might be a better way to do classification for \(K>2\)

\hypertarget{svm-regression}{%
\subsection{SVM Regression}\label{svm-regression}}

We can extend the SVm method to the case of continuous responses (regression), known as support vector machine regression Given the ``loss plus penalty'' paradigm, we can do this by looking for the \(\beta\) coefficients in the function \(f(x) = \beta_0 + x^T \beta\) relative to some loss function.
