To make it analogous to the SVM case, we need a loss function that accommodates something like the margin in the SVM case (i.e., we seek a loss function where only residuals larger in absolute value than some positive constant contribute to the loss):

\[
\underset{\beta_0, \beta}{\operatorname{min}} [ V_\epsilon (y_i - f(x_i)) + \frac{\lambda}{2}||\beta||^2]
\]

where

\[
V_\epsilon(r) \equiv
\begin{cases}
0, & \text{if } |r| < \epsilon \\
|r|- \epsilon, & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

where errors \(< \epsilon\) are ignored \citep[12.3.6]{hastie2001a}

The SVM regression loss function is similar to other robust regression loss functions (e.g., the Huber loss)

\hypertarget{discriminant-analysis}{%
\chapter{Discriminant Analysis}\label{discriminant-analysis}}

Assumption:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Multivariate normality
\item
  Homoskedasticity: assumption for LDA only, QDA can still be used with unequal covariances
\item
  No Multicollinearity: because it can decrease your predictive power
\item
  Independent predictors
\end{itemize}

LDA with \(p\) inputs \(x\) for \(k = 1, \dots ,K\) classes proceeds by finding the minimum value of the discriminant function for a given \(x\)

\[
\delta_k(x) = x^T \Sigma^{-1} \mu_k - \frac{1}{2} \mu_k^T \Sigma^{-1} \mu_k + \log \pi_k
\]

for each class, where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\Sigma\) is the pooled covariance matrix for all classes
\item
  \(\mu_k\) is the class-specific mean (centroid)
\item
  \(\pi_k\) is the prior probability of being in class \(k\)
\end{itemize}

In practice, we don't know the parameters \(\Sigma,\mu_k, \pi_k\) and either estimate them or in the case of \(\pi_k\) specify them.

The quadratic discriminant function (QDA) follows similarly, except we accommodate a different covariance matrix for each class, \(\Sigma_k\), which gives

\[
\delta_k(x) = -\frac{1}{2} \log|\Sigma_k| - \frac{1}{2} (x - \mu_k)^T \Sigma_k^{-1} (x- \mu_k) + \log \pi_k
\]

Virtues of LDA

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Simple and typically good classifier
\item
  It's the estimated Bayes classifier if the observations are really from a multivariate normal distribution with a common covariance matrix (unlikely in practice)
\item
  the decision boundaries are linear, making the decision rule simple to describe and implement
\item
  Provides a natural low-dimensional view of the data
\item
  It often works well because it's simple and has low variance.
\end{itemize}

Problems with LDA

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Linear boundaries are often not adequate to separate classes. when the sample size is larger, we can efficiently estimate nonlinear boundaries
\item
  A single centroid for each class is not sufficient many applications
\item
  In some problems, there may be too many predictors (\(p\) is large relative to \(n\)) and LDA has too many parameters that are estimated with high variance
\end{itemize}

Extensions of LDA

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Recast the LDA problem as a linear regression problem, and then generalize it to include functions of the \(x\) inputs - so called ``flexible discriminant analysis'' (FDA)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    FDA also stands for ``Functional Data analysis'' in the broader statistical jargon
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Penalize the coefficients in the LDA when there are too many coefficients
\item
  Model each class as a mixture of two or more Gaussians with different centroids, but with all fo the Gaussians sharing the same covariance matrix .
\end{itemize}

Background

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Related to fining lower-dimensional linear classifiers (R.A. Fisher), his idea was to obtain a reasonable representation of the class population that involved only a few linear combination fo the observation (e.g., \(a_1' x, a_2'x, \dots\) (which can be useful for visual inspection or graphical description of the class centroids). This is also described as "reduced-rank linear discriminant analysis \citep[4.3.3]{hastie2001a}
\item
  The idea is we seek a reduced set of \textbf{canonical} or \textbf{discriminant variables} to represent the classification. To implement this, we need

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    the between-class covariance matrix \(\mathbf{B}\)
  \item
    the within-class covariance matrix \(\mathbf{W}\)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{reduced-rank-lda}{%
\section{Reduced-Rank LDA}\label{reduced-rank-lda}}

We define the between and within-class covariance matrices as:

\[
\mathbf{B} = \sum_{k=1}^K (\mu_k - \mu)(\mu_k - \mu)'
\]

B looks at how far away the centroids are from the overall mean for each group

\[
\mathbf{W} = \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{j=1}^{n_k} (x_{kj} - \mu_k) (x_{kj}- \mu_k)'
\]

W looks at how far away from the group centroids.

where

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\mu_k\) is the \(p\)-dimensional k-th class mean
\item
  \(\mu\) is the \(p\)-dimensional overall mean
\item
  \(x_{kj}\) corresponds to the \(p\)-dimensional input vector fo the j-th element in the k-th group
\end{itemize}

We then seek the linear combination \(Z = a^T X\) such that the \textbf{between-class variance is maximized relative to the within-class variance} (i.e., the between-class variance of \(Z\) is \(a^T \mathbf{B} a\) and the within-class variance is \(a^T \mathbf{W}a\))

We seek the \(a\) that maximizes the quotient of \(a^T \mathbf{B} a\) divided by \(a^T \mathbf{W} a\)

The solution to the maximization problem is the ``a'' given by the eigenvector associated with the largest eigven value of \(\mathbf{W}^{-1}\mathbf{B}\), which we call \(a_1\)

One can show that the next direction \(a_2\) that maximizes this quotient and is orthogonal to \(a_1\) is the eigenvector associated with the next largest eigenvalue, etc.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \(a_l\) are referred to as \textbf{discriminant coordinates} (not discriminant functions) or canonical \textbf{variates}
\end{itemize}

This sequence of discriminant then provides the low-dimensional presentation fo the data that separates the populations as much as possible.

In addition to separating the populations, these discriminants can be used to classify.

Let \(z_l(x_i) = a^T_l x_i, l = 1, \dots, K-1\) be the discriminants for the \(i\)-th input \(x_i\), which can be shown to have variance of one and are uncorrelated with each other

Then, it can be shown that for an observation, \(x\), the squared distance from \(z_l(x)\) to the \(k\)-th class centroid (\(\mu_k\)) is given by \(\sum_{l=1}^{K-1}(z_l (x) - \mu_{z_l^k})^2\), where \(\mu_{z_l^k}\) is the mean of \(\{z_l(x_j)\}\) for observations \(x_j\) in the \(k\)-th class.

A reasonable classification rule classifies \(x\) to class \(k\) with the smallest value of this squared distance.

\hypertarget{flexible-discriminant-analysis}{%
\section{Flexible Discriminant Analysis}\label{flexible-discriminant-analysis}}

FDA could be confused with functional data analysis

If we have \(L = K - 1\) score functions \(\theta_l(i)\) (a function in \(R^1\) that assigns scores to the classes so taht they can be modeled by ``regression'' functions) then we seek these scores and the coefficients \(\beta_l\) that minimize the average squared residual

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{l=1}^L [\sum_{i=1}^n (\theta_l (i) - x_i^T \beta_l)^2]
\]

Importantly, \textbf{the sequence of} \(\beta_l\)\textbf{'s can be shown to be the same as the discriminant (canonical) vectors} (\(a_l\)) in the \protect\hyperlink{reduced-rank-lda}{Reduced-Rank LDA} methodology, up to a constant, and the scores (\(\theta_s\)) correspond to the \(Z\)'s

The \(\beta\) coefficients act like linear regression coefficients, which mean we can generalize this procedure as we did in nonparametric regression and replace the regression fits by some more flexible class of functions, \(\eta_l(x)\) (e.g., \protect\hyperlink{spline-regression}{Spline}, \protect\hyperlink{multivariate-adaptive-regression-splines}{MARS}, etc.)

Hence, a more general classifier is defined by

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{l=1}^{L} [\sum_{i=1}^n (\theta_l(i) - \eta_l(x_i))^2 + \lambda J(\eta_l)]
\]

where we include the regularization term fro some forms of nonparametric regression (e.g., \protect\hyperlink{spline-regression}{Smoothing splines}, etc.)

Additive spline is a more flexible approach (called ``bruto'' can be found in \texttt{mda} package in R). More details can be found \citep[ 12.5.1]{hastie2001a}

\hypertarget{penalized-discriminant-analysis}{%
\section{Penalized Discriminant Analysis}\label{penalized-discriminant-analysis}}

\protect\hyperlink{flexible-discriminant-analysis}{Flexible Discriminant Analysis} is a form of regularized discriminant analysis.\\
For example, consider an FDA with a linear regression onto a basis expansion \(h(x)\), with a quadratic penalty on the coefficients,

\[
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{l = 1}^L [\sum_{i=1}^n ( \theta_l(i)- h^T (x_i)\beta_l)^2 + \lambda \beta_l^T \mathbf{\Omega} \beta_l]
\]

where the choice of \(\mathbf{\Omega}\) depends on the specific problem,

For example, if \(\eta_l(x) = h(x)^T \beta_l\) is an expansion on the spline basis functions, \(\Omega\) might constrain \(\eta_l\) to be smooth

The idea is that we seek to \textbf{give more weight to ``smooth'' coefficients and less weight to ``rough'' ones, which can be seen if we consider highly correlated predictors} (such as with images).

This procedure is known as \protect\hyperlink{penalized-discriminant-analysis}{Penalized Discriminant Analysis}

\hypertarget{mixture-discriminant-analysis}{%
\section{Mixture Discriminant Analysis}\label{mixture-discriminant-analysis}}

We sometimes refer to LDA as a prototype analysis in that there is just a single centroid for each class.

More generally, such a single prototype classifier might be sufficient if the classes are inhomogeneous.

In that case, we might consider a mixture of Gaussian models to present each class.

Recall, a Gaussian mixture model for the k-th class has density

\[
P(X|G= k ) = \sum_{r=1}^{R_k} q_{kr} \phi(X; \mu_{kr}, \Sigma)
\]

where the mixing proportions \(q_{kr}\) sum to 1.

In this case, there are \(R_k\) mixtures (prototypes) for the k-th class

But, notice that we are assuming the \textbf{same covariance matrix} \(\Sigma\) for each class and each prototype (estimating different covariance matrices can be expensive).

Estimating the parameters corresponding to this distribution can be tricky as the optimization can be difficult.

Typically, we use the expectation-maximization (E-M) algorithm \citep[ 12.7]{hastie2001a} or a fully-Bayesian implementation.

\hypertarget{large-p-small-n-approaches}{%
\section{Large p, small n Approaches}\label{large-p-small-n-approaches}}

Consider some procedures that are especially suited for the large p, small n case

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{diagonal-linear-discriminant-analysis}{Diagonal Linear Discriminant Analysis}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{nearest-shrunken-centroids}{Nearest Shrunken Centroids}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{diagonal-linear-discriminant-analysis}{%
\subsection{Diagonal Linear Discriminant Analysis}\label{diagonal-linear-discriminant-analysis}}

When \(p >> n\), it's not possible to fit a full LDA without some sort fo regularization.

A simple type of regularization assumes \textbf{independence of the features within class}

Thus, the within-class covariance matrix is just a diagonal matrix

But in reality, this typically isn't true, but we don't have enough data to estimate the covariances anyway

the benefit is that this greatly reduces the number of parameters that must be estimated.

Thus, the diagonal-covariance LDA rule is given by the discriminant score (we classify into the class with the maximum of this score):

\[
\delta_k(x^*) = - \sum_{j=1}^p \frac{(x_j^* - \bar{x}_{kj})^2}{s_j^2} + 2 \log \pi_k
\]

where

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(x^*\) is the p-dimensional test observation
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(s_j\) is the pooled within-class standard deviation of the j-th feature
\item
  \(\bar{x}_{kj}\) is the mean of the \(n_k\) values for the j-th feature in class \(k\)
\item
  \(\pi_k\) is the prior probability for class \(k\)
\end{itemize}

We say \(\bar{x}= (\bar{x}_{k1}, \dots, \bar{x}_{kp})^T\) is the centroid of class \(K\)

The first part of the diagonal LDA discriminant score is just the (negative) standardized squared distance of \(x^*\) to the k-th centroid, and

The second term corresponds to the prior classification probability for class \(k\)

Thus, we classify \(x^*\) into the \(k\)-th class that has the highest discriminant score. This classifier is often pretty good in high-dimensional settings.\\
This procedure is equivalent to the nearest centroid classifier

Unfortunately, this classifier has a drawback in that it uses all \(p\) of the features, and thus, it prohibits interpretation.

It's possible with additional regularization, to gain interpretability and improve test error, which gives us the \protect\hyperlink{nearest-shrunken-centroids}{Nearest Shrunken Centroids}

\hypertarget{nearest-shrunken-centroids}{%
\subsection{Nearest Shrunken Centroids}\label{nearest-shrunken-centroids}}

To regularize, we remove features that are not contributing to successful classification.

Hence, we shrink the class means toward the overall mean, for each feature separately.

This gives a regularized version of the \protect\hyperlink{diagonal-linear-discriminant-analysis}{Diagonal Linear Discriminant Analysis} or, equivalently, a regularized version of the nearest centroid classifier

The procedure is known as the \protect\hyperlink{nearest-shrunken-centroids}{Nearest Shrunken Centroids} approach

First, let

\[
d_{kj} = \frac{\bar{x}_{kj} - \bar{x}_j}{m_k (s_j + s_0)}
\]

where \(\bar{x}_j\) is the overall mean for the \(j\)-th feature

\(m^2_k = \frac{1}{n_k} - \frac{1}{n}\) and \(s_0\) is a small positive constant (usually the median of the \(s_j\) values)

We then shrink the \(d_{kj}\) toward 0 using the soft thresholding formula

\[
d_{kj}' = sign (d_{kj}) (|d_{kj}| - \Delta)_+
\]

where \(\Delta\) is usually determined by cross-validation

Note:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(d_{kj}'=0\) if the argument in \((.)_+\) is less than 0
\end{itemize}

Then, replacing \(d_{kj}\) with \(d_{kj}'\) and rearranging gives

\[
\bar{x}_{kj}' = \bar{x}_j + m_k (s_j + s_0) d_{kj}'
\]

which are the \textbf{shrunken centroids}.

These are then used in place of \(\bar{x}_{kj}\) in the discriminant score function for the \protect\hyperlink{diagonal-linear-discriminant-analysis}{Diagonal Linear Discriminant Analysis}

Now, only those \textbf{features that have a nonzero} \(d_{kj}'\) \textbf{for at least one of the classes contribute to the classification rule}, and thus many features are discarded

Example from \citep[ 18.4]{hastie2001a}

\hypertarget{regularized-discriminant-analysis}{%
\subsection{Regularized Discriminant Analysis}\label{regularized-discriminant-analysis}}

\citep[ 4.3.1]{hastie2001a} describes a hybrid approach between quadratic discriminant analysis and LDA where one shrinks the class-specific covariance matrices to a common pooled covariance matrix, which is known as \textbf{regularized discriminant analysis}

\[
\hat{\mathbf{\Sigma}}_k (\alpha) = \alpha \mathbf{\hat{\Sigma}}_k + (1- \alpha) \hat{\mathbf{\Sigma}}
\]

In the large p, small n situation, a simple variant of this is sometimes used to address the fact that the sample covariance matrix, \(\mathbf{\hat{\Sigma}}_k\) (or \(\mathbf{\hat{\Sigma}}\)) is singular when \(n < p\)

In particular, we can consider

\[
\hat{\mathbf{\Sigma}}_k (\gamma) = \gamma \hat{\mathbf{\Sigma}} + (1 - \gamma) diag (\hat{\mathbf{\Sigma}})
\]

for \(\gamma \in [0,1]\) (i.e., when \(\gamma \neq 1\) there is shrinkage of the sample covariance matrix toward its diagonal)

This has a similar flavor to ridge regression \ref{ridge-regression}, but shrinks to a diagonal matrix.

Typically, \(\gamma\) is chosen by cross-validation

Sometimes this is known as \(L_2\)\textbf{-penalized discriminant analysis}

\hypertarget{logistic-regression-with-quadratic-regularization}{%
\subsection{Logistic Regression with Quadratic Regularization}\label{logistic-regression-with-quadratic-regularization}}

We can also regularize multi-class logistic regression.\\
Consider a multi-class extension to logistic regression known as the \textbf{symmetric multiclass logical model} (multinomial):

\[
Pr(G = k |X=x) = \frac{\exp(\beta_{k0} + x^T \beta_k)}{\sum_{l=1}^K \exp(\beta_{l0} + x^T \beta_l)}
\]

To deal with the \(p >>n\) problem, we can regularize by maximizing the \(L_2\)-penalized log-likelihood

\[
\underset{\{\beta_{k0}, \beta_k\}_1^K}{\operatorname{max}} [ \sum_{i=1}^n \log Pr(g_i|x_i) - \frac{\lambda}{2}\sum_{k=1}^K ||\beta_k ||^2]
\]

This automatically forces \(\sum_{k=1}^K \hat{\beta}_{kj} = 0\) for \(j = 1,\dots, p\)

A nice feature of the \(L_2\) penalty is that it tends to shrink \textbf{correlated parameters toward each other.}

However, it doesn't shrink the parameters to exactly zero.

\hypertarget{other-regularization-penalties}{%
\subsection{Other Regularization Penalties}\label{other-regularization-penalties}}

We could consider an \(L_1\) (lasso \ref{lasso-regression}) penalty for the logistic classification, which would have the advantage in that a subset of the \(\beta\) parameters will be shrunk to exactly 0.

Thus, we want to maximize the following penalized log-likelihood:

\[
\underset{\{\beta_{k0}, \beta_k\}_1^K}{\operatorname{max}} [\sum_{i=1}^n \log Pr (g_i |x_i) - \lambda \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{j=1}^p | \beta_{kj}|]
\]

Another useful penalty is the \textbf{elastic net penalty}, which is a mixture of the \(L_1\) and \(L_2\) penalties and takes the form:

\[
\sum_{j=1}^p (\alpha|\beta_j| + (1- \alpha) \beta_j^2)
\]

This is a useful penalty because

\begin{itemize}
\item
  the \(L_2\) portion encourages common shrinkage for correlated parameter and
\item
  the \(L_1\) portion encourages sparsity (e.g., many coefficients shrunk to exactly 0)
\end{itemize}

One can also build penalty function that performs regularization in the context when ``nearby'' parameters are likely o be dependent e.g., as in spatial, time series, and functional data analysis)

The \textbf{fused lasso} is one such example.\\
The penalty function for equally spaced parameters is then

\[
\lambda_1 \sum_{j=1}^p |\beta_j| + \lambda_2 \sum_{j=1}^{p-1}|\beta_{j+1}- \beta_j|
\]

the second component can be modified as described in \citep[ 18.4.2]{hastie2001a} if the parameters are not equally spaced but are still ``neighbors.''

This can be extended to two-dimensional distances in the case of image data. \citep[ 18.4.2]{hastie2001a} show an example for a model of genome order of a gene in a tumor sample using what they call the fused lasso signal approximator which solves

\[
\underset{\beta \in R^n}{\operatorname{min}} \{ \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \beta_0 - \beta_i)^2 + \lambda_1 \sum_{i=1}^n |\beta_i| + \lambda_2 \sum_{i=1}^{n-1} |\beta_{i+1} - \beta_i| \}
\]

The interesting thing about this model is that basically each dat point has its own mean, so there are \(n+1\) parameters and \(n\) samples. \citep[ 18.8]{hastie2001a} (basically, we force all points to have the same mean unless the data suggest otherwise)

As it turns out, the support vector classifier \ref{support-vector-classifiers} works well in the large p, small n problem.

Although we can add regularization to the SVM, typically when \(p >> n\) the unregularized support vector classifier works about well or better than the regularized version

Overfitting is not as much of a problem with the SVM because the inherent dependence on a relatively few support vectors

Remember in the multiclass problem, we can consider the ``one versus one'' or ``one versus all'' approaches.

\hypertarget{part-unsupervised-learning}{%
\part{UNSUPERVISED LEARNING}\label{part-unsupervised-learning}}

\hypertarget{introduction}{%
\chapter{Introduction}\label{introduction}}

Up until now, we covered \textbf{supervised learning methods}

That is, we have a est of \(n\) observations of \(p\) features, \(X^T= (X_i, \dots, X_p)\) and we seek to predict the response \(Y\) on those \(n\) observations

In this sense, the response ``supervises'' (or teaches) the model to find the correct answer, usually relative to some loss function and tested with cross-validation or a validation sample

Now, we focus on \textbf{unsupervised learning} where we still have a set of \(n\) observations of \(p\) features \(X\), but instead of prediction, our goal is to \textbf{find structure in these features}.

We will focus on two general types of unsupervised learning

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{cluster-analysis}{Cluster Analysis} (i.e., data segmentation)
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{dimension-reduction-1}{Dimension Reduction}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{cluster-analysis}{%
\chapter{Cluster Analysis}\label{cluster-analysis}}

Cluster analysis consists of a relatively large set of techniques that are used for finding subgroups, cluster, or segments in a set of features

The idea is that we decide on some way to measure similarity or dissimilarity and try to form clusters that are homogeneous so that observations \textbf{in a subgroup are more similar to each other than to other observations not in the subgroup}.

There are a large number of clustering algorithm.\\
two of the best-known approaches are \textbf{K-means clustering} and \textbf{hierarchical clustering}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  In the case of K-means clustering, we attempt to partition observations into a pre-determined number of clusters
\item
  In hierarchical, clustering, we either

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    start with each observation as its own cluster and aggregate them, or
  \item
    start with one cluster and successively disaggregate them,
  \item
    in both cases giving a tree or dendrogram representation of the clustering
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{We can choose to perform clustering on the basis of the features to get subgroups or observations, or in terms of the observations to get subgroups of features}
\item
  Critical all clustering approaches is the choice of \textbf{distance or proximity} or \textbf{dissimilarity measure between two objects}.
\end{itemize}

Both \protect\hyperlink{discriminant-analysis}{Discriminant Analysis} and \protect\hyperlink{cluster-analysis}{Cluster Analysis} are used to classify.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
& Cluster Analysis & Discriminant Analysis\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
Under framework & Unsupervised Learning & Supervised Learning\tabularnewline
Dependent variable (category) & No & Yes\tabularnewline
Classification rule based on & Closeness & Difference\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{proximity-matrices}{%
\section{Proximity Matrices}\label{proximity-matrices}}

It's convenient to represent the data in terms of \textbf{proximity} (alikeness of affinity)

This can be thought of in terms of similarityor dissimilarity

A similarity measure increases as two objects are more alike, while a dissimilarity measure decreases as two objects become more alike (e.g., Euclidean distance).

Formally, given two \(p\)-dimensional objects \(x_i\) and \(x_i'\), a dissimilarity \textbf{measure} \(d_{ii'}\) satisfies:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(d_{ii'} \ge 0 \forall x_i, x_i'\)
\item
  \(d_{ii'} = 0\) iff \(x_i = x_i'\)
\item
  \(d_{ii'} = d_{i'i}\)
\end{enumerate}

A measure that satisfies all of these conditions is said to be a \textbf{semi}-\textbf{metric}

If it further satisfies the triangle inequality, \(d_{ii'} < d_{ik} + d_{i'k}\), the it is said to form a \textbf{metric}.

We typically form the \(n \times n\) \textbf{proximity matrix}, \(\mathbf{D}\), which in the case of a dissimilarity measure, has \((i,j)\)th element given by \(d_{ij}\)

\hypertarget{dissimilarities-based-on-attributes}{%
\subsection{Dissimilarities based on Attributes}\label{dissimilarities-based-on-attributes}}

In general, we can define the dissimilarity by summing across the \(p\) attributes (i.e., equal weighting):

\[
d_{ii'} = D(x_i, x_i') = \sum_{j=1}^p d_j (x_{ij}, x_{i'j})
\]

A very common choice for the dissimilarity metric is squared distance

\[
d_j(x_{ij}, x_{i'j}) = (x_{ij} - x_{i'j})^2
\]

Issues:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  We may wish more general distance measures
\item
  We may have categorical variables
\item
  We may wish to weight the attributes differently
\end{enumerate}

Fixes

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  A general class of dissimilarities is the \textbf{Minkowski metric}
\end{enumerate}

\[
d_{ii'} = (\sum_{j=1}^p | x_{ij} - x_{i'j}|^\lambda)^{1/\lambda}
\]

Consider the situation when we have ordinal variables (e..g, categorical variables that have order)

Typically, one converts these to a numerical value then uses a quantitative metric.

For example, say we have \(M\) ordinal values, we can then replace these with \((i - 1/2)/M\) for \(i = 1, \dots, M\)

For unordered categorical (nominal) variables, the degree-of-difference between pairs of values must be specified directly. These are selected in a way to maintain the symmetric nature of the dissimilarity matrix

And it's common to weight the elements of the discrepancy function differently depending on attribute

\[
D(x_i, x_i') = \sum_{j=1}^p w_j d_j (x_{ij}, x_{i'j}); \sum_{j=1}^p w_j = 1
\]

where \(w_j\) is a weight assigned to the j-th attribute

As discussed in \citep[ 14.3.3]{hastie2001a}, setting the \(w_j\) to the same value for each variable does not necessarily give all of the attributes equal weight.

They show that setting \(w_j\) proportional to \(1/\bar{d}_j\) where

\[
\bar{d}_j = \frac{1}{n^2} \sum_{i=1}^n \sum_{i' = 1}^n d_j (x_{ij}, x_{i'j})
\]

gives all attributes equal influence, which is not always a good thing in terms of finding subgroups

\hypertarget{combinatorial-cluster-algorithms}{%
\section{Combinatorial Cluster Algorithms}\label{combinatorial-cluster-algorithms}}

Let each observation be uniquely labeled by an integer \(i \in \{1, \dots, n\}\). A pre-specified number of clusters \(K<n\) is then assumed, and each cluster is labeled by an integer \(k \in \{ 1, \dots, K \}\)

Each observation is assigned to one unique cluster.

The assignment is characterized by an \textbf{encoder}, \(k = C(i)\) which assigns the \(i\)-th observation to the \(k\)-th cluster

We seek an encoder based on the dissimilarities \(d(x_i, x_i')\) between every pair of observations.

We have to specify some ``\textbf{loss}'' \textbf{function} that we would like to minimize for clustering. One approach is to define

\[
W(C) = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{C(i)= k} \sum_{C(i') = k} d(x_i, x_{i'})
\]

\[
B(C) = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{C(i) = k} \sum_{C(i') \neq k} d(x_i, x_{i'})
\]

\[
T = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{i=1}^n \sum_{i'=1}^n d(x_i, x_{i'}) = W(C) + B(C)
\]

K-Means Clustering

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  randomly assign a number (\(1 \to K\)), to each of the observations, which will be initial cluster assignments.
\item
  Iterate until the cluster assignments stop changing:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    For each of the K clusters, compute the cluster \textbf{centroid}. The k-th cluster centroid is the vector of the p feature means for the observations in the k-th cluster
  \item
    Assign each observation to the cluster whose centroid is close (where closest is defined by Euclidean distance).
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

Hierarchical Clustering

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Begin with n observations and a measure (e.g., Euclidean distance) of all the \(\binom{n}{2}= n(n-1)/2\) pairwise dissimilarities. Treat each observation as its own cluster.
\item
  For \(i = n, n-1, \dots, 2\):

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \item
    Examine all pairwise inter-cluster dissimilarities among the \(i\) clusters and identify th pair of clusters that are least dissimilar (i.e., most similar). Fuse these two clusters. The dissimilarity between these two clusters indicates the height in the dendrogram at which the fusion should be placed
  \item
    Compute the new pairwise inter-cluster dissimilarities among he \(i-1\) remaining clusters.
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{part-deep-learning}{%
\part{DEEP LEARNING}\label{part-deep-learning}}

\hypertarget{introduction-1}{%
\chapter{Introduction}\label{introduction-1}}

Note: Even though deep learning can be a powerful tool when it comes to fitting a function to data. Unless you have a causal diagram/model, its knowledge is still not tranferable. For example, when you get a new population, you will have to train your deep learning model again.

\citep{LeCun_2015} for a review. \citep{LeCun_2015} defines a deep-learning architecture as ``a multilayer stack of simple modules, all (or most) of which are subject to learning, and many of which compute non-linear input-output mappings''.

\hypertarget{neural-network}{%
\chapter{Neural Network}\label{neural-network}}

Neuron

\[
z = a_1 w_1 + .. + a_k w_k + b
\]

Sigmoid function

\[
\sigma (z) = \frac{1}{1 +e^{-z}}
\]

Different connections lead to different network structures

The neurons have different values of weights and biases.\\
Fully connected Feedforward Network

Framework

Step 1: A set of function\\
Step 2: Goodness of function f\\
Step 3: Pick the best function

\hypertarget{step-1-a-set-of-function}{%
\section{Step 1: A set of function}\label{step-1-a-set-of-function}}

Deep = many hidden layers

Universality Theorem\\
Any continuous, function f

\[
f: R^N \to R^M
\] can be realized by a network with one hidden layer (given enough hidden neurons).

Output layer

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Softmax layer as the output layer\\
  \[
  \sigma(\vec{z})_i = \frac{e^{z_i}}{\sum_{j=1}^k e^{z_j}}
  \]

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    where

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \(\sigma\) = softmax
    \item
      \(\vec{z}\) = input vector
    \item
      \(e^{z_i}\) = standard exponential function for input vector
    \item
      K = number of classes in the multi-class classifier
    \item
      \(e^{z_j}\) = standard exponential function for output vector
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    probability:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \(1 > y_i > 0\)
    \item
      \(\sum_i y_i = 1\)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Monotonicity\\
  \item
    Non-locality
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-2-goodness-of-function}{%
\section{Step 2: Goodness of function}\label{step-2-goodness-of-function}}

Loss can be square error or cross entropy between the network output and target.

With softmax, the summation of all the outputs would be one.

A good function should make the loos of all examples as small as possible

Total Loss:

\[
L = \sum_{r=1}^R l_r
\]

Find a function in function set (the network parameters \(\theta^*\)) that minimizes total loss.

\hypertarget{step-3-pick-the-best-function}{%
\section{Step 3: Pick the best function}\label{step-3-pick-the-best-function}}

Find network parameters \(\theta^*\) that minimize total loss L.

\textbf{Gradient Descent}

Network parameters \(\theta = (w_1,w_2,..,b_1,b_2,...)\)

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Pick an initial value for w
\item
  Compute \(\frac{\partial L}{\partial w}\),

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    if negative, increase w
  \item
    if positive, decrease w
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \(w_{new} = w_{old} - \eta \frac{partial L}{\partial w}\) where \(\eta\) is the learning rate.
\item
  You have problems with:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    plateau: \(\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} \approx 0\)
  \item
    saddle point: \(\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = 0\)
  \item
    Stuck at local minima: \(\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = 0\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  solution:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Different initial point to reach different minima, so different results.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Backpropagation

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  is an efficient way to compute \(\partial L / \partial w\)
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{recipe-of-deep-learning}{%
\section{Recipe of Deep learning}\label{recipe-of-deep-learning}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Not good result on training data
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist

  \protect\hyperlink{choosing-proper-loss}{Choosing proper loss}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{mini-batch}{Mini-batch}

\end{itemize}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  Not good result on testing data
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{early-stopping}{Early Stopping}
\item
  \protect\hyperlink{regularization}{Regularization}

\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{choosing-proper-loss}{%
\subsection{Choosing proper loss}\label{choosing-proper-loss}}

When using softmax output layer, choose cross entropy. \href{http://proceedings.mlr.press/v9/glorot10a/glorot10a.pdf}{Problem}

\hypertarget{mini-batch}{%
\subsection{Mini-batch}\label{mini-batch}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{model.fit(x\_train,y\_train,batch\_size }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,nb\_epoch }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# 100 examples in a mini{-}batch }
\CommentTok{\# repeat 20 times}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Randomly initialize network parameters
\item
  One epoch

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Pick the 1st batch \(L'= l^1 + l^{31} + ..\) Update parameters once.
  \item
    Pick the 2nd batch \(L''=l^2 + l^{16} +...\) Update parameters once
  \item
    Until all mini-batches have been picked
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Repeat the above process
\item
  Mini-batch is faster (with mini-batch. if there are 20 batches, update 20 times in one epoch).
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{new-activation-function}{%
\subsection{New activation function}\label{new-activation-function}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU)
\end{itemize}

Reason:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Fast to compute
\item
  Biological reason
\item
  Infinite sigmoid with different biases
\item
  Vanishing gradient problem.
\end{enumerate}

Parametric ReLU (PReLU) \citep{Glorot_2011}

ReLU - Variant

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Leaky ReLU\\
\item
  Parametric ReLu
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{adaptive-learning-rate}{%
\subsection{Adaptive Learning Rate}\label{adaptive-learning-rate}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Set the learning rate \(\eta\) carefully
\item
  If learning rate is too large
\item
  Total loss may not decrease after each update.
\item
  If learning rate is too small
\item
  Training would be too slow
\item
  Popular and simple idea: reduce the learning rate by some factor every few epochs.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    At the beginning, we are far from the destination, so we use larger learning rate
  \item
    After several epochs, we are close to the destination, so we reduce the learning rate
  \item
    E.g., 1/t decay \(\eta^t = \eta \sqrt{t+1}\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Learning rate cannot be one-size-fits-all

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Giving different parameters different learning rates.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \(\eta\) is unit-dependent (from 0 to 1)
\end{itemize}

Adagrad

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Original: \(w \leftarrow w - \eta \partial L /\partial w\)
\item
  Adagrad: \(w \leftarrow - \eta_{w} \partial L / \partial w\)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    where \(\eta_w\) is parameter dependent learning rate.\\
    \[
    \eta_w = \frac{\eta}{\sqrt{\sum_{i=1}^{t}(g^i)^2}}
    \]
  \item
    \(\eta\) is constant
  \item
    \(g^i\) is \(\partial L / \partial w\) obtained at the i-th update.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Learning rate is smaller and smaller for all parameters
\item
  Smaller derivatives, larger learning rate, and vice versa.
\end{itemize}

\href{https://courses.cs.washington.edu/courses/cse547/15sp/slides/adagrad.pdf}{link}

\hypertarget{momentum}{%
\subsection{Momentum}\label{momentum}}

Hard to find optimal network parameters

Movement = negative of \(\partial L/ \partial w\) + Momentum

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# RMSProp (Advanced Adagrad) + Momentum}
\NormalTok{model.}\BuiltInTok{compile}\NormalTok{(loss}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}categorial\_crossentropy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,optimizer }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ SGD(lr}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{),metrics }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}accuracy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{model.}\BuiltInTok{compile}\NormalTok{(loss}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}categorial\_crossentropy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,optimizer}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{Adam(),metrics }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}accuracy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{early-stopping}{%
\subsection{Early Stopping}\label{early-stopping}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  training data and testing data can be different.
\item
  Learning target is defined by the training data
\item
  The parameters achieving the learning target do not necessary have good results on the testing data.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{regularization}{%
\subsection{Regularization}\label{regularization}}

\hypertarget{dropout}{%
\subsection{Dropout}\label{dropout}}

Training

\begin{itemize}
\item
  each time before updating the parameters

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    each neuron has p\% to dropout.

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      The structure of the network is changed.
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Using the new network for training

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      For each min-batch, we resample the dropout neurons.
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Testing

\begin{itemize}
\item
  No dropout

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If the dropout rate training is p\%, all the weights times 1-p\%
  \item
    Assume that the dropout rate is 50\%. If a weight w = 1 by training, set w = 0.5 for testing.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Dropout is a kind of ensemble.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  training of dropout: M neurons \(\to\) \(2^M\) possible networks.
\item
  Using one mini-batch to train one network
\item
  Some parameters int eh network are shared.
\item
  Training of dropout: assume dropout rate is 50\%
\item
  Testing of dropout: weight
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{model }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Sequential()}
\NormalTok{model.add(Dense(input\_dim }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{28}\OperatorTok{*}\DecValTok{28}\NormalTok{,output\_dim }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{500}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(Activation(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sigmoid\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(dropout(}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(Dense (output\_dim}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{500}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(Activation(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sigmoid\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(dropout(}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(Dense(output\_dim }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{model.add(Activation(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}softmax\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}

\hypertarget{network-structure}{%
\subsection{Network Structure}\label{network-structure}}

Convolutional Neural Network (CNN)

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Widely used in image processing
\item
  use in \protect\hyperlink{step-1-a-set-of-function}{the first step} for deep learning
\end{itemize}

The Whole CNN

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Property 1: Some patterns are much smaller than the whole image\\
\item
  Property 2: The same patterns appear in different regions.\\
\item
  Property 3: Subsampling the pixels will not change the object.
\end{itemize}

CNN in Keras: only modified the network structure and input format (vector to 3-D tensor)

\item
  tfestimators: Implementations of common model types such as regressors and classifiers.
\item
  tensorflow: Low \emph{level interface to the TensorFlow computational graph.}
\item
  tfdatasets: Scalable input pipelines for TensorFlow models.
\end{itemize}

Supporting Tools

\begin{itemize}
\item
  tfruns : Track , visualize, and manage TensorFlow training runs and experiments
\item
  tfdeploy : Tools designed to make exporting and serving TensorFlow models straightforward.
\item
  cloudml : R interface to Google Cloud Machine Learning Engine.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{keras-layers}{%
\section{Keras layers}\label{keras-layers}}

65 layers available

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Dense layers: classic ``fully connected'' neural network layers
\item
  Convolutional layers: "Filters for learning local patterns in data
\item
  Recurrent layers: Layers that maintain state based on on previously seen data
\item
  Embedding layers: Vectorization of text that reflects semantic relationships between words
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{compiling-models}{%
\chapter{Compiling models}\label{compiling-models}}

Model compilation prepares the model for training by:\\
1. Converting the layers into a TensorFlow graph\\
2. Applying the specified loss function and optimizer\\
3. Arranging for the collection of metrics during training

All available at Keras for R \href{https://github.com/rstudio/cheatsheets/raw/master/keras.pdf}{cheatsheet}

Example of \href{https://blogs.rstudio.com/ai/posts/2017\%20*12\%20*14\%20*image\%20*classification\%20*on\%20*small\%20*datasets/}{Image classificaiton}

\hypertarget{nyu}{%
\chapter{NYU}\label{nyu}}

Materials in this chapter is based on \href{https://atcold.github.io/pytorch-Deep-Learning/}{NYU Deep Learning}

Deep in Deep Learning means multi-layers. ``A deep network has several layers and uses them to build a hierarchy of features of increasing complexity''

Supervised Learning (= Function Optimization): With inputs and outputs, you train the machine to tweak its parameter to have the correct outputs from inputs. In ``traditional machine learning'', you have to engineer \textbf{feature extractor}, but in deep learning you can multi-layers feature extractor can be trained. And all layers are non-linear, because if they are linear, then all layers would be collapse into one layer.

Natural Data is compositional. Hence, it is efficiently representable hierarchically.

Revolutions in Deep Learning:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Speech Recognition: 2010
\item
  Image Recognition: 2013
\item
  Natural Language Processing (NLP): 2015
\end{itemize}

Deep Learning = Learning Representations/ Features Representation means you turn raw data into something useful

Image Recognition:\\
Pixel, edge, texton, motif, part, object

Text\\
Character, group, word group, clause, sentence, story

Speech\\
Sample, spectral band, sound, phone, phoneme, word.

Difference between Deep Learning Support-Vector Machines (SVM)

SVM is a 2-layer neural net, where

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  layer 1 = templates
\item
  layer 2 = linear classifier.
\end{itemize}

where Deep Learning is ``A deep network has several layers and uses them to build a hierarchy of features of increasing complexity''

Deep Machines are more efficient than SVM in representing certain classes of functions.

The Manifold Hypothesis ``states that real-world high-dimensional data lie on low-dimensional manifolds embedded within the high-dimensional space''

Difference between Deep Learning and PCA (Principal Components Analysis) is that Deep learning uses non-linear function, while PCA uses linear function.

\hypertarget{model-card}{%
\chapter{Model Card}\label{model-card}}

Originate from the \citep{Mitchell_2019}
\href{https://modelcards.withgoogle.com/about}{Google's Model Card}. This is scientists' attempt to bring transparency, fairness and equity to other stakeholders that are affected by AI models. Two specific examples that serve as a template are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \href{https://modelcards.withgoogle.com/face-detection}{Face Detection}\\
\item
  \href{https://modelcards.withgoogle.com/object-detection}{Object Detection}
\end{itemize}

Although we have pre-defined template in \href{https://github.com/tensorflow/model-card-toolkit}{Python} and \href{https://www.googlenewsapp.com/using-model-card-toolkit-for-tf-model-transparency/}{example}, and \href{https://cloud.google.com/blog/products/ai-machine-learning/create-a-model-card-with-scikit-learn}{deploy}, there doesn't seem to be one in R

\hypertarget{model-stacking}{%
\chapter{Model Stacking}\label{model-stacking}}

Codes are from \href{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/stacks/vignettes/basics.html}{stack vignettes} basic and

\href{https://github.com/tidymodels/stacks}{Source Code}

Steps in doing model staking:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Define your candidate (ensemble) models
\item
  Initialize a \texttt{data\_stack} object with \texttt{stacks()}
\item
  Add candidate candidate models using \texttt{add\_candidates()}
\item
  Evaluate the combined predictions \texttt{blend\_predictions()}
\item
  'fit\_members()` allows you to fit models with non-zero staking coefficients
\item
  \texttt{predict()} new data
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{data}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"tree\_frogs"}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# subset the data}
\CommentTok{\# drops \textless{}{-} c("clutch","hatched")}
\CommentTok{\# tree\_frogs[ , !(names(tree\_frogs) \%in\% drops)]}



\CommentTok{\# subset the data}
\NormalTok{tree\_frogs }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ tree\_frogs }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{filter}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{!}\FunctionTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(latency)) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}

\hypertarget{mongodb}{%
\subsection{MongoDB}\label{mongodb}}

Setup Enviroment

Code to access virtual machine: Cd m103 Cd m103-vagrant-env

Open virtual machine: vagrant up Bring up environment: vagrant provision Connect to ssh: vagrant ssh Version: mongod --version Make sure box is running properly: validate\_box Exit the box: exit Stop the box virtual machine: vagrant halt

Mongod is the main daemon processing of mongDB Run mongod use driver to communicate with application.

Can access mongod or mongo: In Mongo: - show dbs: show database - exit: use admin → db.shutdownServer() \(\to\) exit

In Mongod: -- port : tell MongoDB which port to listen to (default: 27017) -- dbpath the location to store database files -- logpath the location of a logfile for mongod to log information to. -- fork a flag to tell mongod to run as a background process, rather than as an active process which blocks the shell

\includegraphics{images/daemon.PNG}

If set fork path, must set log path Set up mongod on port 30000 with a dbpath to local directory called first\_mongod and fork the process so it would not block the shell. mkdir first\_mongod mongod --port 30000 --dbpath first\_mongod --logpaht first\_mongod/mongod.log --fork

then connect to mongo: mongo --port 30000

\CommentTok{\#\%\% Example exclude "address" }

\NormalTok{mydb }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ myclient[}\StringTok{"mydatabase"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{mycol }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ mydb[}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{]}

  \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(x)}

  \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(x)}

\hypertarget{parallel-computing}{%
\chapter{Parallel Computing}\label{parallel-computing}}

According to \href{https://nceas.github.io/oss-lessons/parallel-computing-in-r/parallel-computing-in-r.html}{\emph{Matt Jones}}, computation is bound by

\begin{itemize}
\item
  CPU (take much CPU time)
\item
  Memory: take memory
\item
  I/O: take time to read/write from disk
\item
  Network: transfer time
\end{itemize}

Parallel computing can help with CPU-bound

By default, computer programming software (e.g., Python and R) usually use one core processor for serial computation. However, with big data, the iterative process can take a lot of time. If we don't have to data analysis in a sequential step, a in an analysis or can break the big data set into smaller chunks and process them simultaneously, it will increase the speed of the analysis process.

Hence, we have parallel computing that utilizes the philosophy of ``divide and conquer.'' Hence, parallel computing

There are two actors in a parallel backend

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Director: who tells workers what to do, and manages computing resources (processors, RAM memory, network bandwidth, etc.)
\item
  Workers: who execute what the director told them to do
\end{enumerate}

A parallel backend is the way workers and director communicate

There are 2 types of parallel backends

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lllll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
Type\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.27}}\raggedright
Available\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.11}}\raggedright
Work in clusters\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.54}}\raggedright
Col4\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
Col5\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
FORK\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.27}}\raggedright
Unix machines (Linux, Mac, etc)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.11}}\raggedright
Nope\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.54}}\raggedright
Workers share the same environment (data, loaded packages, functions) as the director\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
PSOCK\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.27}}\raggedright
Unix and Windows (default for \texttt{forecah} )\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.11}}\raggedright
Yep\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.54}}\raggedright
environment is copied to the environment of each worker\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.04}}\raggedright
\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

FORK is more efficient because there is only one environment and only outputs are sent back to the director (40\% faster than PSOCK)

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  FORK
\item
  PSOCK
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{multidplyr}{%
\section{multidplyr}\label{multidplyr}}

\href{https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2016/12/18/multidplyr.html\#figure1}{\includegraphics{images/multidplyr.png}}

Typically, there are two situations that we can utilize this process:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  A big dataset that needs to be broken up into small ones and perform analysis on each
\item
  A data set that needs to perform multiple analyses (e.g., models).
\end{enumerate}

Old example provided by \href{https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2016/12/18/multidplyr.html\#figure1}{Business Science} (no longer available)

Example provided by the package's author

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(multidplyr)}

\NormalTok{cluster }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{new\_cluster}\NormalTok{(parallel}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{detectCores}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# use all cores except 2}
\FunctionTok{cluster\_library}\NormalTok{(cluster, }\StringTok{"dplyr"}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# you have to load your libraries to be used across workers (clusters)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

First way (more efficient)

read different files on each worker (cluster)

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create a filename vector containing different values on each worker}
\FunctionTok{cluster\_assign\_each}\NormalTok{(cluster, }\AttributeTok{filename =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"a.csv"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"b.csv"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"c.csv"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"d.csv"}\NormalTok{))}

\CommentTok{\# Use vroom to quickly load the csvs}
\FunctionTok{cluster\_send}\NormalTok{(cluster, my\_data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ vroom}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{vroom}\NormalTok{(filename))}

\CommentTok{\# Create a party\_df using the my\_data variable on each worker}
\NormalTok{my\_data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{party\_df}\NormalTok{(cluster, }\StringTok{"my\_data"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

If you put your files separately in a folder already, you can use

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{files }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{dir}\NormalTok{(path, }\AttributeTok{full.names =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{cluster\_assign\_partition}\NormalTok{(cluster, }\AttributeTok{files =}\NormalTok{ files)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Second way

If you load the dataset already, then you have to \texttt{partition} them across workers (clusters).

You can also \texttt{group\_by} before \texttt{partition} if your analysis requires observations to be in the same group

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(nycflights13)}

\NormalTok{flight\_dest }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ flights }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} \FunctionTok{group\_by}\NormalTok{(dest) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} \FunctionTok{partition}\NormalTok{(cluster)}
\NormalTok{flight\_dest}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{flight\_dest }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
  \FunctionTok{summarise}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{delay =} \FunctionTok{mean}\NormalTok{(dep\_delay, }\AttributeTok{na.rm =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{n =} \FunctionTok{n}\NormalTok{()) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%} 
  \FunctionTok{collect}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For finished computation across cores, use \texttt{collect()} to get the data back to one dataframe or one place

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{rm}\NormalTok{(cluster)}
\FunctionTok{detach}\NormalTok{(package}\SpecialCharTok{:}\NormalTok{multidplyr)}
\FunctionTok{rm}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{list =} \FunctionTok{ls}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{parallel}{%
\section{parallel}\label{parallel}}

Example by \href{https://www.blasbenito.com/post/02_parallelizing_loops_with_r/}{Blas M. Benito}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#automatic install of packages if they are not installed already}
\NormalTok{list.of.packages }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}
  \StringTok{"foreach"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"doParallel"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"ranger"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"palmerpenguins"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"tidyverse"}\NormalTok{,}
  \StringTok{"kableExtra"}
\NormalTok{  )}
\NormalTok{new.packages }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ list.of.packages[}\SpecialCharTok{!}\NormalTok{(list.of.packages }\SpecialCharTok{\%in\%} \FunctionTok{installed.packages}\NormalTok{()[,}\StringTok{"Package"}\NormalTok{])]}

\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{length}\NormalTok{(new.packages) }\SpecialCharTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)\{}
  \FunctionTok{install.packages}\NormalTok{(new.packages, }\AttributeTok{dep=}\ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\CommentTok{\#loading packages}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{(package.i }\ControlFlowTok{in}\NormalTok{ list.of.packages)\{}
  \FunctionTok{suppressPackageStartupMessages}\NormalTok{(}
    \FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(}
\NormalTok{      package.i, }
      \AttributeTok{character.only =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}
\NormalTok{      )}
\NormalTok{    )}
\NormalTok{\}}

\CommentTok{\#loading example data}
\FunctionTok{data}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"penguins"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Set up for local computer

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{n.cores }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ parallel}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{detectCores}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{2} \CommentTok{\# use all cores except 2}

\CommentTok{\#create the cluster}
\NormalTok{my.cluster }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ parallel}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{makeCluster}\NormalTok{(}
\NormalTok{  n.cores, }
  \AttributeTok{type =} \StringTok{"PSOCK"} \CommentTok{\# alternatively, we can use FORK}
\NormalTok{  )}

\CommentTok{\#check cluster definition (optional)}
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(my.cluster)}

\CommentTok{\#register it to be used by \%dopar\%}
\NormalTok{doParallel}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{registerDoParallel}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{cl =}\NormalTok{ my.cluster)}

\CommentTok{\#check if it is registered (optional)}
\NormalTok{foreach}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{getDoParRegistered}\NormalTok{()}

\CommentTok{\#how many workers are available? (optional)}
\NormalTok{foreach}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{getDoParWorkers}\NormalTok{()}

\NormalTok{x }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{foreach}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{i =} \DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }
  \AttributeTok{.combine =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}c\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{\%dopar\%}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \FunctionTok{sqrt}\NormalTok{(i)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\CommentTok{\# when done, stop cluster}

\NormalTok{parallel}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{stopCluster}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{cl =}\NormalTok{ my.cluster)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Example by \href{https://towardsdatascience.com/getting-started-with-parallel-programming-in-r-d5f801d43745}{Jens Moll-Elsborg}

\texttt{parLapply} equivalent to \texttt{lapply}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Generate data}

                  \StringTok{"2"} \OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ data,}
                  \StringTok{"3"} \OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ data,}
                  \StringTok{"4"} \OtherTok{=}\NormalTok{ data)}


\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(parallel)}

Another example by \href{https://www.blasbenito.com/post/02_parallelizing_loops_with_r/}{Blas Benito} regarding tuning random forest hyperparameters using parallel computing

\hypertarget{doparallel}{%
